The Four Categories of Actions Based on Belief
Razi corrects misconception and lists four belief-based categories of actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's classification of actions into four categories based on the agent's belief about benefit and harm, and why the motive depends on cognition rather than objective reality.

Core Idea
Actions are judged by the agent's belief, not objective benefit.

Razi begins by correcting a common misconception: the motive for an action is not the action's inherent benefit or harm, but the agent's knowledge, belief, or conjecture about it. He then presents four possible states of belief that cover all actions. This classification is essential for understanding moral responsibility and compulsion in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the foundational claim
Read the opening lines where Razi clarifies what really drives an action. Note how he distinguishes objective benefit/harm from the agent's cognitive state.
2
Identify the four categories
After the clarification, Razi lists four categories. Read them carefully and note that they exhaust all possibilities based on belief.
The categories are: purely beneficial, purely harmful, mixed, and neutral.
3
Reflect on the completeness
Consider why these four are exhaustive. Try to think of any belief about an action that wouldn't fit one of these.
This classification is foundational for later discussions on obligation and coercion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثاني : إنا قد بينا أن الداعي إلى الفعل ليس هو كون الفعل مصلحة في نفسه ، والصارف من الفعل ليس هو كون الفعل مفسدة في نفسه وإنما الداعي إلى الفعل علمه أو اعتقاده أو ظنه في كون ذلك الفعل في نفسه مصلحة ، والصارف عن الفعل أحد هذه الأمور الثلاثة في كون ذلك الفعل مفسدة. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : الشيء إما أن يكون خالص المصلحة في اعتقاده ، أو خالص المفسدة في اعتقاده ، أو يكون مشتملا على المصلحة والمفسدة في اعتقاده ، أو يكون خالياً عنهما في اعتقاده . فهذه أقسام أربعة لا مزيد عليها . أما القسم الأول : فإن عند حصول هذا الاعتقاد يجب الفعل ، ويكون الإنسان كالملجإليه . وأما القسم الثاني : فعند حصول هذا الاعتقاد يجب الترك ويكون الإنسان كالملجإلى الترك. مثال الأول الرجل في الصيف القائظ ، إذا اشتد عطشه ، وعظمت حاجته إلى شرب الماء البارد ، وكان ذلك الماء البارد حاضراً ، والشارع يقول له : اشرب . والطبيب يقول له : اشرب . وعلم أن ذلك الشرب خالص النفع خالي عن جميع جهات الضرر ، فههنا يجب الشرب ، ويكون الإنسان كالملجإلى ذلك الشرب. ومثال الثاني : الإنسان إذا علم أن له في دخول النار ضرراً عظيماً ، والشارع يقول له : لا تدخل النار . والعقلاء أطبقوا على منعه من الدخول في النار ، وعلم ذلك الإنسان أنه ليس له في دخول النار منفعة في الدنيا ولا في الآخرة ، وأن له في دخولها أعظم المضار . فههنا يمتنع منه أن يدخل في النار ، ويكون كالملجإلى أن لا يدخل في النار . نعم الإنسان قد يلتزم قتل نفسه ، وقد يرمي نفسه من شاهق جبل إلا أنه إنما يفعل ذلك إذا صار مضطراً إلى التزام ألم أو غم . وأعتقد أن ذلك الألم وذلك الغم أعظم من الألم الحاصل بسبب القتل . وأعتقد أنه لا يمكنه الخلاص من ذلك الألم الأعظم إلا بالتزام ألم القتل ، فههنا قد يقدم على قتل نفسه . وإنما فعل ذلك لاعتقاده أن التزام هذا الألم يوجب الخلاص من ألم آخر أعظم منه ، فهو إنما التزم تحمل هذا الألم العظيم ، لكونه دافعاً للقدر الزائد عليه من الضرر ، ودفع الضرر بقدر الإمكان مطلوب في العقل . وأما القسم الثالث : وهو أن يكون الفعل مشتملا على المصلحة والمفسدة
The second section: We have shown that the motive for an action is not that the action is a benefit in itself, and the deterrent from an action is not that it is a harm in itself. Rather, the motive for action is one's knowledge, belief, or conjecture that the action is in itself a benefit, and the deterrent from action is one of these three [modes of cognition] that the action is a harm. When you know this, we say: A thing is either purely beneficial in one's belief, purely harmful in one's belief, or includes both benefit and harm in one's belief, or is devoid of both in one's belief. These are four categories with no more. As for the first category: when this belief occurs, the action is necessary, and the person is as if compelled to it. As for the second category: when this belief occurs, refraining is necessary, and the person is as if compelled to refrain. An example of the first: a man in sweltering summer, when his thirst intensifies and his need to drink cold water becomes great, and that cold water is present, and the Lawgiver says to him: 'Drink,' and the doctor says to him: 'Drink,' and he knows that the drinking is purely beneficial, free from all aspects of harm—then drinking is necessary, and the person is as if compelled to that drinking. An example of the second: a man who knows that entering fire causes him great harm, and the Lawgiver says to him: 'Do not enter the fire,' and the rational people have agreed to prevent him from entering the fire, and that man knows that he has no benefit in the fire in this world or the hereafter, and that in entering it he has the greatest harms—then he is prevented from entering the fire, and he is as if compelled not to enter the fire. However, a person may commit suicide or throw himself from a high mountain; but he does that only when he becomes compelled to endure pain or sorrow, and he believes that that pain and sorrow are greater than the pain caused by killing himself, and he believes that he cannot escape from that greater pain except by enduring the pain of killing himself. In that case, he may kill himself. He does that because of his belief that enduring this pain brings escape from another greater pain. He only endures this great pain because it repels an excess of harm beyond it, and repelling harm as much as possible is required by reason. As for the third category: that the action includes benefit and harm...
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is a classification of actions based on the agent's cognitive state regarding benefit and harm. The author first clarifies that the motive is not the objective nature of the act but the agent's knowledge, belief, or conjecture about its benefit or harm. Then he lists four possibilities for the agent's belief: purely beneficial, purely harmful, mixed, or neutral. For the first two, he gives detailed examples. In a purely beneficial scenario (like drinking cold water when extremely thirsty, with legal and medical endorsement), the action becomes necessary in the sense that the agent feels compelled. In a purely harmful scenario (like entering fire with no benefit), refraining is necessary. The author then addresses an objection: sometimes people kill themselves despite apparent harm. He explains that this occurs when they believe that the pain/sorrow they are enduring is greater than the pain of death, so they choose the lesser harm. This aligns with the rational principle that repelling harm as much as possible is required. The passage ends introducing the third category (mixed benefit and harm), which is left incomplete in this window. This classification is foundational for understanding moral obligation and coercion in kalam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that an action's objective benefit or harm determines whether it is obligatory or prohibited.
Correction: Razi clarifies that it is the agent's belief (knowledge, conjecture, or belief) about benefit or harm that motivates action or restraint, not the objective nature of the act.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what determines the motive for an action?
A) The action's inherent benefit or harm.
B) The agent's knowledge, belief, or conjecture about the action's benefit or harm.
C) The consensus of rational people.
D) The command of the Lawgiver alone.

Key Takeaway
An action's moral assessment depends on the agent's belief about its benefit or harm, not its objective nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent decision you made based on your belief that it would benefit you. Did you ever stop to consider that your belief might be mistaken? How would you react if you discovered your belief was based only on conjecture, not certain knowledge? Will you now ask yourself before every major action: 'Is this based on knowledge or mere conjecture?'
?
You often face actions that have both potential benefit and harm (mixed category). How do you decide which outweighs the other? Are you honestly weighing the harm, or are you downplaying it because you desire the benefit? What is one area in your life where you are ignoring the harm because you want the benefit, and what will you do about it today?
?
This lesson teaches that your motive is your belief, not objective reality. When you sin, you believe at that moment that it brings some benefit. Are you willing to admit that your own beliefs can be wrong? God has given you the intellect to correct them. What false belief about benefit are you holding onto right now, and when will you actively seek the truth to uproot it?

Examples of Pure Benefit and Pure Harm
Razi provides examples for pure benefit and pure harm, including suicide as a special case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi illustrates the first two categories with concrete examples, including the case of suicide, and why the principle of repelling harm is rational.

Core Idea
When an action is believed purely beneficial, action is compelled; when purely harmful, refraining is compelled.

In this lesson, we examine Razi's detailed examples for the first two categories: pure benefit (drinking cold water when thirsty with all approvals) and pure harm (entering fire with no benefit). He also addresses an objection about suicide, explaining that it is a choice of lesser harm. These examples ground the abstract classification in everyday reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the first example (pure benefit)
Read the example of the thirsty man in summer. Notice all the conditions that lead to the belief of pure benefit.
The man has knowledge, the Lawgiver and doctor approve, and there's no apparent harm.
2
Read the second example (pure harm)
Read the example of entering fire. Note the belief of pure harm with no benefit in either world.
The person is prevented by reason and consensus.
3
Study the suicide objection
Razi addresses a potential counterexample: suicide appears to be a harmful act that people sometimes choose. Read his explanation carefully.
He argues that it is a choice of lesser harm to avoid greater pain.
4
Extract the rational principle
Notice the concluding statement: 'دفع الضرر بقدر الإمكان مطلوب في العقل'. This is a key rational principle.

The Fourfold Classification and the First Two Divisions
Enumeration of four divisions and explanation of first two.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourfold classification of actions based on belief of benefit or harm, and the rulings for the first two categories: pure benefit and pure harm.

Core Idea
Actions are judged by what we believe about their benefit or harm, not just objective reality.

This lesson introduces the foundational classification of actions according to the agent's belief. The author presents four exhaustive categories. We will focus on the first two: actions believed to be purely beneficial (obligatory action) and those believed to be purely harmful (obligatory avoidance). Examples from everyday life illustrate the principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification introduction
Read the first few lines where the author states the four divisions. Notice the four possibilities: pure benefit, pure harm, mixed, and devoid of both.
The classification is based on what the agent believes (في اعتقاده).
2
Understand the first division: pure benefit
When an action is believed to be purely beneficial, action becomes obligatory and the agent is compelled to do it. Study the example of the thirsty fasting person.
3
Understand the second division: pure harm
When an action is believed to be purely harmful, avoidance is obligatory and the agent is compelled to refrain. Study the example of entering fire.
4
Reflect on the suicide example
The author discusses how even suicide can be chosen to avoid a greater harm, demonstrating that compulsion is based on belief of greater benefit or lesser harm.
This shows that the principle extends to apparent self-harm when it is believed to prevent a worse fate.

يجب الشرب، ويكون الإنسان كالملجأ إلى ذلك الشرب. ومثال الثاني: الإنسان إذا علم أن له في دخول النار ضرراً عظيماً، والشارع يقول له: لا تدخل النار. والعقلاء أطبقوا على منعه من الدخول في النار، وعلم ذلك الإنسان أنه ليس له في دخول النار منفعة في الدنيا ولا في الآخرة، وأن له في دخولها أعظم المضار. فههنا يمتنع منه أن يدخل في النار، ويكون كالملجأ إلى أن لا يدخل في النار. نعم الإنسان قد يلتزم قتل نفسه، وقد يرمي نفسه من شاهق جبل إلا أنه إنما يفعل ذلك إذا صار مضطراً إلى التزام ألم أو غم، واعتقد أن ذلك الألم وذلك الغم أعظم من الألم الحاصل بسبب القتل، واعتقد أنه لا يمكنه الخلاص من ذلك الألم الأعظم إلا بالتزام ألم القتل، فههنا قد يقدم على قتل نفسه. وإنما فعل ذلك لاعتقاده أن التزام هذا الألم يوجب الخلاص من ألم آخر أعظم منه، فهو إنما التزم تحمل هذا الألم العظيم، لكونه دافعاً للقدر الزائد عليه من الضرر، ودفع الضرر بقدر الإمكان مطلوب في العقل. وأما القسم الثالث: وهو أن يكون الفعل مشتملا على المصلحة والمفسدة في اعتقاده فهذا ينقسم إلى ثلاثة أقسام، لأنه إما أن يكون جانب المصلحة راجحاً في اعتقاده، وإما أن يكون جانب المفسدة راجحاً في اعتقاده، وإما أن يكون متعادل الجانبين. فإن كان جانب المصلحة راجحاً في اعتقاده وجب الفعل ويدل عليه وجهان: الأول: إنه مقابل المثل بالمثل فيبقى العدد الزائد داعية خالصة إلى الفعل. والثاني: وهو أنه لما كان جانب الفعل راجحاً على جانب الترك في اعتقاده، فلو رجح جانب الترك كان ذلك ترجيحاً للمرجوح على الراجح وذلك محال في العقول. وإذا امتنع هذا وجب ترجيح الجانب الراجح وذلك هو المطلوب. وإما إن كان جانب المفسدة راجحاً وجب الترك لمعنى الدليلين المذكورين. وأما إن حصل التعادل بحسب اعتقاده، وجب التوقف وحينئذ يبقى ما كان على ما كان. وأما القسم الرابع: وهو أن لا يحصل اعتقاد كون الفعل مشتملا على المصلحة وعلى المفسدة. فعند هذا لم يحصل الداعي إلى الفعل ولا الصارف عنه، بل يبقى ما كان على ما كان من غير تغيير أصلاً.
If you know this, we say: a thing is either pure benefit in his belief, or pure harm in his belief, or comprises benefit and harm in his belief, or is devoid of both in his belief. These are four divisions with no addition. As for the first division: when this belief occurs, action is obligatory, and man becomes compelled to it. As for the second division: when this belief occurs, abandonment is obligatory, and man becomes compelled to abandonment. Example of the first: the fasting person in the intense summer when his thirst intensifies, his need to drink cold water becomes great, and that cold water is present, and the lawgiver says to him: drink, and the doctor says to him: drink, and he knows that drinking is pure benefit devoid of all aspects of harm, then here drinking is obligatory, and man becomes compelled to that drinking. Example of the second: when a man knows that entering fire causes great harm, and the lawgiver says to him: do not enter the fire, and the rational people agree to prevent him from entering the fire, and that man knows that he has no benefit in entering the fire in this world or the next, and that for him in entering it are the greatest harms, then here it is impossible for him to enter the fire, and he becomes compelled to not enter the fire. Yes, a man may commit suicide or throw himself from a mountaintop, but he only does that when he becomes necessitated to endure pain or grief, and he believes that pain and grief are greater than the pain caused by death, and he believes that he cannot escape that greater pain except by enduring the pain of death, then here he may proceed to kill himself. He only does that because of his belief that enduring this pain necessitates escape from another greater pain, so he only undertakes to bear this great pain because it repels the excess of harm beyond it, and repelling harm as much as possible is rationally demanded. As for the third division: that the action comprises benefit and harm in his belief, then this divides into three divisions: either the side of benefit is preponderant in his belief, or the side of harm is preponderant in his belief, or the two sides are equal. If the side of benefit is preponderant in his belief, then action is obligatory, and two proofs indicate it: first: it matches like with like, and the excess number remains a pure motive for action. Second: since the side of action is preponderant over the side of abandonment in his belief, if he were to prefer the side of abandonment, that would be a preference of the less preponderant over the more preponderant, and that is impossible in reason. When this is impossible, preference of the preponderant side is obligatory, and that is what is sought. If the side of harm is preponderant, then abandonment is obligatory by the meaning of the two proofs mentioned. If equality occurs according to his belief, then suspension is obligatory, and then what was remains as it was. As for the fourth division: that no belief occurs that the action comprises benefit and harm. Then here there occurs neither motive for action nor deterrent from it, rather what was remains as it was without any change.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of actions based on the agent's belief about their benefit or harm. The author begins by stating that after understanding the previous principle, we can say that any thing (action) falls into one of four categories in the agent's belief: pure benefit, pure harm, mixed, or devoid of both. These are exhaustive. For the first category (pure benefit), when such a belief is present, the action becomes obligatory and the agent is compelled to do it, like a thirsty person who knows drinking water is purely beneficial. For the second (pure harm), abandonment becomes obligatory and the agent is compelled to refrain, like avoiding fire. The author notes that even suicide can occur when a greater evil is believed to be avoided, demonstrating that compulsion is based on belief. For the third category (mixed benefit and harm), further subdivision occurs: if benefit outweighs, action is obligatory; if harm outweighs, abandonment is obligatory; if equal, suspension is necessary. The author provides rational proofs for these rulings, including the impossibility of preferring the less preponderant. For the fourth category (no belief of benefit or harm), no motive or deterrent exists, so the status quo remains unchanged. The entire classification relies on the agent's subjective belief, not objective reality, which is a key point for later arguments about obligation and compulsion.

Key Terms
مصلحة
مفسدة
اعتقال
داعية
صارف
إلجاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the classification is based on objective benefit and harm.
Correction: The author repeatedly says 'in his belief' (في اعتقاده), emphasizing that it is the agent's subjective assessment that determines the ruling.

Checkpoint
What is the ruling for an action believed to be purely beneficial according to the passage?
A) Action is optional
B) Action is obligatory
C) Abandonment is obligatory
D) Suspension is required

Key Takeaway
When an action is believed to be purely beneficial it becomes obligatory, and when believed purely harmful avoidance becomes obligatory, based on the agent's belief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a decision you are currently facing. Are you honestly evaluating it as pure benefit, pure harm, or mixed? What belief is driving your choice, and is it truly based on all available evidence?
?
When you avoid something difficult (like a difficult conversation or a religious obligation), ask yourself: Is it because you believe it is purely harmful, or because you are avoiding discomfort? What would the author say about your belief?
?
The example of suicide shows that even extreme actions can be compelled by stronger beliefs about harm. Is there any area of your life where you are tolerating a small harm because you believe avoiding it would lead to a greater harm? Challenge your own assumptions today.

Rational Judgments
intermediate
5 min
The Third Division: Mixed Benefit and Harm
Detailed exposition of the third division with three subcases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third category of actions that are believed to have both benefit and harm, and the three subcategories: preponderant benefit, preponderant harm, and equal.

Core Idea
When benefit and harm are mixed, the ruling depends on which side is believed to be stronger.

The author now turns to the third category: actions believed to contain both benefit and harm. This category splits into three subcases based on which side is preponderant in the agent's belief: benefit, harm, or equal. For each, a rational ruling is given, supported by proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the third division description
Read where the author states that actions comprising both benefit and harm in belief divide into three subcases.
2
Understand the subcase of preponderant benefit
If the benefit side is believed to be stronger, action becomes obligatory. Study the two proofs provided.
The proofs involve matching like with like and the impossibility of preferring the less preponderant.
3
Understand the subcase of preponderant harm
If the harm side is believed stronger, abandonment becomes obligatory, by the same reasoning.
4
Understand the subcase of equal benefit and harm
If belief is exactly balanced, suspension (tawaqquf) is obligatory, leaving the status quo unchanged.

If you know this, we say: a thing is either pure benefit in his belief, or pure harm in his belief, or comprises benefit and harm in his belief, or is devoid of both in his belief. These are four divisions with no addition. As for the first division: when this belief occurs, action is obligatory, and man becomes compelled to it. According to the passage, what is the proof for the obligation of action when benefit is preponderant?
A) The excess number remains a pure motive

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Do you rely on your own beliefs to determine right action, or do you actively seek truth from revelation and reason? This lesson shows that your belief alone carries moral weight—are you comfortable that your beliefs are well-founded? What step will you take today to strengthen your certainty?

The Fourth Division: No Belief of Benefit or Harm
Concluding the classification with the fourth division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth category: actions for which no belief of benefit or harm exists, and the ruling that the status quo remains unchanged.

Core Idea
Without any belief of benefit or harm, there is no motive or deterrent, so no change occurs.

The final category in the classification is when the agent has no belief that an action contains either benefit or harm. In this case, there is no motive (dā'ī) to act nor deterrent (ṣārif) to refrain. Consequently, the original state remains as it was, with no compulsion to act or avoid.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth division description
Read the brief passage where the author states that when there is no belief of benefit or harm, no motive or deterrent arises.
2
Understand the implication
Without motive or deterrent, the agent remains in a neutral state. The status quo is preserved.
This contrasts with the first three categories where a belief leads to obligation or suspension.
3
Relate to the overall classification
Recall the complete classification: four categories covering all possibilities. This fourth one is the default when belief is absent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: الشيء إما أن يكون خالص المصلحة في اعتقاده، أو خالص المفسدة في اعتقاده، أو يكون مشتملا على المصلحة والمفسدة في اعتقاده، أو يكون خالياً عنهما في اعتقاده. فهذه أقسام أربعة لا مزيد If you know this, we say: a thing is either pure benefit in his belief, or pure harm in his belief, or comprises benefit and harm in his belief, or is devoid of both in his belief. These are four divisions with no addition. As for the first division: when this belief occurs, action is obligatory, and man becomes compelled to it. As for the second division: when this belief occurs, abandonment is obligatory, and man becomes compelled to abandonment. Example of the first: the fasting person in the intense summer when his thirst intensifies, his need to drink cold water becomes great, and that cold water is present, and the lawgiver says to him: drink, and the doctor says to him: drink, and he knows that drinking is pure benefit devoid of all aspects of harm, then here drinking is obligatory, and man becomes compelled to that drinking. Example of the second: when a man knows that entering fire causes great harm, and the lawgiver says to him: do not enter the fire, and the rational people agree to prevent him from entering the fire, and that man knows that he has no benefit in entering the fire in this world or the next, and that for him in entering it are the greatest harms, then here it is impossible for him to enter the fire, and he becomes compelled to not enter the fire. Yes, a man may commit suicide or throw himself from a mountaintop, but he only does that when he becomes necessitated to endure pain or grief, and he believes that pain and grief are greater than the pain caused by death, and he believes that he cannot escape that greater pain except by enduring the pain of death, then here he may proceed to kill himself. He only does that because of his belief that enduring this pain necessitates escape from another greater pain, so he only undertakes to bear this great pain because it repels the excess of harm beyond it, and repelling harm as much as possible is rationally demanded. As for the third division: that the action comprises benefit and harm in his belief, then this divides into three divisions: either the side of benefit is preponderant in his belief, or the side of harm is preponderant in his belief, or the two sides are equal. If the side of benefit is preponderant in his belief, then action is obligatory, and two proofs indicate it: first: it matches like with like, and the excess number remains a pure motive for action. Second: since the side of action is preponderant over the side of abandonment in his belief, if he were to prefer the side of abandonment, that would be a preference of the less preponderant over the more preponderant, and that is impossible in reason. When this is impossible, preference of the preponderant side is obligatory, and that is what is sought. If the side of harm is preponderant, then abandonment is obligatory by the meaning of the two proofs mentioned. If equality occurs according to his belief, then suspension is obligatory, and then what was remains as it was. As for the fourth division: that no belief occurs that the action comprises benefit and harm. Then here there occurs neither motive for action nor deterrent from it, rather what was remains as it was without any change.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of actions based on the agent's belief about their benefit or harm. The author begins by stating that after understanding the previous principle, we can say that any thing (action) falls into one of four categories in the agent's belief: pure benefit, pure harm, mixed, or devoid of both. These are exhaustive. For the first category (pure benefit), when such a belief is present, the action becomes obligatory and the agent is compelled to do it, like a thirsty person who knows drinking water is purely beneficial. For the second (pure harm), abandonment becomes obligatory and the agent is compelled to refrain, like avoiding fire. The author notes that even suicide can occur when a greater evil is believed to be avoided, demonstrating that compulsion is based on belief. For the third category (mixed benefit and harm), further subdivision occurs: if benefit outweighs, action is obligatory; if harm outweighs, abandonment is obligatory; if equal, suspension is necessary. The author provides rational proofs for these rulings, including the impossibility of preferring the less preponderant. For the fourth category (no belief of benefit or harm), no motive or deterrent exists, so the status quo remains unchanged. The entire classification relies on the agent's subjective belief, not objective reality, which is a key point for later arguments about obligation and compulsion.

Key Terms
داعية
صارف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Action becomes obligatory
B) Abandonment becomes obligatory
C) Suspension is required
D) The status quo remains unchanged

Key Takeaway
When an agent has no belief of benefit or harm in an action, there is no motive or deterrent, and the original state persists unchanged.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of an area in your life where you have been stuck—no progress, no change. Is it possible that you have no genuine belief that change would bring benefit or harm? What would it take for you to form such a belief and finally move?
?
Consider a sin you keep committing despite knowing it is wrong. Are you actually convinced of its harm, or do you only have a theoretical knowledge? If you truly believed it was harmful, would the deterrent inside you be stronger? What step can you take today to cultivate that belief?

Classification of Belief on Benefit and Harm
Classification of belief about benefit/harm into three cases and a fourth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how belief about an action's benefit and harm rationally determines its obligation, avoidance, or suspension.

Core Idea
Rational obligation depends on the agent's belief about benefit and harm.

This passage from Sanusi establishes a fundamental principle of rational ethics: human obligation is determined not by the objective reality of benefit and harm alone, but by the agent's belief about them. Before we can judge an action, we must know what the agent believes. By classifying three states of belief and a fourth case, Sanusi shows how rational reasoning leads to necessary conclusions about what one must do.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the logical structure: three clear divisions plus a fourth case.
The author uses conditional phrases (فإن, وإما) to mark each case.
2
Identify the three main divisions
List the three cases based on the agent's belief: preponderant benefit, preponderant harm, and equal sides. For each, note the ruling: action obligatory, avoidance obligatory, suspension.
The fourth case is when there is no belief about benefit/harm at all.
3
Understand the two proofs for preponderant benefit
The passage gives two rational proofs for why action is obligatory when benefit is believed preponderant. Read these carefully. The first uses a 'matching like with like' argument; the second uses the impossibility of preponderating the weaker side.
The same two proofs apply by analogy to the case of preponderant harm.
4
Apply to the other cases
Confirm how the two proofs also support avoidance for preponderant harm and suspension for equality. Then note the fourth case: no belief means no motive or deterrence.
This shows the rational consistency of Sanusi's system.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في اعتقاده فهذا ينقسم إلى ثلاثة أقسام، لأنه إما أن يكون جانب المصلحة راجحاً في اعتقاده، وإما أن يكون جانب المفسدة راجحاً في اعتقاده، وإما أن يكون متعادل الجانبين. فإن كان جانب المصلحة راجحاً في اعتقاده وجب الفعل ويدل عليه وجهان: الأول: إنه مقابل المثل بالمثل فيبقى العدد الزائد داعية خالصة إلى الفعل. والثاني: وهو أنه لما كان جانب الفعل راجحاً على جانب الترك في اعتقاده، فلو رجح جانب الترك كان ذلك ترجيحاً للمرجوح على الراجح وذلك محال في العقول. وإذا امتنع هذا وجب ترجيح الجانب الراجح وذلك هو المطلوب. وإما إن كان جانب المفسدة راجحا وجب الترك لمعنى الدليلين المذكورين. وأما إن حصل التعادل بحسب اعتقاده، وجب التوقف وحينئذ يبقى ما كان على ما كان. وأما القسم الرابع: وهو أن لا يحصل اعتقاد كون الفعل مشتملا على المصلحة وعلى المفسدة. فعند هذا لم يحصل الداعي إلى الفعل ولا الصارف عنه، بل يبقى ما كان على ما كان من غير تغيير أصلاً.
In his belief, this divides into three divisions: because either the side of benefit is preponderant in his belief, or the side of harm is preponderant in his belief, or the two sides are equal. If the side of benefit is preponderant in his belief, action is obligatory, and there are two proofs for this: first, it is matching like with like, so the additional number remains a pure motive to action. Second, because when the side of action is preponderant over the side of inaction in his belief, if he were to preponderate the side of inaction, that would be a preponderance of the preponderate over the preponderant, and that is impossible in intellects. And if this is impossible, then preponderating the preponderant side is obligatory, and that is what is sought. And if the side of harm is preponderant, avoidance is obligatory by the meaning of the two mentioned proofs. And if equality occurs according to his belief, suspension is obligatory, and in that case what was remains as it was. As for the fourth division: that there is no belief that the action includes benefit and harm. Then there is no motive to action nor deterrence from it, rather what was remains as it was without any change at all.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage classifies the states of belief (i'tiqad) regarding an action's benefit and harm. The author divides belief into three cases: preponderance of benefit, preponderance of harm, and equality. For each, he derives the rational obligation. For preponderant benefit, action is obligatory, supported by two rational proofs: first, the extra benefit is a pure motive; second, preferring the weaker side over the stronger is rationally impossible. For preponderant harm, avoidance is obligatory by analogous reasoning. For equal sides, suspension is obligatory, leaving the previous state unchanged. Then he adds a fourth case: absence of any belief about benefit or harm—here there is no motive or deterrence, so the state remains unchanged. The passage establishes that obligation follows belief about objective benefit and harm, not the mere existence of benefit or harm in itself.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the action's objective benefit or harm determines obligation, not the agent's belief.
Correction: The passage clearly bases obligation on the agent's belief (fi i'tiqadihi), not on objective reality. The fourth case confirms this: if no belief, no obligation.

Key Takeaway
Rational obligation follows the agent's belief about preponderance of benefit or harm, not the objective balance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The passage says that when you believe harm is preponderant, avoidance is obligatory. Is there an area in your life where you know something is harmful but you keep doing it? What would it cost you to truly adopt the belief that avoidance is obligatory and act on it?

Are there areas of your life where you remain deliberately ignorant, avoiding forming a belief so that you can stay in a state of inaction? If your state remains unchanged, are you responsible for seeking knowledge? What will you do today to move from ignorance to a rational conviction?

Stable vs. Changing Beliefs and Their Effect on Action
Explains how stable or changing belief determines stability or fluctuation of action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the stability or changeability of a belief about the preponderance of benefit or harm determines whether action or avoidance is consistent or disturbed.

Core Idea
Steady beliefs yield steady actions; flinching beliefs yield flinching actions.

This passage continues the discussion on the relationship between belief and action, focusing on the second branch: the effect of belief stability. Building on the earlier principle that belief in preponderance imposes an obligation, the author now introduces a crucial variable—whether that belief is permanent or changeable. This distinction explains why human actions sometimes appear consistent and sometimes erratic.

Guided Path
1
Recall the principle
Review the earlier rule: if a belief occurs that an action has preponderant benefit, action is obligatory; if harm, avoidance is obligatory.
This principle is the foundation for the new discussion.
2
Identify the two types of belief
The passage distinguishes between permanent beliefs and changeable beliefs. Notice how each category is defined.
Permanent beliefs lead to stable outcomes; changeable beliefs lead to fluctuation.
3
Trace the consequences of stable beliefs
If belief in benefit remains, action remains; belief in harm remains, avoidance remains; belief in equality remains, opposition remains.
This shows a one-to-one correspondence between belief and action.
4
Trace the consequences of changing beliefs
If beliefs flip—e.g., from thinking benefit preponderant to thinking harm preponderant—the agent's action flips from action to avoidance. This leads to disturbance.
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the pattern is clear.
5
Reflect on the phenomenon of disturbance
Consider why the succession of different beliefs necessarily produces disturbance in action and avoidance.
This explains real-world human indecision and inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع الثاني: إنا قد بينا أنه حصل اعتقاد أن الفعل راجح المصلحة وجب الفعل. وإذا حصل اعتقاد أن الفعل راجح المفسدة وجب الترك ثم نقول: هذه الاعتقادات قد تكون باقية، وقد تكون متغيرة، فإن كان اعتقاد رجحان جانب الصلاح باقياً، بقي الفعل، وإن كان اعتقاد رجحان جانب الفساد باقياً بقي الترك، وإن كان اعتقاد التعادل باقياً بقي التعارض، ما كان على ما كان. أما إذا كانت هذه الاعتقادات شريعة الزوال والتبدل فتارة يقع في القلب: أن الراجح هو جانب الصلاح. وفي اللحظة الثانية ينقلب فيحصل في القلب: أن الراجح هو جانب الفساد، فعند ذلك يتغير الإنسان من الفعل إلى الترك تارة. ومن الترك إلى الفعل أخرى، ففي اللحظة التي حصل في القلب اعتقاد أن جانب الصلاح أرجح يصدر عنه الفعل. وفي اللحظة الثانية التي حصل في القلب اعتقاد جانب الفساد أرجح يحصل الترك ثم لما توالت هذه الاعتقادات المختلفة والتصورات المتباينة. لا جرم يقع الاضطراب في الفعل والترك، فتارة يقدم على الفعل، وأخرى يقدم
The second branch: we have already shown that if a belief occurs that the action has preponderant benefit, action is obligatory; and if a belief occurs that the action has preponderant harm, avoidance is obligatory. Then we say: these beliefs may be permanent, or may be changeable. If the belief in the preponderance of the side of benefit remains permanent, action remains; if the belief in the preponderance of the side of harm remains permanent, avoidance remains; if the belief of equality remains permanent, opposition remains, as it was. But if these beliefs are prone to disappearance and change, then sometimes it occurs in the heart that the preponderant is the side of benefit, and in the second moment it flips such that it occurs in the heart that the preponderant is the side of harm. Then the human changes from action to avoidance sometimes, and from avoidance to action at other times. So in the moment in which the belief that the side of benefit is more preponderant occurs in the heart, action issues from him; and in the second moment in which the belief that the side of harm is more preponderant occurs in the heart, avoidance occurs. Then when these various beliefs and diverse conceptions follow one another, necessarily disturbance occurs in action and avoidance, so sometimes he proceeds to action, and at other times he proceeds...
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage discusses the second branch, concerning the effect of belief stability on action. Building on the earlier principle that belief in preponderance determines obligation, the author notes that beliefs can be either permanent (baqiya) or changeable (mutaghayyira). If belief in the preponderance of benefit is permanent, action remains constant; similarly for harm and equality. If beliefs fluctuate—alternating between thinking benefit preponderant and harm preponderant—then the agent's actions fluctuate accordingly: when the belief in benefit occurs, he acts; when the belief in harm occurs, he avoids. This succession of differing beliefs naturally leads to disturbance (idṭirāb) in action, sometimes doing, sometimes refraining. The passage concludes mid-sentence, but the pattern is clear: the fluctuation in belief causes a corresponding fluctuation in action.

Correction: The passage emphasizes that it is the subjective belief in preponderance that drives action, and changes in that belief directly cause changes in action.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Refuting the Claim that Obligation Requires Conflicting Motives
Refutes the claim that obligation requires conflicting motives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that obligation (taklif) requires conflicting motives is logically incoherent, as argued in this text.

Core Idea
Conflicting motives do not coexist; each moment compels a single action.

This passage takes on a key objection in the theology of obligation: that valid obligation only occurs when a person has conflicting motives. The author argues that this is false because at any given moment, only one motive prevails, effectively compelling the person. Thus, the alleged state of conflicting motives does not actually exist.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the English translation. Note the author's direct refutation style.
The author begins by quoting the opposing claim and immediately declares it false.
2
Analyze the argument structure
Identify the two moments the author describes: the moment with motive to act and the moment with motive to omit. Notice how he says the person is 'like one compelled' in each moment.
The author uses the concept of 'compulsion' (iljā') to show that there is no genuine conflict.
3
Appreciate the refutation's force
Consider why the author concludes that the opposing claim has 'no meaning'. Reflect on how this argument supports the possibility of obligation without conflicting motives.
If the person is always compelled by the prevailing motive, then the condition of conflicting motives is never actually met.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فكان قوله : ( التكليف إنما يصح حال ما يكون الإنسان متردد الدواعي ) كلاماً باطلاً . والذي نريد تقريره : أنه لا معنى لكونه متردد الدواعي إلى الفعل وإلى الترك إلا أن يكون الحاصل في اللحظة الأولى هو الداعي إلى الفعل ، ويكون الحاصل في اللحظة الثانية هو الداعي إلى الترك إلا أنه في اللحظة التي حصل فيها الداعي إلى الفعل يكون الإنسان كالملجأ إلى الفعل . وفي اللحظة التي حصل فيها الداعي إلى الترك يكون الإنسان كالملجأ إلى الترك . وعلى هذا التقدير فلا يبقى لقولهم : إن التكليف إنما يصح حال كون الإنسان متردد الدواعي إلى الفعل وإلى الترك معنى .
Then his statement: (Obligation is only valid in the state when a person has conflicting motives) is false speech. And what we intend to establish: that there is no meaning to his having conflicting motives towards action and towards omission, except that what occurs in the first moment is the motive towards action, and what occurs in the second moment is the motive towards omission, except that in the moment in which the motive towards action occurs, the person is like one compelled towards action, and in the moment in which the motive towards omission occurs, the person is like one compelled towards omission. And on this estimation, there remains no meaning to their statement: 'Obligation is only valid in the state when a person has conflicting motives towards action and towards omission.'
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of an opposing view regarding the condition for valid obligation (taklif). The author begins by quoting the opposing claim: 'Obligation is only valid in the state when a person has conflicting motives.' He immediately declares it false speech (kalam bāṭil). Then he proceeds to establish his own position. He argues that there is no real meaning to having conflicting motives simultaneously; rather, at any given moment, only one motive is present—either the motive towards action or the motive towards omission. In the moment the motive to act occurs, the person is compelled (mulja’) to act; similarly, when the motive to omit occurs, he is compelled to omit. Therefore, the person is never truly in a state of conflicting motives but is rather compelled in each successive moment. Consequently, the claim that obligation is valid only when motives conflict loses all meaning, since such a state does not genuinely exist.

Key Terms
تكليف
تردد الدواعي
إلجاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That conflicting motives provide a basis for genuine choice.
Correction: The author argues that conflicting motives cannot coexist simultaneously; each moment compels action, so there is no genuine conflict.

Checkpoint

A) It is valid.
B) It is invalid because the person is compelled in each moment.
C) It is valid only if the person can choose.
D) It is invalid because motives are irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
The claim that obligation requires conflicting motives is false because such a state cannot exist without compulsion in each moment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

According to this lesson, that feeling of being torn is actually a sequence of moments where each motive overwhelms you. How does this realization affect your understanding of your own responsibility in those moments? How will you live with the weight of that accountability tomorrow?
?
The opponent claimed that obligation requires conflicting motives. But if motives compel in each moment, then when is a person truly free to obey or disobey? This lesson challenges the very foundation of moral responsibility. If you cannot find a moment of genuine choice, what is the basis for your hope in divine judgment? Search your heart: do you believe you are truly responsible, or is this argument making you uncomfortable? Commit to seeking an answer from the Qur'an and Sunnah.

Beliefs, Changing Motives, and the Person of Sudden Thoughts
Explains the effect of changing beliefs on action and describes the person of sudden thoughts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how changing beliefs about benefit and harm lead to instability in action, and what it means to be a person of sudden thoughts (dhu badawat).

Core Idea
When beliefs change, actions follow; a person of sudden thoughts has no constancy.

This passage from Al-Matalib al-Aliyah explores the psychological dynamics behind human action. Building on the principle that action is caused by capability and motives, the author explains how fluctuating beliefs about benefit and harm produce instability, and how some individuals are described as 'people of sudden thoughts.' This lesson unpacks that description and its implications for understanding human behavior.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Get a sense of the author's argument about constant vs. changing beliefs.
The passage is complex; take your time.
2
Identify the two cases
Notice the distinction between beliefs that remain constant and those that change. How does each case affect action or omission?
The author uses 'bāqiyan' (remaining) and 'mutaghayyira' (changing).
3
Focus on 'dhu badawat'
Find the description of a person with sudden thoughts. What does the author say about such a person's constancy and why?
The phrase 'dhū badawāt' is key.
4
Reflect on the concluding observation
Read the final sentence about the intellect's awareness of interests and harms. How does this relate to stability or confusion?
The author ends with an epistemological point.

الفرع الثاني : إنا قد بينا أنه حصل اعتقاد أن الفعل راجح المصلحة وجب الفعل . وإذا حصل اعتقاد أن الفعل راجح المفسدة وجب الترك ثم نقول : هذه الاعتقادات قد تكون باقية ، وقد تكون متغيرة ، فإن كان اعتقاد رجحان جانب الصلاح باقياً ، بقي الفعل ، وإن كان اعتقاد رجحان جانب الفساد باقياً بقى الترك، وإن كان اعتقاد التعادل باقياً بقي التعارض ، ما كان على ما كان . أما إذا كانت هذه الاعتقادات شريعة الزوال والتبدل فتارة يقع في القلب : أن الراجح هو جانب الصلاح . وفي اللحظة الثانية ينقلب فيحصل في القلب : أن الراجح هو جانب الفساد ، فعند ذلك يتغير الإنسان من الفعل إلى الترك تارة . ومن الترك إلى الفعل أخرى ، ففي اللحظة التي حصل في القلب اعتقاد أن جانب الصلاح أرجح يصدر عنه الفعل . وفي اللحظة الثانية التي حصل في القلب اعتقاد جانب الفساد أرجح يحصل الترك ثم لما توالت هذه الاعتقادات المختلفة والتصورات المتباينة . لا جرم يقع الاضطراب في الفعل والترك ، فتارة يقدم على الفعل ، وأخرى يقدم على الترك . والناس يقولون لصاحب هذه الخواطر المختلفة إنه إنسان ذو بدوات ، وأنه ليس له في شيء من الأفعال ثبات ومعنى كونه ذو بداوات: إنه تارة يبدو له كون الفعل راجح الصلاح، وتارة أخرى يبدو له كون الفعل راجع الفساد . وإنما كان مثل هذا الإنسان قليل الثبات ، لأنه لما كان الفعل معللا بالقدر مع الدواعي ، فعند تغير الدواعي يجب تغير الفعل . فالإنسان الذي يكون ذا بداوات ، لا بد وأن يكون له في شيء من الأفعال ثبات واعلم : أن الجهات المعتبرة بحسب المصالح والمفاسد كثيرة خارجة عن الضبط ، وكلما كان وقوف العقل عليها أكثر كانت الحيرة والاضطراب أكثر ، وكلما كان وقوف العقل عليها أقل كان البقاء والثبات على الطريق الواحد أقل .
The second branch: We have shown that when a belief occurs that an action is preponderant in benefit, the action is obligatory. And when a belief occurs that an action is preponderant in harm, omission is obligatory. Then we say: These beliefs may be lasting, and may be changing. If the belief in the preponderance of the side of benefit remains, the action remains. If the belief in the preponderance of the side of harm remains, omission remains. If the belief in equivalence remains, the conflict remains, as it was. But if these beliefs are liable to vanish and change, then sometimes it occurs in the heart that the preponderant is the side of benefit, and in the second moment it reverses and occurs in the heart that the preponderant is the side of harm. At that time, the person changes from action to omission sometimes, and from omission to action another time. So in the moment when the belief that the side of benefit is more preponderant occurs in the heart, action issues from him. And in the second moment when the belief that the side of harm is more preponderant occurs in the heart, omission occurs. Then when these different beliefs and diverse conceptions follow one another, inevitably disturbance occurs in action and omission, so sometimes he proceeds to action, and another time proceeds to omission. And people say of the one who has these varying thoughts that he is a person of sudden thoughts (dhu badawat), and that he has no constancy in any of his actions. And the meaning of his being of sudden thoughts: that sometimes it appears to him that the action is preponderant in benefit, and another time it appears to him that the action is preponderant in harm. And such a person is only of little constancy, because since action is caused by capability (qudra) together with motives, when motives change, action must change. So the person who is of sudden thoughts must have no constancy in any of his actions. And know: the aspects considered according to interests and harms are many, beyond control. And whenever the intellect's stop [awareness] of them is greater, confusion and disturbance are greater. And whenever the intellect's stop of them is less, permanence and constancy upon a single path are less.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage is the second branch of the argument, explaining the relationship between beliefs and actions. The author begins by reiterating that if one holds the belief that an action is preponderant in benefit, the action becomes obligatory; if the belief is that it is preponderant in harm, omission becomes obligatory. He then notes that these beliefs can be constant or changeable. If constant, the corresponding action or omission remains. If the belief alternates, the action alternates accordingly. He describes a person whose beliefs change rapidly as 'dhu badawat' (one of sudden thoughts), someone who lacks constancy because his beliefs shift. The author then links this to the principle that action is caused by capability (qudra) combined with motives; when motives change, the action changes. He adds a general observation that the aspects of interests and harms are numerous and beyond complete grasp; greater awareness of them leads to more confusion, while less awareness leads to more stability on a single path. This underscores the psychological and epistemological dimensions of human action.

No Effect of Belief in Harm on Omission
Proves that belief in harm's preponderance has no effect on omission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that belief in harm's preponderance has no causal effect on omission, and how this connects to the principles of capability and motive.

Core Idea
Belief in harm does not cause omission; omission is merely the default when no cause for action exists.

In this passage, the author continues the discussion of omission by examining whether belief that an action is harmful plays any role in causing omission. He argues that it does not: omission occurs regardless of whether such a belief is present. This conclusion reinforces the earlier findings that neither capability nor motive have any effect on omission. Let's see how he builds this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the translation. Pay attention to the logical flow.
The passage uses technical terms like 'رجحان المفسدة' and 'عدم العلة'.
2
Identify the two scenarios
The author presents two cases: one with belief in harm's preponderance and one without. Notice that in both, omission occurs.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that belief in harm has no effect on omission. He then links this to earlier proofs about capability and motive.
The final sentence is cut off but the argument is complete.

The third branch: We have mentioned that when an action is preponderant in harm in the belief of the agent, omission is necessary. And if no belief in obtaining benefit occurs in it, omission is necessary. If you have understood this, we say: There is no meaning to omission except the thing's remaining upon the original non-existence. So if, on the assumption that a belief in preponderance of harm occurs, there occurs only omission. And on the assumption of absence of belief in preponderance, neither in the action nor in omission, there occurs only omission. Thus it appears that there is no effect at all for belief in its being preponderant in harm. Rather, if a belief occurs that it is preponderant in action, action occurs. And if this belief does not occur, the action remains upon its original non-existence, based on the principle that the cause of non-existence is the absence of the cause. Therefore, there is no effect at all for belief that this action is preponderant in harm upon omission. This indicates that belief in its being preponderant in harm has no effect at all upon omission. And we had already proven at the beginning of this chapter that capability (qudra) has no effect at all upon omission. So it has become clear through the investigation we have mentioned: that capability has no effect at all upon omission, and that the motive (da'iya) has no effect at all upon omission. And that establishes what we mentioned: that the reliance upon
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is the third branch, which aims to show that belief in the preponderance of harm has no actual causal effect on omission. The author first clarifies that omission simply means the thing remaining in its original non-existence. He analyzes two scenarios: (1) when a belief in preponderance of harm occurs, omission follows; (2) when no such belief occurs, omission still follows because no cause for action is present. In both cases, omission occurs regardless of the belief. Therefore, the belief in harm's preponderance cannot be said to be a cause of omission; it is merely concomitant. The true cause of action is the belief in preponderance of benefit. If that belief is absent, the action remains non-existent due to the absence of its cause. The author then connects this to an earlier conclusion that capability (qudra) also has no effect on omission, and similarly the motive (da'iya) has no effect. Thus, the whole discussion reinforces the main theme that neither power nor motive nor belief in harm produces omission; it is simply the default state when no action-causing factor is present.

Definition of Benefit and Harm and Their Varying Degrees
Defines benefit and harm and argues for their unlimited degrees.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of benefit and harm as pleasure/pain and the rational and empirical proofs that they admit of unlimited degrees.

Core Idea
Benefit and harm vary endlessly in strength, both by reason and experience.

Before discussing divine actions, we must clarify what benefit and harm mean. The author defines them in terms of pleasure and pain, then demonstrates that they come in countless degrees. This lesson will walk you through the definitions and two key arguments for their infinite variation.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions of benefit and harm
Start with the opening lines where manfa'a and mafsada are defined. Note that they are tied to pleasure/joy and pain/grief, or anything that leads to them.
This establishes the conceptual foundation.
2
Examine the first proof: intuitive variation
The first aspect argues that pleasure and pain are self-evidently subject to degrees. Stronger and weaker pleasures/pains exist, and the mind can conceive of even stronger ones without limit.
This is a rational, a priori argument.
3
Examine the second proof: empirical variation
The second aspect uses experience: the same thing can be pleasurable to one person and painful to another, and its intensity varies across individuals. This shows relativism of benefit and harm.
This is an empirical, a posteriori argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المنفعة يكون مؤدياً إليهما، أو إلى أحدهما. والمفسدة عبارة عن الألم والغم، أو ما يكون مؤدياً إليهما أو إلى أحدهما. إذا عرفت هذا، فنقول: يجب أن تكون المنافع والمضار مختلفة المراتب والدرجات بحسب كونه منافع ومضار. وبيان من وجوه: الثالث: إنا بينا أن المنفعة عبارة عن اللذة أو السرور، أو ما الوجه الأول: إن اللذة والسرور قابلان للأشد والأضعف. فقد تكون لذة أقوى من لذة وسرور أقوى من سرور، وكذلك فقد يكون ألم أقوى من ألم، وغم أقوى من غم. والعلم بكون هذه الأنواع قابلة للأشد والأضعف علم ضروري، لا شبهة للعاقل فيه. وأيضاً فدرجات هذا التفاوت غير مضبوطة ولا محصورة، فإنه ما من مرتبة من مراتب الألم إلا ويجوز العقل وجود ألم آخر أقوى منه، وكذا القول في الألم واللذة والسرور. ثم من المعلوم أن الرغبة في اللذة القوية أقوى من الرغبة في اللذة الضعيفة، والرغبة في السرور القوي أقوى من الرغبة في السرور الضعيف، وكذا القول في الألم والغم. فلما كانت درجات هذه الأغراض في القوة والضعف غير مضبوطة كانت درجات المنافع والمضار كثيرة غير مضبوطة. والوجه الثاني في تقرير هذا التفاوت: إن التجربة دلت على أن الشيء الواحد قد يكون لذيذاً عند شخص، ويكون هو بعينه مكروهاً عند شخص آخر. وأيضاً فقد يكون في غاية اللذة عند شخص ومتوسط الحال عند شخص ثاني، وفي غاية النفرة عند ثالث. وأيضاً قد يكون موقع الألم القليل عند شخص أكثر من موقع الألم العظيم عند شخص آخر. ولما كان اختلاف
Benefit (manfa'a) is what leads to them (pleasure and joy) or to one of them. Harm (mafsada) is pain (alam) and grief (ghamm), or what leads to them or to one of them. If you understand this, we say: it is necessary that benefits and harms differ in ranks and degrees according to their being benefits and harms. The exposition from aspects: the third [aspect]: we have shown that benefit is pleasure (ladhdha) or joy (surur) or what... The first aspect: pleasure and joy are susceptible to the stronger and the weaker. There may be a pleasure stronger than a pleasure and a joy stronger than a joy, and likewise there may be a pain stronger than a pain and a grief stronger than a grief. And the knowledge that these types are susceptible to the stronger and the weaker is necessary knowledge, concerning which no rational person has any doubt. Also, the degrees of this variation are unregulated and countless, for there is no rank among the ranks of pain except that the intellect allows the existence of another pain stronger than it, and the same applies to pain, pleasure, and joy. Further, it is known that the desire for a strong pleasure is stronger than the desire for a weak pleasure, and the desire for a strong joy is stronger than the desire for a weak joy, and the same applies to pain and grief. Since the degrees of these ends in strength and weakness are unregulated, the degrees of benefits and harms are many and unregulated. The second aspect in establishing this variation: experience indicates that one and the same thing may be pleasurable to one person, yet the very same thing may be disliked by another person. Also, it may be extremely pleasurable to one person, of moderate state to a second person, and extremely repugnant to a third. Also, a slight pain may have more significance for one person than a great pain for another. And since variation...
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage defines benefit and harm and argues for their varying degrees. The author first defines benefit (manfa'a) as pleasure (ladhdha) or joy (surur), or what leads to them; harm (mafsada) as pain (alam) or grief (ghamm), or what leads to them. He then states that benefits and harms necessarily vary in ranks and degrees. He presents three aspects for this, but only the first and second are preserved in this text. The first aspect relies on intuitive knowledge: pleasure and pain admit of stronger and weaker degrees – this is self-evident. Moreover, the degrees are unlimited; for any pain, a stronger pain is conceivable. Consequently, desires for stronger pleasures are stronger, and since the ends vary in strength, benefits and harms also vary unboundedly. The second aspect relies on empirical observation: the same thing can be pleasurable to one person and disliked to another, and its intensity differs across persons. A slight pain may affect one person more than a great pain affects another. Thus, benefits and harms are relative and varied.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How can this lesson reshape your empathy?
?

Are you pursuing pleasures that are truly strong and lasting, or are you settling for weak ones? Will you re-evaluate what you consider 'beneficial' and 'harmful' in light of the endless degrees that reason and experience reveal?

Six Proofs for the Differentiation of Benefits and Harms
The author defines benefit and harm and gives six proofs for their varying degrees.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six rational proofs that benefits and harms vary in degree, and why this variation is necessary for theological reasoning.

Core Idea
Benefits and harms are not uniform; their degrees are determined by multiple objective and subjective factors.

This passage lays a foundational principle for Islamic theology: that benefits and harms differ in rank and degree. The author first defines key terms, then presents six philosophical proofs ranging from the intrinsic intensity of pleasure and pain to empirical observations and intermediate factors. Understanding this variation is essential for grasping moral responsibility and divine justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview. Notice the author begins with definitions before listing six distinct proofs.
The passage is dense; read it once without stopping for analysis.
2
Identify the six proofs and their logic
Each proof is introduced by 'الوجه الأول' etc. Identify each proof's core argument: how it demonstrates variation in benefits and harms.
Make note of whether the proof is based on intrinsic qualities, experience, or relational factors.
3
Reflect on the significance of variation
Consider why establishing this variation matters for theology. It shows that divine commands and human responsibilities take into account the degree of benefit and harm.
This connects to broader concepts of divine wisdom and justice.

الوجه الأول : إن اللذة والسرور قابلان للأشد ، والأضعف . فقد تكون لذة أقوى من لذة وسرور أقوى من سرور ، وكذلك فقد يكون ألم أقوى من ألم ، وغم أقوى من غم. والعلم يكون هذه الأنواع قابلة للأشد والأضرف علم ضروري ، لا شبهة للعاقل فيه . وأيضاً : فدرجات هذا التفاوت غير مضبوطة ولا محصورة ، فإنه مرتبة من مراتب الألم إلا ويجوز العقل وجود الم آخر ، أقوى منه : وكذا القول في الفم واللذة والسرور . ثم من المعلوم أن الرغبة في اللذة القوية أقوى من الرغبة في اللغة الضعيفة ، والرغبة في السرور القوي أقوى من الرغبة في السرور الضعيف وكذا القول في الألم والغم . فلما كانت درجات هذه الأغراض في القوة والضعف غير مضبوطة كانت درجات المنافع والمضار كثيرة غير مضبوطة ..
والوجه الثاني في تقرير هذا التفاوت : إن التجربة دلت على أن الشيء الواحد قد يكون لذيذاً عند شخص ، ويكون هو بعينه مكروهاً عند شخص آخر. وأيضاً فقد يكون في غاية اللذة عند شخص ومتوسط الحال عند شخص ثاني . وفي غاية النفرة عند ثالث . وأيضاً : قد يكون موقع الألم القليل عند شخص أكثر من موقع الألم العظيم عند شخص آخر . ولما كان اختلاف النفوس في جواهرها وفي أمزجتها، وفي أحوالها خارجاً عن الضبط، كانت أيضاً مراتب المنفعة والمضرة غير مضبوطة ، بحسب هذا الاعتبار .
الوجه الثالث في بيان التفاوت : إنا بينا أن المطلوب بالذات هو اللذة والسرور ، وإن المكروه بالذات هو الألم والغم ، وأن ما سوى هذه الأشياء ، فهو مطلوب بالتبع أو مكروه بالتبع ، ومن المعلوم : أن الرغبة فيها يكون مطلوباً بالذات أقوى من الرغبة فيما يكون مطلوباً بالتبعية ، فقد حصل التفاوت في مراتب المصالح والمفاسد بحسب هذا الاعتبار .
والوجه الرابع في بيان هذا التفاوت : أن المطلوب بالذات ، وإن كان راجحاً على المطلوب بالغير، إلا أنه قد لا يبعد أن يصير المطلوب بالذات مرجوحاً بالنسبة إلى المطلوب بالغير، لأجل أن المطلوب بالغير أقوى وأشد فائدة من المطلوب بالذات مثل : أن وجدان غيره دنانير ، أسر من أكل اللقمة الواحدة.
والوجه الخامس : إن المؤدي إلى المطلوب بالذات قد يكون مؤدياً إليه بواسطة واحدة ، وقد يكون مؤدياً إليه بواسطتين . وكذا القول في الوسائط الثلاثة والأربعة والخمسة ، وهلم جرا ، إلى مراتب لا يضبطها الحس والعقل . ومعلوم أن الرغبة فيما يفضي إلى المطلوب بالذات بوسائط قليلة فوق الرغبة فيما يفضي إليه بوسائط كثيرة ، لكن ههنا اعتبار آخر ، وهو أن الذي تكثر فيه الوسائط قد يكون أقوى وأعظم فيصبر معادلاً لما تقل الوسائط فيه بسبب أن الكيفية من أحد الجانبين تقابل الكيفية من الجانب الآخر.
والوجه السادس : إن التجربة تدل على أن الشيء الواحد قد يكون منشئاً للخير والمصلحة من وجه وللشر والمفسدة من وجه آخر . ومن المعلوم أن الشيء
The powerful agent is nothing but the existence of the act. As for its non-existence, there is no influence of power in it, nor influence of the motive. And God knows best. The division is that it leads to both of them, or to one of them. And harm (mafsadah) is an expression for pain (alam) and grief (ghamm), or that which leads to both or to one of them. When you know this, we say: It is necessary that benefits (manafi') and harms (madarr) are different in ranks and degrees according to their being benefits and harms. And an exposition of it from aspects: The first aspect: Indeed pleasure (ladhdhah) and delight (surur) are susceptible to intensity and weakness. So there may be a pleasure stronger than a pleasure and a delight stronger than a delight, and likewise there may be a pain stronger than a pain and a grief stronger than a grief. And knowledge that these types are susceptible to intensity and weakness is necessary knowledge, no doubt about it for a rational person. Also, the degrees of this variation are not limited nor enumerated, for every rank of pain, the intellect permits the existence of another pain stronger than it; and likewise the statement concerning grief, pleasure, and delight. Then it is known that desire for a strong pleasure is stronger than desire for a weak pleasure, and desire for a strong delight is stronger than desire for a weak delight; and likewise the statement concerning pain and grief. Since the degrees of these objects in strength and weakness are not limited, the degrees of benefits and harms become many and unlimited. The second aspect in establishing this variation: Indeed experience indicates that one and the same thing may be pleasurable for one person, and the very same thing may be disliked by another person. Also, it may be extremely pleasurable for one person and moderate for a second, and extremely repulsive for a third. Also, the significance of a small pain for one person may be greater than the significance of a great pain for another. And since the difference of souls in their essences, their temperaments, and their states is beyond control, the ranks of benefit and harm also become uncontrolled according to this consideration. The third aspect in explaining the variation: Indeed we have shown that what is desired for itself (al-matlab bi al-dhat) is pleasure and delight, and what is disliked for itself is pain and grief, and that whatever is other than these things is desired as a consequence or disliked as a consequence. And it is known that desire for what is desired for itself is stronger than desire for what is desired as a consequence; thus the variation in the ranks of interests (masalih) and harms (mafasid) occurs according to this consideration. The fourth aspect in explaining this variation: That what is desired for itself, although it is preponderant over what is desired for another, yet it is not unlikely that what is desired for itself becomes outweighed in comparison to what is desired for another, because what is desired for another is stronger and more beneficial than what is desired for itself—such as finding gold coins being more joyful than eating a single morsel. The fifth aspect: That which leads to what is desired for itself may lead to it through one intermediary, and may lead to it through two intermediaries; and likewise the statement concerning three, four, five intermediaries, and so on, up to ranks that sense and intellect cannot control. And it is known that desire for what leads to what is desired for itself through few intermediaries is above desire for what leads to it through many intermediaries. But here there is another consideration: that which has many intermediaries may be stronger and greater, so it becomes equivalent to that which has few intermediaries due to the quality on one side countering the quality on the other side. The sixth aspect: Indeed experience indicates that one and the same thing may give rise to good and benefit in one aspect and evil and harm in another aspect. And it is known that the thing...
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is a proof that benefits and harms have varying degrees and ranks. The author begins by defining 'benefit' (manfa'ah) as pleasure (ladhdhah) or delight (surur) or what leads to them, and 'harm' (mafsadah) as pain (alam) or grief (ghamm) or what leads to them. Then he states that it is necessary that benefits and harms differ in rank and degree. He provides six aspects (wujuh) to establish this variation. The first aspect argues that pleasure and pain themselves are susceptible to intensity and weakness, and since there is no limit to the degrees of intensity, the ranks of benefits and harms are unlimited. The second aspect uses empirical evidence: the same thing can be experienced as pleasurable by one person and painful by another, and even for the same person the intensity varies, and because souls (essences, temperaments, states) differ beyond control, the ranks vary accordingly. The third aspect notes that what is desired for itself (pleasure/delight) is stronger in desire than what is desired as a consequence, creating a natural variation in interests. The fourth aspect shows that even though what is desired for itself is normally preponderant, a strong desired-for-other can outweigh it, as in preferring money over a small pleasure. The fifth aspect considers the number of intermediaries to the desired end: fewer intermediaries create stronger desire, but the quality of the end can compensate, leading to equivalence. The sixth aspect points out that a single thing can produce both benefit and harm from different angles, further showing variation. Throughout, the author uses logical and empirical arguments to demonstrate that benefits and harms are not uniform but rather infinitely differentiated.

Checkpoint

A) Because pleasure and pain are not real.
B) Because pleasure and pain are susceptible to intensity and weakness without limit.
C) Because benefits are always stronger than harms.
D) Because experience shows they are the same for everyone.

Key Takeaway
Benefits and harms vary infinitely in degree due to intrinsic, empirical, and relational factors, forming a foundation for moral reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How does this lesson's proof that desires are strongest for what is desired for itself expose the areas where you are most vulnerable to prioritising short-term gratification over eternal reward? What specific step will you take today to rebalance your desires?

According to the second proof, the same thing can be beneficial to one and harmful to another. How can you learn to see beyond your own perspective to discern true benefit in your interactions? Whose counsel do you need to seek?
?

In your life, are there pursuits (like wealth or status) that you have allowed to eclipse the intrinsic good of drawing closer to Allah? What specific change in your daily priorities is required for you to realign with the truth that the ultimate desired-for-itself is the pleasure of Allah, and everything else is merely instrumental?

Two Proofs That Power Does Not Require Knowledge
Two proofs that a powerful agent can act without knowing details.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two rational arguments demonstrating that an agent's power over something does not entail detailed knowledge of it.

Core Idea
Power can exist without detailed knowledge.

This passage presents two clear arguments to separate the concepts of power and knowledge. The first uses a simple physical example, while the second relies on a logical consequence about necessary knowledge. By studying these proofs, you'll see why capability does not imply detailed awareness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Get a feel for the two arguments.
2
Examine the first argument (finger movement)
Notice how the example of moving a finger without knowing its constituent parts shows that power does not require knowledge.
This is a concrete analogy.
3
Examine the second argument (disagreement on actions)
This argument uses a conditional: if power required knowledge, then knowledge would be necessary; since it is not, power can exist without knowledge.
This is a logical / dialectical proof.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Both arguments together firmly establish that capability does not imply knowing every detail. Think about why this matters for both human and divine agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على عدد أجزاء تلك الإصبع، وبعدد أجزاء تلك المسافة، فيثبت أن القادر قد يكون قادراً على ما لا يعلمه ولا يعتقده، ولا يظنه. الثاني: إن الناس اختلفوا في أن الأجناس المقدورة للعباد كم هي؟ فلو لزم من كونه قادراً على الشيء، كونه عالماً به، لزم أن يكون العلم بالأجناس المقدورة للعباد علماً ضرورياً، ولما لم يكن كذلك، ثبت أنا قد نقدر على أشياء مع أنا لا نعلمها.
According to the number of parts of that finger, and by the number of parts of that distance, it is established that the powerful agent may be powerful over what he does not know, nor believe, nor suppose. Second: People have disagreed about how many kinds of actions are within the power of servants. If it followed from his being powerful over a thing that he is knowledgeable of it, then knowledge of the kinds of actions within the power of servants would be necessary knowledge. Since it is not so, it is established that we can be powerful over things while not knowing them.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage provides two arguments to prove that an agent can have power over something without having knowledge of it. The first argument uses a concrete example: a person can move his finger along a distance without knowing the exact number of parts involved in that movement, yet he is capable of performing the action. Thus, capability does not imply detailed knowledge. The second argument is dialectical: if capability required knowledge, then knowledge of the kinds of actions within human power would be innate (necessary knowledge). Since such knowledge is not innate, capability must be possible without knowledge. The passage thus establishes a key point in the debate on the relationship between divine power and knowledge, and also applies to human agency.

Misconception: Being powerful over something implies knowing every detail about it.
Correction: The passage shows that one can have power over things one does not know in detail, as exemplified by the finger movement and the non-necessity of knowing all possible actions.
