Refutation of the Distinction: Possibility Necessitates a Preponderator
The author refutes a previous distinction and argues that possibility necessitates a preponderator unconditionally.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that possibility itself necessitates a preponderator, regardless of any external conditions.

Core Idea
Possibility alone demands a preponderator; no extra condition is needed.

In the previous lesson, a distinction was made that requiring a preponderator for a capable agent leads to absurdity. Here, the author refutes that distinction. He argues that the very nature of possibility — something that can either exist or not — requires an external cause to tip the balance. He then considers and rejects a potential condition, showing that the need for a preponderator is unconditional.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
The author begins by stating that the distinction is rejected. Locate his core reasoning: whenever something is equally possible to exist or not, a preponderator is needed.
Notice the phrase 'كلما علمنا' — whenever we know.
2
Understand the foundation: possibility as the source of need
The author asserts that the intellect judges that the need for an external cause arises from the very concept of possibility (imkan) and permissibility (jawaz).
This is the key premise.
3
Follow the conditional argument
The author considers whether permissibility only necessitates a preponderator if it is known that no harm results. He shows that this condition is impossible to fulfill except by a weak premise.
Pay attention to the logical structure: either/or.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The author concludes that the condition is invalid, and the only consistent options are that possibility always or never needs a cause. The context implies it always does.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : إن هذا الفرق مدفوع . وذلك لأنا كلما علمنا في الشيء أنه يجوز حصوله ويجوز عدمه، وليس لأحد الطرفين مزية على الآخر بوجه من الوجوه ، قطع عقلنا بأنه رجحان أحد الطرفين على الآخر ، إلا المرجح ، ثبت هذا : أن العقل حاكم بأن منشأ الحاجة إلى المؤثر المنفصل هو مسمى الإمكان . والجواز . وإذا كان كذلك ، فأينها حصل الجواز والإمكان وجب أن يحصل الافطار إلى المؤثر ، وعلى هذا التقدير فالفرق المذكور ساقط . وأيضاً : نقول : الجواز من حيث هو جواز : إما أن يكون محوجاً إلى المرجح ، أو لا يكون إلا بشرط أن يعلم أنه لا يفضي إلى باطل ومحذور. فإن كان الحق هو الأول لزم من حصول الجواز أينما كان ، حصول الحاجة، أينما كانت وهو المطلوب وأما إن قلنا : إن الجواز إنما يحوج إلى المقتضى بشرط أن لا يفضي إلى محذور ومفسدة ) فنقول : هذا باطل لأن أقصى ما في الباب أن نعلم أنه لا يلزم منه المفسدة الفلانية ، والفلانية . فأما أن نعلم أنه لا يلزم منه شيء من المفاسد أصلاً ، فذلك مما لا سبيل إليه ، إلا إذا قلنا : إن عدم علمنا بالشيء علمنا بعدم ذلك الشيء، إلا أن هذه المقدمة في غاية الضعف، وإن وقفنا كون الجواز علة للحاجة إلى المرجح على هذه المقدمة لزم بطلان هذا الأصل بالكلية ، فعلمنا أن كون الجواز علة للحاجة لا يعتبر فيه هذا القيد اصلا ، بل وجب الحكم عليه : إما بأنه لا يحوج إلى المؤثر البتة ، أو بأنه يحوج إلى المؤثر على الإطلاق . والله أعلم
And the answer: This distinction is rejected. That is because whenever we know about a thing that it is possible for it to occur and possible for it not to occur, and neither side has any advantage over the other in any respect, our intellect judges that the preponderance of one side over the other is only due to a preponderator. This is established: the intellect rules that the origin of the need for an external efficient cause is the very notion of possibility (imkan) and permissibility (jawaz). And if this is so, wherever possibility and permissibility occur, the need for an efficient cause must occur, and on this estimation the aforementioned distinction is invalid. Furthermore, we say: Permissibility (jawaz) in itself—either it necessitates a preponderator, or it only does so under the condition that it is known that it does not lead to falsehood and harm. If the first is correct, then from the occurrence of permissibility wherever it occurs, the occurrence of need wherever it occurs follows, and that is what is sought. But if we say that permissibility only necessitates the efficient cause under the condition that it does not lead to harm and corruption, then we say: This is false, because the utmost in this matter is that we know that it does not entail a particular corruption. As for knowing that it does not entail any corruption at all, that is impossible to attain, unless we say that our lack of knowledge of a thing constitutes knowledge of the non-existence of that thing—but this premise is extremely weak. And if we make the fact that permissibility is a cause for the need of a preponderator conditional upon this premise, then the total invalidation of this principle would follow. Thus we know that the condition that permissibility is a cause for need is not considered at all; rather it must be judged either that it does not necessitate an efficient cause at all, or that it necessitates an efficient cause absolutely. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is the author's refutation (al-jawab) of the distinction made in the previous lesson. The author argues that the distinction is rejected because the intellect recognizes that any possible thing—something that can both exist and not exist—requires a preponderator (murajjih) to tip the scales. The need for an external cause (mu'aththir) arises from the very nature of possibility (imkan) and permissibility (jawaz). Therefore, wherever possibility is found, the need for a cause is found, and so the previous distinction (that requiring a preponderator for a capable agent leads to absurdity) is invalid. The author then considers a possible objection: perhaps permissibility only necessitates a preponderator if it is known that no harm or falsehood results from it. He rejects this condition because we can never know that no harm whatsoever results; we can only know that a specific harm does not result. The only way to know general harmlessness is the fallacious premise that ignorance implies nonexistence. Thus the condition is untenable, and the only consistent positions are either that permissibility never needs a cause or that it always needs a cause. The author clearly supports the latter, as implied.

اقتضاء (iqtida')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the need for a preponderator only applies to possibilities that are known to be harmless or beneficial.
Correction: The author argues that the need for a preponderator arises from the very nature of possibility itself, not from any additional condition. The condition of harmlessness is impossible to know universally, so it cannot be a requirement.

Fourth Proof: An Act Without a Motive Is Impossible
Fourth proof using independence of capability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that capability alone is insufficient for an act, necessitating the role of a motive.

Core Idea
If capability were independent, motives would be pointless—but they aren't.

This lesson presents the fourth proof that a motive is necessary for an act. The author argues that if mere capability were sufficient, then motives would have no effect. Since we know motives do matter, capability must not be independent. A clear example of a stone's weight versus its color illustrates the point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the flow.
Notice the conditional structure: 'If X were the case, then Y would follow; but Y is false, so X is false.'
2
Understand the entailment
The author explains why independence of capability would eliminate the role of motive. Grasp the principle: if an attribute is independently sufficient, any addition is superfluous.
This is a key logical move in the proof.
3
Analyze the stone analogy
The example of the stone's weight vs. color clarifies the principle. Reflect on why color doesn't affect falling, and how this parallels capability and motive.
The analogy makes the abstract argument concrete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : على أن الفعل بدون الداعي محال أن نقول : لو كان مجرد كون القادر قادراً كافياً في حصول الفعل، لامتنع أن يحصل للداعي أثر في الترجيح في شيء من المواضع . وهذا باطل . فذاك باطل . بيان الملازمة : أن الوصف إذا كان مستقلاً باقتضاء الأثر ، فإذا انضم إليه شيء آخر ، فقد انضم هذا الزائد إلى شيء كان في نفسه مستقلاً باقتضاء الأثر ، وإذا كان كذلك ، فقد وقع الأثر بذلك المستقل ولا يبقى لهذا الزائد فيه أثر البتة ، لأن الشيء الواحد، لا يكون واجباً لذاته ولغيره معاً . ومثاله : أنه لما كان ثقل الحجر مستقلاً باقتضاء الهوى ، لا جرم لم يبق لكون الحجر أسود، أو أبيض، أثر في اقتضاء ذلك النزول ، فكذا ههنا، لو كان مجرد كونه قادراً مستقلاً باقتضاء الفعل لامتنع أن يحصل للداعية المنضمة إلى القادرية أثر في اقتضاء الفعل، فيثبت بما ذكرنا أن القادرية لو كانت مستقلة
The fourth proof: that the act without a motive is impossible. We say: If the mere fact that the capable agent is capable were sufficient for the occurrence of the act, then it would be impossible for the motive to have any effect in preponderating in any situation. This is false, so that is false. Explanation of the entailment: If a description is independent in requiring the effect, and then something else is joined to it, then this added thing has been joined to something that is in itself independent in requiring the effect. If this is the case, then the effect occurs by that independent thing, and there remains for this added thing no effect in it at all, because one thing cannot be necessary by itself and by another simultaneously. An example: Because the weight of a stone is independent in requiring falling, therefore the fact that the stone is black or white has no effect in requiring that descent. Similarly here, if the mere fact of being capable were independent in requiring the act, then it would be impossible for the motive, when combined with capability, to have any effect in requiring the act. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned that if capability were independent...
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is the fourth proof (al-hujjah al-rabi'ah) that an act without a motive (da'i) is impossible. The author presents a conditional argument: if mere capability were sufficient for the act, then the motive would have no role in causing the act. But we know that motives do play a role (this is taken as self-evident). Therefore, capability alone is not sufficient. The reasoning is: if an attribute (here, capability) is independently sufficient to produce an effect, then any additional factor (like motive) would be superfluous and could not contribute any effect. This is illustrated by the example of a stone: its weight alone suffices to make it fall, so its color (black or white) has no effect on the falling. Similarly, if capability were independent, the motive could not influence the act. But motives do influence acts, so capability cannot be independent. The conclusion (though cut off) is that a motive is necessary for the act to occur.

استقلال (istiqlal)
اقتضاء (iqtida')
واجب بالذات (wajib bi al-dhat)
واجب بالغير (wajib bi al-ghayr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that capability alone is sufficient for an act to occur.
Correction: The proof shows that if capability were sufficient, motives would be superfluous, but we observe that motives do influence acts, so capability cannot be sufficient.

Checkpoint

C) The motive would be necessary.
D) The agent would be a necessitator.

Key Takeaway
A motive is necessary for an act because capability alone cannot account for the act's occurrence.

Refuting the Harm-Free Condition on Permissibility
The author refutes the claim that permissibility requires a necessitating factor only when no harm follows.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the condition that permissibility requires a necessitating factor only when no harm follows is untenable, and the implications for the principle of permissibility.

Core Idea
You cannot know that an action leads to no harm whatsoever, so the condition is unworkable.

This lesson presents a key argument against a condition placed on the principle of permissibility. The author shows that the condition would require knowing universal absence of harm, which is impossible without an invalid premise. This refutation is important for understanding how rational judgments about permissibility work.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow of the argument. Then read the English translation to ensure understanding.
2
Identify the condition being refuted
Notice that the author first states the condition: permissibility only requires a necessitating factor if it does not lead to harm or corruption.
This is the view he will argue against.
3
Understand the refutation
The author argues that we can never be certain that an action leads to absolutely no harm. He points out the flaw in assuming that lack of knowledge equals non-existence.
This is the core of the argument.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The author concludes that either permissibility always requires an effector or never does, and the condition is irrelevant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجواز أينما كان، حصول الحاجة أينما كانت وهو المطلوب. وأما إن قلنا: إن الجواز إنما يحوج إلى المقتضى بشرط أن لا يفضي إلى محذور ومفسدة فنقول: هذا باطل لأن أقصى ما في الباب أن نعلم أنه لا يلزم منه المفسدة الفلانية والفلانية. فأما أن نعلم أنه لا يلزم منه شيء من المفاسد أصلاً، فذلك مما لا سبيل إليه، إلا إذا قلنا: إن عدم علمنا بالشيء علمنا بعدم ذلك الشيء، إلا أن هذه المقدمة في غاية الضعف، وإن وقفنا كون الجواز علة للحاجة إلى المرجح على هذه المقدمة لزم بطلان هذا الأصل بالكلية، فعلمنا أن كون الجواز علة للحاجة لا يعتبر فيه هذا القيد أصلًا، بل وجب الحكم عليه: إما بأنه لا يحوج إلى المؤثر البتة، أو بأنه يحوج إلى المؤثر على الإطلاق. والله أعلم.
Permissibility wherever it occurs, the occurrence of need wherever it occurs, and that is what is desired. And if we say: Permissibility only requires a necessitating factor on condition that it does not lead to harm and corruption, then we say: This is false because the most that can be said is that we know that it does not entail such-and-such harm. But as for knowing that it does not entail any harms at all, that is impossible unless we say that our lack of knowledge of something is knowledge of the non-existence of that thing, except that this premise is extremely weak. And if we make the fact that permissibility is a cause for the need for a preponderating factor conditional upon this premise, the total invalidation of this principle follows. So we know that the fact that permissibility is a cause for the need is not considered with this condition at all. Rather, it must be judged either that it does not need an effector at all, or that it needs an effector absolutely. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a condition placed on the principle that permissibility (jawaz) requires a necessitating factor (muqtada). The author first states the condition: that permissibility only requires a necessitating factor if it does not lead to harm or corruption (mafsada). He then declares this condition false. The reason given is that we can only know that a particular action does not lead to specific harms, but we cannot know that it leads to no harms at all. To claim that we can know universal absence of harm would require the premise that our lack of knowledge of something implies its non-existence, which is an extremely weak premise. Therefore, if the principle that permissibility is a cause for the need of a preponderating factor (murajjih) were made dependent on such a condition, the entire principle would be invalidated. Hence, the condition is not considered; rather, permissibility must either not need an effector (mu'aththir) at all, or need one absolutely. The author leaves the matter open, concluding with 'And God knows best.'

Misconception: That the author is denying any condition on permissibility altogether.
Correction: The author does not deny conditions in general; he specifically argues that the proposed condition (requiring knowledge of universal absence of harm) is unworkable and therefore should not be considered.

Checkpoint

A) Because permissibility never leads to harm.
B) Because we cannot know that no harm whatsoever follows from an action.
C) Because the necessitating factor itself prevents harm.
D) Because harm is irrelevant to permissibility.

Key Takeaway
The condition that permissibility requires a necessitating factor only if no harm follows is invalid because we cannot know universal absence of harm.

The Necessity of the Act When a Motive Arises
Sanusi presents the first proof against the view that a motive only makes an act preferable, using the logic of equivalence and contradictories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that a preponderant motive renders the act necessary, not merely preferable.

Core Idea
A preponderant motive makes the opposite side impossible, thus necessitating the act.

This passage tackles a key debate in kalam: does a motive that makes an act preferable also make it obligatory? The author refutes the view that it only creates preferability, using a logical proof based on the states of equivalence and preponderance. Let's follow the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the claim being refuted
The author reports a view that a motive only makes an act 'first' (أولى) but not obligatory. Note the author's firm rejection.
The claim is that preferability does not reach the level of obligation.
2
Understand the logical stages of the proof
The proof uses the concept of equivalence (تساوي) before the motive, then shows that after the motive, the opposite side becomes preponderated (مرجوح), which is even weaker, leading to impossibility of the opposite and thus necessity of the act.
The key is that impossibility of one contradictory entails necessity of the other.
3
Consider the objection and its impact
The objector argues that the preponderance of one side does not necessitate the preponderatedness of the other, because non-existence is a permanent continuum. Notice that the answer is incomplete in the text.
This objection challenges the proof's step that the opposite becomes preponderated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثامن: أن عند الداعي [؟] وبيان أن عند حصول الداعي يجب [الفعل] ولا يبقى الجواز [البنية؟]
اعلم أن من الناس من قال : إن عند حصول الداعي يصير الفعل أولى بالوقوع . لكنه لا يبلغ تلك الأولوية إلى حد الوجوب . واعلم أن هذا القول باطل ويدل عليه وجوه :
الحجة الأولى : إن الفعل والترك قبل حصول الداعي كانا في حد التساوي . وقد بينا أنه ما دام هذا الاستواء يكون باقياً ، فإنه يمتنع حصول الفعل فإذا حصلت الداعية الموجبة لرجحان أحد الطرفين ، فعند هذه الحالة صار الطرف الثاني مرجوحاً . والمرجوح أضعف حالا من المتساوي ، ولما كان عند حصول الاستواء : ممتنع الحصول، مستحيل الوقوع، فعند حصول المرجوحية كان أولى بالامتناع. وإذا صار أحد الطرفين ممتنع الوقوع، صار الطرف الآخر واجب الوقوع . ضرورة أن الخروج من طرف النقيضين محال ، فيثبت بهذا البرهان . أن الفعل يصير واجب الوقوع عند حصول الداعي
فإن قيل : إنه إذا حصلت الداعية المرجحة لخاطر [؟] وجود الفعل ، صار جانب وجوده راجحاً. وهذا لا يقتضي ضرورة جانب العدم مرجوحاً ، لأن ذلك العدم عدم باقي مستمر ( والعلم الباقي المستمر لا يكون التأثير فيه حال بقائه على ذلك العدم المستمر ، فيثبت أن العدم الأصلي نفي ((١) كما كان حال
Chapter Eight: That concerning the motive [?] and the demonstration that when the motive occurs, the act becomes obligatory and permissibility does not remain [?]. Know that among people there is one who says: When the motive occurs, the act becomes preferable to occur, but that preferability does not reach the limit of obligation. And know that this statement is false, and proofs indicate it: The first proof: The act and the abandonment before the occurrence of the motive were in the state of equivalence. And we have shown that as long as this equivalence remains, the occurrence of the act is impossible. So when the motive that necessitates the preponderance of one of the two sides occurs, then in this situation the second side becomes preponderated. And the preponderated is weaker in state than the equal. And since at the occurrence of equivalence the occurrence was impossible, at the occurrence of preponderatedness it is more deserving of impossibility. And when one of the two sides becomes impossible to occur, the other side becomes necessary to occur, of necessity, for exiting from one of the two contradictories is impossible. So by this proof it is established that the act becomes necessary to occur when the motive occurs. If it is said: When the preponderating motive for the side of existence of the act occurs, the side of its existence becomes preponderant, and this does not necessitate that the side of non-existence becomes preponderated, because that non-existence is a permanent continuing non-existence (and the permanent continuing state is not affected by its remaining upon that permanent non-existence), so it is established that the original non-existence is negation ((1) as it was.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a specific claim regarding the effect of a motive (داعي) on human action. The author begins by stating the chapter heading, which asserts that when a motive arises, the act becomes obligatory and permissibility is removed. He then reports a contrary view: some say that a motive only makes an act preferable (أولى) but does not elevate it to the level of obligation (وجوب). The author declares this view false and says there are proofs against it, but here he presents only the first proof (الحجة الأولى).

The proof proceeds logically: Before the motive arises, the act and its abandonment are in a state of equivalence (تساوي), meaning neither is more likely than the other. The author has previously established that in such a state of perfect equilibrium, the act cannot occur—it is impossible (ممتنع). Now, when a motive appears that preponderates (ترجيح) one side (say, the side of action), the other side (abandonment) becomes preponderated (مرجوح), i.e., weaker in likelihood. Since the preponderated is weaker than the equal, and the equal already made the act impossible, then the preponderated side is even more deserving of impossibility. Therefore, the side of action becomes the only possible one, and by the law of contradictories (if one opposite is impossible, the other is necessary), the action becomes obligatory (واجب الوقوع). Thus, the author concludes that the act is necessary at the moment the motive is present.

At the end, an objection (فإن قيل) is introduced: the objector argues that the preponderant motive only gives preponderance to the side of existence, but does not necessarily make the side of non-existence preponderated because non-existence is a permanent continuum and remains unaffected. The objection is cut off, but the author presumably answered it in the missing text.

Key Terms

وجوب
تساوي
رجحان
مرجوحية
امتناع
نقيضان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think a preponderant motive only makes an act preferable, not obligatory.
Correction: The proof shows that a truly preponderant motive renders the opposite side impossible, thereby making the act necessary.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof in this passage, when a preponderant motive arises, what happens to the side of the act?
A) It becomes preferable but not obligatory.
B) It becomes obligatory because the opposite side becomes impossible.
C) It remains in a state of equivalence.
D) It becomes impossible.

Key Takeaway
A truly preponderant motive necessitates the act because it renders the opposite impossible.

?

?

The Third Proof: Preference Requires a Preponderant Factor
Sanusi presents the third proof that preference requires a preponderant factor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalām proof that rational preference cannot occur without a preponderant factor, and how this establishes the necessity of a motive for divine action.

Core Idea
Preference without a cause is rationally impossible.

This lesson presents a decisive proof from kalām that any preference (tarjīḥ) between two equal possibilities requires a preponderant factor. Without it, neither side can occur. The argument is foundational for understanding divine action and human responsibility within Ashʿarī theology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
The proof begins by establishing that when ability relates equally to two opposites, preference for one is impossible. Read the first few lines to grasp this starting point.
This premise is not proven here; it is taken from earlier discussions.
2
Follow the two cases
The argument considers two scenarios: equality (no preference) and when a preponderant motive arises. Notice that in equality, neither cause nor preventative exists; when preference occurs, the efficient cause for the opposite is absent and a preventative is present.
The preventative is the preference itself for the first side.
3
Compare impossibility
Since impossibility occurs in equality (where only absence of cause exists), it must also occur when both absence of cause and presence of a preventative are present. This forces the second side to be impossible and the first to be necessary.
The logic is a fortiori (awlā): if impossible without cause, then even more so with a preventative.
4
Appreciate the conclusion
The proof ends by stating that the second side becomes impossible and the first side necessary, with no middle ground. This is termed a decisive proof (burhān qāṭiʿ).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة: إنا بينا أن عند استواء نسبة القدرة إلى الفعل وإلى الترك، فإنه يمتنع رجحان أحد الجانبين على الآخر. إذا عرفت هذا. فنقول: إن عند استواء أحد الطرفين لم يوجد، لا المقتضي للرجحان، ولا المانع منه. وأما عند حصول الداعية المرجحة لأحد الجانبين، فقد حصل المانع من رجحان الطرف الثاني. لأن رجحان أحد طرفي النقيض كالمانع من رجحان الطرف الثاني. وإذا عرفت هذا فنقول: إن عند حصول الاستواء، لم يوجد المقتضي ولا المانع. ثم كان حصول الرجحان في هذا الوقت ممتنعاً، فعند حصول رجحان أحد الطرفين لم يوجد المقتضي برجحان الطرف الثاني. وحصل المانع منه. لأنا بينا أن رجحان الطرف الأول مانع من رجحان الطرف الثاني. وإذا كان الشيء عند عدم المقتضى ممتنع الوقوع فبأن يصير عند عدم المقتضى وحصول المانع ممتنع الوقوع كان أولى. وإذا صار هذا الجانب ممتنع الوقوع، صار الجانب الثاني واجب الوقوع ضرورة أنه لا واسطة بين الوقوع وبين اللاوقوع. وهذا أيضاً برهان قاطع
The third proof: We have shown that when the relation of ability (al-qudra) to action and to omission is equal, then preference of one side over the other is impossible. If you understand this, we say: When there is equality of two sides, neither the efficient cause (al-muqtaḍī) for preference nor the preventative (al-māniʿ) exists. But when the preponderant motive (al-dāʿiya al-murajjiḥa) for one side occurs, then the preventative against preference for the second side exists. Because preference of one of the two contradictories is like a preventative against preference for the second side. And if you understand this, we say: When equality occurs, neither the efficient cause nor the preventative existed. Then the occurrence of preference at that time was impossible. So when preference for one side occurs, the efficient cause for preference of the second side does not exist, and the preventative from it exists. For we have shown that preference of the first side is a preventative against preference of the second side. And if a thing is impossible of occurrence when the efficient cause is absent, then it is even more so when the efficient cause is absent and the preventative is present. And when this side becomes impossible of occurrence, the second side becomes necessary of occurrence necessarily, since there is no intermediate between occurrence and non-occurrence. And this too is a decisive proof.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a proof (ḥujja) in kalām demonstrating that when a capable agent (qādir) has equal ability towards an action and its omission, a preference (tarjīḥ) for one side cannot occur without a preponderant factor (murajjiḥ). The author, likely al-Āmidī or Ibn al-Ḥājib, begins by stating the premise: when ability is equally related to action and omission, preference of one side is impossible. Then he explains that in a state of equality, neither the efficient cause for preference nor the preventative exists. When a preponderant motive arises for one side, that very preference becomes a preventative against preference for the opposite side. This is because the two sides are contradictories (naqīḍ): the existence of one necessitates the non-existence of the other. Next, he compares the state of equality (where neither efficient cause nor preventative exists) and the state after preference (where the efficient cause for the second side is absent and a preventative is present). Since the first state makes preference impossible, the second state, which adds a preventative, makes the second side even more impossible. Consequently, the first side becomes necessary (wājib) of occurrence, as there is no middle ground between occurrence and non-occurrence. The proof is termed 'decisive' (qāṭiʿ) and is foundational for the doctrine that the capable agent does not act without a preponderant motive.

Key Terms
قدرة
رجحان
داعية مرجحة
مقتضي
مانع
نقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof argues that the agent's ability is inherently limited or that the agent is coerced.
Correction: The proof does not claim the agent lacks ability; rather, it establishes that rational preference without a cause is impossible. It affirms that choice requires a motive, not that the agent is powerless.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, when a preponderant motive arises for one side, what happens to the second side?
A) It becomes equally possible.
B) It becomes impossible.
C) It remains merely possible.
D) The agent's ability is negated.

Key Takeaway
Preference without a preponderant factor is rationally impossible; when one side is preferred, the opposite becomes impossible and the preferred side necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever acted without a reason, yet believed you were free? This proof shows that preference without a cause is impossible. Reflect on a time you thought you chose arbitrarily. Was there truly no motive at all? Now consider your ultimate choice between obedience and disobedience. What preponderant motives direct your heart? Are they aligned with what you know to be true?
?
If preference requires a preponderant factor, then what is the deepest motive that has turned your heart away from following the truth? Identify it honestly—not as an excuse, but as the actual cause of your neglect. What would it take for the truth to become the preponderant side for you?
?
This proof is called 'decisive.' Do you live as if it is? That is, do you examine the motives behind your actions, or do you drift with whim? Commit today to pause before every significant choice to ask: 'What is the real cause of my preference?'

The Division of Motives: Need and Beneficence
Classification of motives into need and beneficence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of divine motives into the motive of need and the motive of beneficence, and how they are rooted in pleasure and delight.

Core Idea
Motive of need seeks benefit for self; motive of beneficence seeks benefit for others.

The author begins by introducing a crucial classification within Mu'tazilite theology: the division of motives (al-dawā'ī) into two types. This distinction forms the basis for many discussions in the divine sciences. In this lesson, we will explore what each motive means and how they are defined.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification passage
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Notice the two types of motives introduced.
The author aligns this division with Mu'tazilite principles.
2
Identify the two motives
Find the definitions of 'motive of need' and 'motive of beneficence'. Note how they differ based on the recipient of the benefit.
Motive of need involves conveying benefit to oneself; beneficence to another.
3
Understand the foundation in pleasure
The author reduces wisdom, benefit, and good to pleasure and delight. This is the underlying motivation.
Pleasure and delight are the ultimate good.
4
Note the attributes considered
Realize that the motive of need considers the agent's attribute (need), while the motive of beneficence considers the act's attribute (goodness or evil).
This distinction is key for later rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل العاشر
في
تحقيق الكلام في تقسيم الدواعي إلى ما يكون داعية الحاجة، وإلى ما يكون داعية الإحسان
( الداعية : إما تكون داعية الحاجة. وإما أن تكون داعية الإحسان )(۱) . واعلم أن هذا التقسيم هو اللائق بأصول المعتزلة . وعليه فرعوا كثيراً من مباحثهم في علوم الإلهيات . ونقول : قد بينا أنه لا معنى للحكمة والمصلحة والخير إلا اللذة والسرور ، أو ما يكون مؤدياً (٢) إليهما ، أو إلى أحدهما . ونقول : العلم بكون الفعل منفعة إما أن يدعوه إلى إيصال تلك المنفعة إلى نفسه أو إلى غيره ، والأول : هو داعية الحاجة. والثاني هو داعية الإحسان ، فههنا الداعي للفاعل إلى فعله مجرد كونه في نفسه حسناً، ويكون الداعي له إلى تركه مجرد كونه قبيحاً . فداعي الحاجة : اعتبار صفة الفاعل ، وهي كونه محتاجاً إلى ذلك الشيء . وأما داعية الحكمة : فهي اعتبار صفة الفعل لا اعتبار صفة الفاعل . وأعني باعتبار صفة الفعل ، كونه في نفسه حسناً ، أو كونه قبيحاً .
ثم قالوا : وحكم هذين القسمين مختلف لأن قبحه يدعوه إلى الترك على سبيل الجزم . ولا يجوز خلافه . وأما حسنه فيدعو إلى الفعل من غير وجوب ثم إن انضاف إلى حسنه وجه يقتضي أن يكون فعله أولى من تركه صار هذا الداعي أقوى لكنه لا يبلغ مبلغ الوجوب . فإن انضاف إليه ما يقتضي الوجوب
The Tenth Chapter: On the Investigation of the Discourse Concerning the Division of Motives into What Is a Motive of Need and What Is a Motive of Beneficence. (The motive: either it is a motive of need, or it is a motive of beneficence.) (1) Know that this division is suitable for the principles of the Mu'tazilites. And upon it they based many of their investigations in the divine sciences. And we say: we have explained that there is no meaning for wisdom, benefit, and good except pleasure and delight, or that which leads (2) to them, or to one of them. And we say: the knowledge that an act is a benefit either calls him to convey that benefit to himself or to another. The first is the motive of need; the second is the motive of beneficence. So here, the motive for the agent to do the act is merely its being good in itself, and the motive for him to abandon it is merely its being evil. So the motive of need is the consideration of the attribute of the agent, namely his being in need of that thing. As for the motive of wisdom, it is the consideration of the attribute of the act, not the consideration of the attribute of the agent. And by consideration of the attribute of the act I mean its being good in itself or its being evil. Then they said: the ruling of these two divisions is different, because its evil calls him to avoidance decisively, and it is not permissible to contravene it. As for its goodness, it calls him to act without obligation. Then if there is added to its goodness a consideration that requires that doing it is preferable to abandoning it, this motive becomes stronger, but it does not reach the level of obligation. Then if there is added to it what requires obligation...
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a classification of motives (al-dawā'ī) into two types: the motive of need (dā'iyat al-ḥājah) and the motive of beneficence (dā'iyat al-iḥsān). The author begins by stating the division, noting that it aligns with Mu'tazilite principles and that they built many theological discussions upon it. Next, the author clarifies his own position: wisdom (ḥikmah), benefit (maṣlaḥah), and good (khayr) reduce to pleasure and delight (al-ladhah wal-surūr) or what leads to them. Then he explains the mechanism: when one knows an act is beneficial, it motivates either delivering that benefit to oneself (motive of need) or to another (motive of beneficence). The author then notes that the mere goodness of an act itself can serve as a motive for the agent to perform it, and its evil as a motive to abandon it. The motive of need considers the agent's attribute of being in need, whereas the motive of wisdom (or beneficence) considers the attribute of the act itself—whether it is good or evil. Finally, the author presents the ruling of these two divisions: evil compels avoidance decisively and cannot be opposed; goodness invites action but not obligation. If additional factors make the act preferable, the motive strengthens but still does not create obligation; only if what necessitates obligation is added does it become obligatory. The passage ends mid-sentence at the bottom of the page.

صفة الفعل (ṣifat al-fiʿl)
اللذة والسرور (al-ladhah wal-surūr)

A) The attribute of the act
B) The attribute of the agent
C) The obligation to perform the act
D) The universal benefit

Key Takeaway
Motives are divided by whether the benefit is sought for oneself (need) or for another (beneficence), and the motive of need focuses on the agent's need while beneficence focuses on the act's inherent goodness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If you were to remove all thought of personal benefit—pleasure, reward, or even self-image—would your actions change? Which acts would vanish from your life, and which would remain because they are good in themselves? What does that reveal about your heart?

Rulings of Motives: Evil Compels, Good Invites
Rulings of evil and good motives regarding obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the moral quality of an act determines the strength of the motive: evil compels avoidance decisively, while goodness invites action without obligation, and how obligation can arise.

Core Idea
Evil compels avoidance; goodness invites but does not obligate.

After classifying motives, the author turns to their rulings. The key is that good and evil have different motivational forces. Evil compels avoidance, whereas goodness only invites action, and only with additional factors does it become obligatory. This lesson unpacks that logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the rulings passage
Read the second part of the passage carefully. Notice the contrast between evil and goodness.
2
Identify the ruling for evil
Find the phrase stating that evil calls to avoidance decisively and cannot be opposed.
This is a strong, binding motive.
3
Identify the ruling for goodness
Find that goodness calls to action without obligation (wujūb).
It invites but does not necessitate.
4
Understand the strengthening process
See that additional factors can make the motive stronger, but it still does not reach obligation unless further conditions are added.
The passage ends mid-sentence indicating a condition for obligation.

الفصل العاشر
في
تحقيق الكلام في تقسيم الدواعي إلى ما يكون داعية الحاجة، وإلى ما يكون داعية الإحسان
( الداعية : إما تكون داعية الحاجة. وإما أن تكون داعية الإحسان )(۱) . واعلم أن هذا التقسيم هو اللائق بأصول المعتزلة . وعليه فرعوا كثيراً من مباحثهم في علوم الإلهيات . ونقول : قد بينا أنه لا معنى للحكمة والمصلحة والخير إلا اللذة والسرور ، أو ما يكون مؤدياً (٢) إليهما ، أو إلى أحدهما . ونقول : العلم بكون الفعل منفعة إما أن يدعوه إلى إيصال تلك المنفعة إلى نفسه أو إلى غيره ، والأول : هو داعية الحاجة. والثاني هو داعية الإحسان ، فههنا الداعي للفاعل إلى فعله مجرد كونه في نفسه حسناً، ويكون الداعي له إلى تركه مجرد كونه قبيحاً . فداعي الحاجة : اعتبار صفة الفاعل ، وهي كونه محتاجاً إلى ذلك الشيء . وأما داعية الحكمة : فهي اعتبار صفة الفعل لا اعتبار صفة الفاعل . وأعني باعتبار صفة الفعل ، كونه في نفسه حسناً ، أو كونه قبيحاً .

The Tenth Chapter: On the Investigation of the Discourse Concerning the Division of Motives into What Is a Motive of Need and What Is a Motive of Beneficence. (The motive: either it is a motive of need, or it is a motive of beneficence.) (1) Know that this division is suitable for the principles of the Mu'tazilites. And upon it they based many of their investigations in the divine sciences. And we say: we have explained that there is no meaning for wisdom, benefit, and good except pleasure and delight, or that which leads (2) to them, or to one of them. And we say: the knowledge that an act is a benefit either calls him to convey that benefit to himself or to another. The first is the motive of need; the second is the motive of beneficence. So here, the motive for the agent to do the act is merely its being good in itself, and the motive for him to abandon it is merely its being evil. So the motive of need is the consideration of the attribute of the agent, namely his being in need of that thing. As for the motive of wisdom, it is the consideration of the attribute of the act, not the consideration of the attribute of the agent. And by consideration of the attribute of the act I mean its being good in itself or its being evil. Then they said: the ruling of these two divisions is different, because its evil calls him to avoidance decisively, and it is not permissible to contravene it. As for its goodness, it calls him to act without obligation. Then if there is added to its goodness a consideration that requires that doing it is preferable to abandoning it, this motive becomes stronger, but it does not reach the level of obligation. Then if there is added to it what requires obligation...

Commentary
This passage is a classification of motives (al-dawā'ī) into two types: the motive of need (dā'iyat al-ḥājah) and the motive of beneficence (dā'iyat al-iḥsān). The author begins by stating the division, noting that it aligns with Mu'tazilite principles and that they built many theological discussions upon it. Next, the author clarifies his own position: wisdom (ḥikmah), benefit (maṣlaḥah), and good (khayr) reduce to pleasure and delight (al-ladhah wal-surūr) or what leads to them. Then he explains the mechanism: when one knows an act is beneficial, it motivates either delivering that benefit to oneself (motive of need) or to another (motive of beneficence). The author then notes that the mere goodness of an act itself can serve as a motive for the agent to perform it, and its evil as a motive to abandon it. The motive of need considers the agent's attribute of being in need, whereas the motive of wisdom (or beneficence) considers the attribute of the act itself—whether it is good or evil. Finally, the author presents the ruling of these two divisions: evil compels avoidance decisively and cannot be opposed; goodness invites action but not obligation. If additional factors make the act preferable, the motive strengthens but still does not create obligation; only if what necessitates obligation is added does it become obligatory. The passage ends mid-sentence at the bottom of the page.

The Mu'tazilite Division of Motives: Need and Beneficence
Defines the Mu'tazilite division of motives into need and beneficence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite distinction between the motive of need (dā'iyat al-hājah) and the motive of beneficence (dā'iyat al-iḥsān), and how each considers a different attribute (agent vs. act).

Core Idea
Motives are either driven by personal need or by the inherent goodness of the act.

This passage introduces a key Mu'tazilite classification of the motives that lead an agent to act. The division into a motive of need, which looks to the agent's own requirement, and a motive of beneficence, which looks to the act's inherent value, underpins many of their theological discussions. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping later arguments about God's actions and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation, paying attention to the two types of motives and how the author defines them.
2
Identify the two motives
Find where the motive of need and motive of beneficence are defined. Notice the phrases 'داعية الحاجة' and 'داعية الإحسان'.
3
Understand the basis in pleasure
The author first recaps that wisdom and benefit reduce to pleasure. See how this foundation leads to the distinction.
4
Grasp the key difference
The motive of need considers the agent's attribute (need), while the motive of beneficence considers the act's attribute (good/evil). This is the core conceptual contrast.
5
Connect to broader theology
Reflect on why this division matters for discussions about God's actions. The motive of beneficence becomes important for arguments about God's wisdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الداعية : إما تكون داعية الحاجة. وإما أن تكون داعية الإحسان . واعلم . أن هذا التقسيم هو اللائق بأصول المعتزلة . وعليه فرعوا كثيراً من مباحثهم في علوم الإلهيات . ونقول : قد بينا أنه لا معنى للحكمة والمصلحة والخير إلا اللذة والسرور ، أو ما يكون مؤدياً إليهما ، أو إلى أحدهما . ونقول : العلم بكون الفعل منفعة إما أن يدعوه إلى إيصال تلك المنفعة إلى نفسه أو إلى غيره ، والأول : هو داعية الحاجة. والثاني هو داعية الإحسان ، فههنا الداعي للفاعل إلى فعله مجرد كونه في نفسه حسناً ، ويكون الداعي له إلى تركه مجرد كونه قبيحاً . فداعي الحاجة : اعتبار صفة الفاعل ، وهي كونه محتاجاً إلى ذلك الشيء . وأما داعية الحكمة : فهي اعتبار صفة الفعل لا اعتبار صفة الفاعل . وأعني باعتبار صفة الفعل ، كونه في نفسه حسناً ، أو كونه قبيحاً .
The motive: either it is the motive of need, or it is the motive of beneficence. Know that this division is appropriate to the principles of the Mu'tazila, and upon it they branched many of their investigations in the divine sciences. We say: we have shown that there is no meaning to wisdom, benefit, and good except pleasure and delight, or what leads to them or to one of them. We say: knowledge that an act is a benefit either calls him to convey that benefit to himself or to another. The first is the motive of need, and the second is the motive of beneficence. So here the motive for the agent to perform an act is merely its being good in itself, and the motive for him to leave it is merely its being evil in itself. The motive of need is consideration of the attribute of the agent, that is, his being in need of that thing. As for the motive of wisdom, it is consideration of the attribute of the act, not consideration of the attribute of the agent. By consideration of the attribute of the act I mean its being good in itself or evil in itself.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage defines the Mu'tazilite division of motives into two types: the motive of need and the motive of beneficence. The author begins by stating that this division is consistent with Mu'tazilite principles, and that they have built many of their discussions in theology upon it. He then explains that he has already established that wisdom, benefit, and good are ultimately reducible to pleasure and delight, or to what leads to them. Based on this, knowledge that an act is beneficial moves the agent either to bring that benefit to himself (motive of need) or to another (motive of beneficence). The motive for action is merely the act's being good in itself, and the motive for omission is its being evil in itself. The key distinction is that the motive of need considers the agent's attribute of being in need, while the motive of wisdom (beneficence) considers the attribute of the act itself—its inherent goodness or evil—without regard to the agent's state.

Mu'tazilite Classification of Motives
Mu'tazilite ruling on two motives and their strength levels

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila distinguish between the motives of evil and good, and how the strength of the good motive increases from mere permission to obligation.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila teach that only evil decisively commands omission; good acts vary in strength.

The Mu'tazilite theologians developed a detailed classification of moral motives. They distinguished between the motive arising from the evil of an act and the motive arising from its goodness. This passage lays out their ruling on how these motives differ in strength, from mere permission to absolute obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own words introduce the classification.
Notice that the passage begins with 'Then they said' — it continues an earlier discussion.
2
Identify the two types of motives
Look for the two categories: motive from evil (qubh) and motive from good (husn). Understand their basic rulings.
The ruling for evil is decisive omission; for good, it is action without obligation.
3
Trace the strengthening of the good motive
See how an added consideration (wajh) can make the good motive stronger but still not obligatory. Then see what happens when a factor requiring obligation is added.
The passage describes three levels: mere goodness, preferable goodness, and obligatory goodness.
4
Reflect on the Mu'tazilite stance
Notice that for the Mu'tazila, only evil compels omission rationally. Good acts require extra conditions to become obligatory.
This contrasts with other schools that may hold different views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قالوا : وحكم هذين القسمين مختلف لأن قبحه يدعوه إلى الترك على سبيل الجزم . ولا يجوز خلافه . وأما حسنه فيدعو إلى الفعل من غير وجوب ثم إن انضاف إلى حسنه وجه يقتضي أن يكون فعله أولى من تركه صار هذا الداعي أقوى لكنه لا يبلغ مبلغ الوجوب . فإن انضاف إليه ما يقتضي الوجوب صار في غاية القوة ، ( لأنه صار بحيث لا يجوز في العقل تركه ، فهذا تفصيل هذا التقسيم على أصول المعتزلة ) .
Then they said: And the ruling of these two divisions is different, because its evil calls him to omission definitively, and its opposite is not permissible. As for its goodness, it calls to the act without obligation. Then if to its goodness is added a consideration that requires that its performance be preferable to its omission, this motive becomes stronger, but it does not reach the level of obligation. Then if what requires obligation is added to it, it becomes at the utmost strength (because it becomes such that it is not rationally permissible to omit it). This is the detailed explanation of this division according to the principles of the Mu'tazila.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage clarifies the Mu'tazilite ruling on the two types of motives. The author explains that the rulings differ: the evil of an act decisively motivates its omission, and it is not permissible to act against that. In contrast, the goodness of an act motivates performance without obligating it. If further reason makes the act preferable to omission, the motive strengthens but still does not reach obligation. Only when an additional factor necessitates obligation does the motive become absolutely compelling, such that reason does not permit its omission. This concludes the detailed presentation of the Mu'tazilite position on the division of motives.

قبح
حسن
وجوب
أولى
غاية القوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A good act always obligates action regardless of other factors.
Correction: The Mu'tazila hold that a good act only becomes obligatory when an additional factor necessitating obligation is present; otherwise, it is merely recommended or permissible.

Key Takeaway
According to the Mu'tazila, only evil motives compel omission; good motives obligate only when augmented by a necessitating factor.

Philosophers' Denial of the Motive of Beneficence: The First Maqam
Philosophers present the first maqam of their argument: reducing good/evil to benefit/harm and denying it for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' first argument that good and evil are reducible to benefit and harm, and why this implies that attributing moral value to God's actions is impossible.

Core Idea
Good and evil are nothing but causing benefit or harm, and God is beyond both.

The philosophers and theologians who deny rational good and evil present their case in two stages. In this lesson, we examine the first stage, where they argue that 'good' and 'evil' have no meaning independent of benefit and harm. Since God is necessary of existence and cannot be affected by benefit or harm, it follows that His actions cannot be described as good or evil in the usual sense. This argument undermines the very basis for a motive of beneficence in God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage slowly, then its literal translation. Note the structure: the philosophers state two positions, then begin the first.
2
Identify the two positions (maqamain)
The passage mentions two positions. Focus on the first: 'al-bahth 'an kawn al-shay' hasanan fi nafsihi wa qabihan fi nafsihi'. Understand that the first position is about defining good/evil in themselves.
The second position is not elaborated here.
3
Analyze the definition of good and evil
According to the argument, good is what leads to preponderant benefit, evil what leads to preponderant harm. There is no additional meaning – goodness is identical to causing benefit.
This reduces objective good/evil to consequentialist terms.
4
Apply the definition to God
Since benefit and harm are impossible for the Necessary Existent, considering good/evil in His actions becomes impossible. Ask yourself: does this argument succeed in denying a motive of beneficence?
Consider how an opponent might respond.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الفلاسفة والمتكلمون . الذين ينكرون ( القول بتحسين العقل وتقبيحه ، فقد أطبقوا على إنكار داعية الإحسان ، وقالوا : لنا في ) إنكار هذا الكلام مقامان . المقام الأول : البحث عن كون الشيء حسناً في نفسه ، وقبيحاً في نفسه ، والمقام الثاني : أنه بعد تلخيص معنى الحسن والقبح . هل يمكن أن يقال : إن العلم به يصلح أن يكون داعياً إلى الفعل ؟ أما المقام الأول . فتقريره : إنا بينا أن المنفعة مطلوبة بالذات ، وإن المضرة مكروهة بالذات، فكل ما أفضى إلى حصول المنفعة الراجحة كان حسناً ، ولا معنى لحسنه إلا كونه كذلك ، وكل ما أفضى إلى حصول المضرة الراجحة كان قبيحاً ولا معنى لقبحه إلا ذلك . وإذا ثبت أنه لا معنى للحسن والقبح إلا كونه منشئاً للمصالح والمفاسد ، فحينئذ لم يكن اعتبار الحسن والقبح أمراً مغايراً لرعاية المصالح والمفاسد ، بل كان هذا عين هذا القسم . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : اعتبار أحوال المصالح والمفاسد إنما يصح في حق من يجوز عليه المنفعة والمضرة ، ولما ثبت أن إله العالم واجب الوجود لذاته ، في ذاته وفي صفاته ، كان النفع والضرر محالا في حقه ، فوجب أن يكون اعتبار معنى الحسن والقبح في أفعاله محالاً
As for the philosophers and theologians who deny the doctrine of rational good and evil, they have agreed upon denying the motive of beneficence, and said: We have two positions in denying this statement. The first position: the investigation of whether a thing is good in itself and evil in itself. The second position: after summarizing the meaning of good and evil, can it be said that knowledge of it suffices to be a motive for action? As for the first position, its explanation: We have shown that benefit is desired in itself, and harm is hated in itself. So everything that leads to achieving preponderant benefit is good, and its goodness has no meaning except its being so. And everything that leads to achieving preponderant harm is evil, and its evil has no meaning except that. And when it is established that there is no meaning for good and evil except their being causes of benefits and harms, then the consideration of good and evil is not a thing distinct from the consideration of benefits and harms; rather, this is identical to that consideration. And when this is established, we say: the consideration of states of benefits and harms is only valid for one who is subject to benefit and harm. And since it is established that the Lord of the world is necessary of existence in Himself, in His essence and attributes, benefit and harm are impossible with respect to Him, so it must be that the consideration of the meaning of good and evil in His actions is impossible.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage presents the rejection of the motive of beneficence by philosophers and theologians who deny rational good and evil. They have two stations of argument. The first station investigates whether a thing can be good or evil in itself. Their explanation: benefit is intrinsically desired, harm intrinsically disliked. Whatever leads to preponderant benefit is good, and its goodness is nothing other than that causation; similarly for evil. Thus, good and evil are identical to causing benefit or harm. The consideration of good and evil therefore is not distinct from the consideration of benefit and harm. But such consideration is only valid for an entity susceptible to benefit and harm. Since God is necessary of existence and beyond benefit and harm, it is impossible to ascribe good and evil to His actions. This effectively denies the basis for a motive of beneficence grounded in intrinsic good.

Key Terms
تحسين العقل وتقبيحه
داعية الإحسان
منفعة
مضرة
حسن
قبح
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers deny all moral values, including for humans.
Correction: Actually, they argue that good and evil are defined by benefit and harm, which applies to beings subject to benefit/harm. For humans, actions can still be judged good/evil based on consequences; for God, it is impossible because He is beyond benefit/harm.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' first argument, why is it impossible to ascribe good and evil to God's actions?
A) Because God's actions are always good.
B) Because good and evil are based on benefit and harm, and God is beyond benefit and harm.
C) Because the intellect cannot judge good and evil.
D) Because good and evil are subjective.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that good and evil are nothing but causing benefit or harm, and since God is beyond benefit and harm, His actions cannot be morally evaluated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own acts of worship, do you secretly define 'good' by the benefit you receive (peace of mind, social approval) or by something beyond benefit? Are you willing to worship a God who does not fit your framework of 'good' and 'evil'?
?
If God's actions are beyond moral evaluation based on benefit and harm, how does that affect the way you interpret events in your life that you consider 'bad' (loss, illness, hardship)? Can you still trust in His wisdom without imposing your own categories of good and evil?
?
The philosophers argue that considering good and evil in God's actions is impossible. Yet you often supplicate to God asking for benefit and protection from harm. Does this contradict your belief that God is beyond benefit and harm? How will you reconcile your prayers with this philosophical stance?

Mu'tazilite Proof: Good/Evil Are Not Benefit/Harm
Mu'tazila present a proof for the distinctness of good/evil from benefit/harm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that good and evil are distinct from benefit and harm based on the possibility of something being good yet harmful or evil yet beneficial.

Core Idea
If good could harm, then good is not the same as benefit.

The Mu'tazila challenge a purely consequentialist view of ethics by arguing that good and evil are not reducible to benefit and harm. Their proof is simple: we can conceive of things that are evil but beneficial, and things that are good but harmful. If good and evil were identical to benefit and harm, such cases would be impossible. This passage presents their core argument, setting the stage for a deeper discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice the key terms: حسن (good), قبح (evil), مصلحة (benefit), مفسدة (harm).
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the proof premise
The Mu'tazila state that a thing may be evil yet beneficial, or good yet harmful. Reflect on examples from everyday life or revelation that support this.
This is the core of their argument.
3
Understand the conclusion
They conclude that this possibility necessitates that the consideration of good/evil is distinct from the consideration of benefit/harm. Why does that follow?
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction matters for Islamic theology. It defends the objective reality of moral values independent of worldly outcomes.
This argument is a counter to the philosophers who reduce ethics to consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت المعتزلة : الدليل على أن اعتبار الحسن والقبح مغاير لاعتبار كونه مصلحة ومفسدة : أن الشيء قد يكون قبيحاً مع كونه نافعاً وقد يكون حسناً مع كونه ضاراً وذلك يوجب المغايرة . وبيانه من وجوه .
The Mu'tazila said: The proof that the consideration of good and evil is distinct from the consideration of its being a benefit or harm is that a thing may be evil while being beneficial, and may be good while being harmful, and that necessitates distinctness. Its clarification is from several aspects.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite rebuttal to the philosophers' reduction of good and evil to benefit and harm. The Mu'tazila assert that the consideration of good and evil is distinct from the consideration of benefit and harm. Their proof: a thing can be evil yet beneficial, or good yet harmful. If good and evil were identical to benefit and harm, such a situation would be impossible. Therefore, they must be distinct. The author notes that this argument will be clarified from several aspects (presumably in the following pages). This is a direct counter to the first maqām of the philosophers.

Key Terms
حسن
قبح
مصلحة
مفسدة
مغايرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila deny that benefit and harm are relevant to moral judgment.
Correction: The Mu'tazila do not deny relevance; they affirm that good/evil are additional properties beyond benefit/harm, not that benefit/harm are irrelevant.

Checkpoint

C) God's actions are always good.
D) Benefit and harm are not real.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of good being harmful and evil being beneficial shows that good and evil are not simply benefit and harm.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What commitment can you make today to align your actions with genuine goodness, even at the cost of personal harm?

Mu'tazila's Four Proofs for Rational Good and Evil
Mu'tazila present four proofs that good and evil are distinct from benefit and harm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's four proofs that rational judgments of good and evil are independent of considerations of benefit and harm.

Core Idea
Good and evil are known by reason alone, not by consequences.

The Mu'tazila argue that reason can judge actions as good or evil without reference to benefit or harm. They present four concrete examples to demonstrate this distinction. In this passage, you will see how each proof isolates the rational judgment from any utilitarian consideration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Read the first sentence where the Mu'tazila state their conclusion: that good/evil are distinct from benefit/harm. This is the thesis they will prove.
Notice the phrase 'يوجب المغايرة' (necessitates distinction).
2
Examine the first proof: injustice
The first proof uses the example of injustice. Although it benefits the unjust person, reason clearly judges it as evil. This shows that the judgment of evil does not depend on harm.
3
Examine the second proof: blasphemous poem
The second proof considers a beautifully composed poem that insults prophets. Despite its aesthetic pleasure, reason judges it evil due to its content.
4
Examine the third proof: beneficial lies
The third proof highlights that beneficial lies, though useful, are still judged evil by reason. Benefit does not change the moral verdict.
5
Examine the fourth proof: selfless help
The final proof describes helping a blind man with no observers and no possibility of reward, even for a materialist. Reason judges this good despite personal loss and no benefit.
This proof eliminates all possible external motives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت المعتزلة : الدليل على أن اعتبار الحسن والقبح مغاير لاعتبار كونه مصلحة ومفسدة : أن الشيء قد يكون قبيحا مع كونه نافعا وقد يكون حسنا مع كونه ضارا وذلك يوجب المغايرة . وبيانه من وجوه . الأول : إن الظلم نافع للظالم ، مع أن الظالم يشهد صريح عقله يكون الظلم قبيحا . والثاني : إنا إذا فرضنا إنسانا أنشأ قصيدة غراء بألفاظ فصيحة ، وكتبها بخط حسن . وقرأها بأصوات طيبة ، وكانت تلك القصيدة مشتملة على شتم الملائكة والأنبياء والصالحين . كان سماع تلك الألفاظ الفصيحة والتركيبات الكاملة بتلك الأصوات الطيبة لذيذ ، مع أن صريح العقل يحكم فيها بالقبح . والثالث : إن الكذب النافع منتفع به ، مع صريح العقل ينادي عليه بالقبح . والرابع : إن من رأى إنسانا مريضا ، أعمى مشرفا على الموت في صحراء ليس فيها أحد ، فإن عقله يدعوه إلى الإحسان إلى ذلك المريض الأعمى فههنا صريح العقل حكم بحسن ذلك الإحسان ، وذلك الإحسان يوجب تنقيص المال وتحمل المشقة في النفس . فههنا صريح عقله يدعوه إلى فعل ذلك الإحسان . وليس له فيه البتة شيء من وجوه النفع. وذلك لأن دفع المال إليه تنقيص للمال ، هو ضرر ، وذلك المريض الأعمى لا يعرفه فلا يطمع هذا المعطي في أن يذكره بالثناء والحمد . ولم يحضر في تلك الصحراء أحد ، حتى يقال : إنه إنما أحن إليه لأجل أن الحاضرين يمدحونه . وأيضا : فربما كان هذا المعطى دهريا ينكر الإله والمعاد ، فلا يمكن أن يقال : إنه إنما أقدم على ذلك الإحسان لأجل الرغبة في الثواب .
The Mu'tazila said: The proof that the consideration of good and evil is distinct from the consideration of its being a benefit or harm is that a thing may be evil while being beneficial, and may be good while being harmful, and that necessitates distinction. Its explanation is from aspects. First: Injustice is beneficial to the unjust person, while the unjust person's clear reason testifies that injustice is evil. Second: If we suppose a person who composed a splendid poem with eloquent words, wrote it with beautiful handwriting, and recited it with pleasant voices, and that poem contained insulting the angels, prophets, and righteous people. Hearing those eloquent words and perfect constructions with those pleasant voices would be delightful, while clear reason judges them to be evil. Third: Beneficial lying is beneficial, while clear reason pronounces it to be evil. Fourth: If someone sees a sick, blind person near death in a desert with no one else, his reason calls him to do good to that blind sick person. Here clear reason judges that good deed to be good, and that good deed necessitates loss of wealth and enduring hardship in oneself. Here his clear reason calls him to do that good deed, and he has absolutely no benefit in it. That is because giving money to him is a loss of wealth, which is harm, and that blind sick person does not know him, so this giver does not hope that he will mention him with praise and thanks. No one is present in that desert, so it could be said that he did it so that those present would praise him. Also, perhaps this giver is a materialist who denies God and the Resurrection, so it cannot be said that he undertook that good deed out of desire for reward.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila's objection against the philosophers and mutakallimun who deny that reason alone can judge actions as good or evil (taḥsīn and taqbīḥ). The Mu'tazila argue that the notion of good and evil is distinct from the notion of benefit and harm. They provide four proofs to demonstrate this distinction. First: Injustice is beneficial to the oppressor, yet reason clearly judges it evil. Second: A beautiful but blasphemous poem may be aesthetically pleasing, but reason judges it evil due to its content. Third: A beneficial lie provides utility, yet reason judges it evil. Fourth: The case of helping a blind, dying man in a desert with no observers and no possibility of reward or recognition—reason judges this act good even though it involves personal loss and no conceivable benefit, especially if the helper is a materialist who denies God and the afterlife. Thus, the judgment of good and evil is independent of benefit and harm. The author is presenting the Mu'tazila's argument; the response of the mutakallimun will follow.

Key Terms
الحسن
القبح
التحسين والتقبيح العقليين
المعتزلة
صريح العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila deny that benefit and harm have any role in moral evaluation.
Correction: The Mu'tazila do not deny that benefit and harm are relevant; they only argue that reason can know good and evil independently. Benefit and harm may still coincide with good and evil, but the judgment is not based on them.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the four proofs presented by the Mu'tazila in this passage?
A) Injustice is beneficial but judged evil by reason.
B) A beautiful poem insulting prophets is judged evil by reason despite its beauty.
C) Lying is always harmful and therefore evil.
D) Helping a blind dying man in a desert with no observers is judged good despite personal loss.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila demonstrate through four concrete examples that rational judgments of good and evil operate independently of considerations of benefit and harm.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The fourth proof describes a person who does good with absolutely no hope of return – not even from God. When was the last time you did something good solely because it was right, without any expectation of reward, recognition, or even self-satisfaction? If you cannot think of such a moment, what does that reveal about the state of your conscience?
?
In the second proof, the beauty of the poem does not change its evil content. Think of something in your life that feels pleasurable or beneficial but which you know, in your 'clear reason', is wrong. What do you do with that tension? Do you suppress the rational judgment or act on it?
?
The Mu'tazila argue that injustice is evil even when it benefits you. Reflect on a time you justified a small injustice because it worked to your advantage. Did your conscience protest? How did you silence it? And what would it mean for you to start trusting that inner rational voice over the pull of benefit?

The Fourth Argument: Reason Judges Independently of Benefit
Presents the proponents' fourth argument using a desert charity scenario.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fourth argument uses a scenario devoid of any possible benefit to argue that reason's judgment of good and bad is independent of seeking benefits or avoiding harms.

Core Idea
Moral judgment persists even when all possible benefits are absent.

This lesson presents the proponents' fourth argument, which uses a carefully crafted scenario to show that reason can judge an act as good even when there is no personal gain, no social recognition, and no religious expectation. The argument aims to demonstrate that rational moral judgment is not reducible to calculations of benefit and harm.

Guided Path
1
Read the scenario
Read the Arabic passage and translation describing the situation of a sick blind man in a desert.
Notice the three conditions that remove all possible motives.
2
Identify the three eliminative conditions
The argument systematically removes personal benefit, social approval, and religious reward. Find each condition in the text.
3
Understand the conclusion
The argument concludes that reason's judgment of goodness is independent of these factors. See how this supports their overall claim about rational judgments.
This is a key claim in the debate on rational good and bad.
4
Consider the context
Note that this is only the argument, not the refutation. Think about how one might object to this scenario.
The refutation is not included here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرابع: إن من رأى إنساناً مريضاً، أعمى مشرفاً على الموت في صحراء ليس فيها أحد، فإن عقله يدعوه إلى الإحسان إلى ذلك المريض الأعمى فههنا صريح عقله يدعوه إلى فعل ذلك الإحسان. وليس له فيه البتة شيء من وجوه النفع. وذلك لأن دفع المال إليه تنقيص للمال، هو ضرر، وذلك المريض الأعمى لا يعرفه فلا يطمع هذا المعطي في أن يذكره بالثناء والحمد. ولم يحضر في تلك الصحراء أحد، حتى يقال: إنه إنما أحن إليه لأجل أن الحاضرين يمدحونه. وأيضاً: فربما كان هذا المعطى دهرياً ينكر الإله والمعاد، فلا يمكن أن يقال: إنه إنما أقدم على ذلك الإحسان لأجل الرغبة في الثواب. فههنا العقل حكم بحسن ذلك الإحسان، مع أنه خالي عن جميع جهات النفع، فظهر بهذه الوجوه أن حكم العقل بالتحسين والتقبيح مغاير لحكمة طلب المنافع والمضار.
And the fourth: If a person sees a sick, blind man on the verge of death in a desert with no one else, his reason calls him to do good to that sick blind person. Here his clear reason calls him to perform that good deed. And there is absolutely no aspect of benefit for him in it. That is because giving money to him is a decrease of wealth, which is a harm, and that sick blind person does not know him, so this giver does not hope that he will mention him with praise and thanks. And no one is present in that desert, so that it could be said that he only inclined to him because the onlookers would praise him. Also, perhaps this giver is a materialist (dahrī) who denies God and the Return, so it cannot be said that he only undertook that good deed out of desire for reward. So here reason judged the goodness of that good deed, while it is devoid of all aspects of benefit. Thus, it appears by these points that reason's judgment of good and bad is different from the intention of seeking benefits and avoiding harms.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage presents the proponents' fourth argument, which is designed to isolate moral judgment from any possible self-interest. The scenario involves a potentially life-saving act of charity under three conditions: (1) no personal benefit (loss of money), (2) no social reward (the recipient is unknown and no one is watching), and (3) no religious motive (the giver may be an atheist who denies God and the afterlife). Yet reason still judges the act as good. The proponents conclude from this that rational judgment of good and bad is independent of calculations of benefit and harm. Note: This argument is not followed by a refutation in the present pages.

Key Terms
الإحسان (doing good)
دهري (materialist/atheist)
التحسين والتقبيح (rational judgment)
جهات النفع (aspects of benefit)

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) To prove that atheists cannot be moral.
D) To encourage charity in deserts.

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument uses a scenario devoid of any possible benefit to argue that rational moral judgment is independent of seeking benefits or avoiding harms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
