The First Argument for Rational Judgment and Its Refutation
Proponents' first argument and opponents' refutation regarding injustice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proponents' first argument that even the unjust person's reason declares injustice ugly, and the opponents' refutation that this judgment stems from a desire to protect one's own life and property.

Core Idea
Moral judgments of good and bad are not independent; they stem from self-preservation.

This lesson unpacks a key exchange in the debate on rational judgment. The proponents argue that reason inherently knows good and bad, citing the example of an unjust person who still deems injustice ugly. The opponents respond by showing that this judgment is actually a calculation of self-interest: if the unjust person approved of injustice, he would have no grounds to protect himself from being wronged. Thus, they conclude that what seems like a moral verdict is really prudence.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument passage
Read the Arabic passage along with its translation. Notice the structure: first the proponents' claim, then the opponents' response beginning with 'So we say'.
The passage is a single argument-response unit.
2
Identify the proponents' claim
The proponents say that injustice is beneficial to the unjust person, yet his own clear reason judges it as ugly. This is meant to show that reason's judgment is independent of benefit.
3
Analyze the opponents' refutation
The opponents argue that if the unjust person judged injustice as good, he would be unable to defend himself from being wronged in turn. Thus, he judges it ugly only to protect his own life and property.
This refutation reduces the moral judgment to a prudential calculation.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this exchange illustrates the fundamental disagreement: do our judgments of good and bad have an objective basis, or are they rooted in self-interest?
This lesson covers only the first argument; other arguments follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول: إن الظلم نافع للظالم، مع أن الظالم يشهد صريح عقله يكون الظلم قبيحاً. وأما الحجة الأولى: وهي قوله: إن الظالم ينتفع بظلمه مع عقله يحكم بقبح ذلك الظلم. فنقول: لو حكم الظالم بحسن الظلم فحينئذ لا يمكنه دفع ذلك الظلم عن نفسه، وحينئذ تصير روحه عرضة للقتل، وماله عرضة للنهب فيجب عليه في رعاية مصالح نفسه وماله أن يحكم بقبح الظلم، حتى تبقى روحه وماله محفوظين عن الهلاك والتلف.
First: Injustice is beneficial to the unjust, while the unjust person's clear reason witnesses that injustice is ugly. And as for the first proof: which is his statement: 'The unjust person benefits from his injustice while his reason judges the ugliness of that injustice.' So we say: If the unjust person were to judge injustice as good, then he would not be able to repel that injustice from himself, and then his soul becomes liable to killing and his wealth liable to plunder, so he must, in consideration of the interests of himself and his wealth, judge injustice as ugly, so that his soul and wealth remain preserved from destruction and loss.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage contains a complete argument-rebuttal pair. It begins by presenting the first argument of the proponents of rational judgment (al-tahsin wa al-taqbīḥ): they claim that injustice can be beneficial to the unjust person, yet even the unjust person's own reason declares it ugly. This is meant to show that reason judges independently of benefit. The opponents of rational judgment then respond by denying that reason judges purely morally; instead, they analyze the situation in terms of self-interest. They argue that if the unjust person were to see injustice as good, he would not be able to protect himself from being wronged in turn, because he would have no rational basis to condemn it. Therefore, in order to preserve his own life and property, he must condemn injustice. Thus, the judgment of ugliness is actually a calculation of personal advantage, not a pure moral verdict. This reduces the seeming moral judgment to prudence.

Key Terms
الظلم (injustice)
صريح العقل (clear reason)
التحسين والتقبيح (rational judgment of good and bad)
مصلحة (interest)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the opponents deny any rational judgment, but they only deny it is independent of benefit.
Correction: The opponents accept that people judge actions as good or bad, but they argue that these judgments are ultimately based on considerations of self-interest, not on an inherent moral quality.

Checkpoint
According to the opponents of rational judgment, why does the unjust person judge injustice as ugly?
A) Because injustice is inherently ugly in itself.
B) Because he fears punishment from God.
C) Because he wants to protect his own life and property from being harmed in retaliation.
D) Because he has been taught by society.

Key Takeaway
The first argument for rational judgment is refuted by showing that the judgment of ugliness stems from self-preservation, not from an intrinsic moral perception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call something 'unjust', is your judgment ever tainted by the fear that you might suffer similarly? Can you identify a situation where you condemned someone for something you have done yourself? Repent and resolve to be consistent.
?
Your reason tells you that injustice is wrong. But what if you were in a position where injustice would benefit you and you could get away with it? Would your reason still cry out against it, or would you rationalize it? Search your heart and ask Allah for sincerity.
?
This argument shows that even the unjust person uses reason to condemn injustice out of self-interest. What does this reveal about your own moral evaluations? Are you truly seeking justice for its own sake, or are you merely protecting your own security and comfort? Commit today to act justly even when it hurts.

The Proponents' Second Argument: Aesthetic Pleasure vs. Rational Ugliness
The proponents present a hypothetical scenario to argue for intrinsic rational judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proponents' second argument that reason can judge actions as ugly independently of their pleasurable or beneficial consequences.

Core Idea
Reason judges moral ugliness even when an action is aesthetically pleasing.

The proponents present a vivid scenario: a beautifully composed poem that contains insults against prophets and angels. While listening is pleasurable, reason clearly judges the insults as ugly. This argument aims to show that moral judgments are not based on pleasure or utility but on an intrinsic quality perceived by reason. As you read, notice how the proponents separate aesthetic appreciation from moral evaluation.

Guided Path
1
Read the proponents' scenario
Read the Arabic passage describing the brilliant poem with eloquent words, beautiful handwriting, and pleasant recitation, but containing insults.
Notice the contrast between the pleasing medium and the repugnant content.
2
Identify the two elements
The passage mentions two things: the aesthetic pleasure (لذيذاً) and the rational judgment of ugliness (قبح).
3
Understand the argument's purpose
Ask yourself: What does this example prove? The proponents use it to argue that reason's judgment of ugliness is independent of consequences or pleasure.
This challenges the opponents' view that moral judgments are based on utility.
4
Reflect on the debate
Consider how this argument fits into the larger discussion about whether rational judgments are intrinsic or consequential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني: إنا إذا فرضنا إنساناً أنشأ قصيدة غراء بألفاظ فصيحة، وكتبها بخط حسن، وقرأها بأصوات طيبة، وكانت تلك القصيدة مشتملة على شتم الملائكة والأنبياء والصالحين، كان سماع تلك الألفاظ الفصيحة والتركيبات الكاملة بتلك الأصوات الطيبة لذيذاً، مع أن صريح العقل يحكم فيها بالقبح. وأما الحجة الثانية: وهي القصيدة الغراء المشتملة على شتم الملائكة والأنبياء. فجوابها: إن الحكم بحسن ذلك على خلاف مصالح العالم. وبيانه من وجهين: الأول: إنا إذا جوزنا ذلك الشتم والإساءة فحينئذ لا يبقى لأمر الله - تعالى - ونهيه وقع في القلوب. وذلك يوجب الهرج والمرج ويوثب الأراذل من الخلق على أفاضلهم. والثاني: إن أشرف الموجودات هو الله سبحانه، وأكثرهم إنعاماً على المحتاجين هو الله تعالى، فإذا لم تكن إساءة القول فيه ممنوعا منه. فحينئذ لا يمكن التوسل بالفضائل إلى دفع المضار، وذلك ضد مصلحة العالم.
And the second: If we suppose a person who composes a brilliant poem with eloquent words, writes it with beautiful handwriting, and recites it with pleasant voices, and that poem contains insults to the angels, prophets, and righteous people, the hearing of those eloquent words and perfect constructions with those pleasant sounds is pleasurable, while clear reason judges them as ugly. And as for the second proof: which is the brilliant poem containing insults to the angels and prophets. Its answer: The judgment that this is good is contrary to the interests of the world. Its explanation is from two aspects: First, if we permit such insult and abuse, then the command and prohibition of God – exalted is He – will no longer have weight in hearts. That leads to chaos and upheaval and causes the lowly among people to overcome the virtuous. Second, the most honorable of existents is God the Exalted, and the most bountiful to the needy is God the Exalted. If speech insulting Him is not prohibited, then virtues cannot be used to repel harms, and that is contrary to the interest of the world.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument of the proponents and the opponents' response. The proponents argue that one can derive aesthetic pleasure from a poem that includes insults against sacred figures, yet reason still judges those insults as morally ugly. This, they claim, shows that reason's judgment is not about pleasure but about moral value. The opponents reply that judging such insults as ugly is actually based on the interests of the world (maṣāliḥ al-ʿālam). They give two reasons: (1) permitting insults against prophets and angels would undermine the authority of God's commands and prohibitions, leading to social chaos and the dominance of the lowly over the virtuous; (2) God is the most honorable and the greatest benefactor, so allowing insults against Him would prevent virtues from being used to repel harms – that is, it would be against the overall benefit of the world. Thus, the judgment is again reduced to social utility, not pure reason.

Key Terms
قصيدة غراء (brilliant poem)
شتم الملائكة والأنبياء (insulting angels and prophets)
لذيذاً (pleasurable)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the proponents, what is the relationship between the aesthetic pleasure of the poem and rational judgment of its content?
A) They are identical.
B) The pleasure determines the judgment.
C) The judgment remains ugly despite pleasure.
D) The judgment is based on the poet's intention.

Key Takeaway
The proponents use the example of a beautiful but insulting poem to argue that reason can judge an action as ugly independent of its pleasurable or beneficial aspects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you enjoyed something – a conversation, a piece of media, a success – that your conscience quietly told you was wrong? What did you do with that judgment?
?
This passage shows that even a beautiful, eloquent poem can be morally ugly. What 'beautiful' things in your life (your skills, your words, your relationships) might be masking something your clear reason condemns?
?
If clear reason (ṣarīḥ al-ʿaql) cannot be silenced by pleasure, then the ugliness you ignore today will still stand before you tomorrow. What is one rational judgment you have been avoiding because it costs you pleasure? Will you face it now?

The Opponents' Refutation: Consequences Undermine Authority
The opponents refute the second argument with two consequentialist reasons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponents' refutation that permitting insults against prophets and angels leads to societal chaos and undermines divine authority.

Core Idea
Permitting insults against the sacred leads to the collapse of moral order.

The opponents respond to the proponents' poem argument by showing that the judgment against the insults is actually based on societal interests. They provide two reasons: first, allowing such insults would cause God's commands to lose their weight in hearts, leading to chaos; second, it would prevent virtues from being used to repel harms. This refutation argues that the prohibition is for the greater good of the world.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponents' response
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'وأما الحجة الثانية' to the end. It contains the refutation and two reasons.
Notice how the opponents tie the judgment to مصلحة العالم (interests of the world).
2
Identify the two reasons
The passage lists two clear reasons. Find and note each one.
First reason: loss of weight of divine commands leads to chaos. Second: if God is insulted, virtues cannot repel harms.
3
Understand each reason's logic
For each reason, ask: How does permitting insults lead to harm? The first reason focuses on social order, the second on the inability to use virtues for protection.
4
Evaluate the refutation
Consider whether these reasons successfully counter the proponents' claim that the judgment is intrinsic. Do they reduce everything to utility?

?
In your daily life, do you give proper weight to God's commands and prohibitions, or do you prioritize personal convenience? ?
When you encounter insults against prophets or God—whether in media, online, or in conversation—do you feel a moral urgency, or has indifference crept in? How does remaining silent align with the chaos this passage warns against, and what concrete action will you take the next time you witness such irreverence?
?
If you truly believed that a permissive attitude toward sacred insults leads to societal breakdown, what specific changes would you make in your immediate circle—family, friends, or online interactions—to actively uphold reverence for the divine, even at the cost of popularity or comfort?

The Third Argument: Beneficial Lies and Social Harm
Sanusi presents the proponents' third argument and the opponents' refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the opponents of rational moral judgment refute the argument that beneficial lies prove moral judgments are not based on benefit.

Core Idea
A single lie may benefit, but systematic lying destroys social trust.

The third argument for rational judgment claims that beneficial lies are still considered ugly by reason, proving morality is not about benefit. In response, the defenders of interest-based ethics argue that the real calculus must consider society-wide consequences. This lesson unpacks that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the argument's flow. Notice the proponents' claim and then the opponents' response.
The Arabic has a small textual uncertainty: 'ينادي عليه' might mean 'denounces it as ugly'.
2
Identify the proponents' claim
The proponents say a beneficial lie is still considered ugly by reason. They use this to argue that moral judgment is not based on benefit.
They assume that if morality were based on benefit, a beneficial lie would be good, but it is not.
3
Follow the opponents' response
The opponents shift from individual benefit to systemic social consequences. They argue that permitting lying undermines trust and wastes actions built on false statements, harming overall social welfare.
This refutes the proponents by showing that the judgment of ugliness is indeed based on long-term benefit.
4
Reflect on the logical structure
Ask yourself: How does focusing on social welfare refute the argument? The opponents accept that a particular lie might bring short-term benefit, but they argue that the very permission of lying destroys the basis for reliable communication.
This is a common move in ethical debates: shifting from individual acts to rules or systems.
5
Connect to the broader debate
Consider why this argument matters for the overall question of whether moral judgments are rational or based on benefit. The opponents show that even the 'ugliness' of lying is tied to interest when properly understood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث: إن الكذب النافع منتفع به، مع صريح العقل ينادي عليه [بالقبح؟]. وأما الحجة الثالثة: وهي قولهم: الكذب النافع منتفع به، مع العقل يقضي بقبحه. فالجواب: إن تجويز الكذب على خلاف مصلحة العالم لأنا إذا جوزنا الكذب فالذي نسمعه قد تنبني عليه أغراض كثيرة في الفعل والترك. فإذا ظهر أنه كان كذباً في تلك الأحوال، فحينئذ تضيع تلك الأعمال المبنية عليه، ويضيق قلب ذلك الفاعل، ويضيع عمره. وكل ذلك على ضد مصلحة العالم.
And the third: Beneficial lying is benefited from, while clear reason calls out [against it? ugliness?]. And as for the third proof: which is their statement: 'Beneficial lying is benefited from, while reason decrees its ugliness.' The answer: Permitting lying is contrary to the interest of the world, because if we permit lying, then what we hear [from someone] may have many purposes built upon it in action and omission. If it then becomes clear that it was a lie in those situations, then the actions built upon it are wasted, the heart of that doer becomes constricted, and his life is wasted. All that is contrary to the interest of the world.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage presents the proponents' third argument: sometimes a lie can be beneficial (e.g., saving a life), yet reason still judges it as ugly. This, they say, proves that moral judgment is not about benefit. The opponents respond by arguing that permitting lying is detrimental to the overall welfare of society. They explain that if lying is allowed, then any statement we hear could have important consequences—people build actions on it. If the statement later turns out to be false, then all those actions are wasted, causing distress and loss. Therefore, society has an interest in condemning all lies, even if a particular lie seems beneficial in the short term. Thus, the judgment of ugliness is again based on public interest.

Key Terms
الكذب النافع (beneficial lie)
منتفع به (benefited from)
مصلحة العالم (interest of the world)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponents argue that some lies are beneficial but still wrong.
Correction: The opponents actually argue that permitting lying is always harmful to social welfare, so no lie is truly beneficial when considering systemic effects.

Checkpoint
What is the opponents' main concern regarding permitting lying?
A) It displeases God.
B) It leads to individual guilt.
C) It wastes the actions based on false statements and harms overall social benefit.
D) It makes people less intelligent.

Key Takeaway
The refutation shows that even seemingly beneficial lies are harmful because they undermine the system of trust upon which social cooperation depends.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you chose a 'small' lie to avoid discomfort? How did that choice affect your own sense of integrity and the trust someone placed in you? Will you commit to honesty even in trivial matters, knowing that a pattern of small lies erodes the fabric of trust?
?
Consider a time you discovered that someone you trusted had lied to you. How did that revelation affect your willingness to rely on them? Now reflect on your own statements—are there any areas where you habitually bend the truth? What would it cost you to be fully truthful, and is that cost greater than the harm of a single lie?
?
Imagine if everyone acted on the principle that 'a lie is permissible if it benefits someone.' How would that change your daily interactions? Would you be able to trust any news, advice, or promise? Are you willing to be part of the solution by being scrupulously truthful, or have you already contributed to the problem?

The Deniers' Answer: Morality as Self-Interest
Deniers respond to first argument by reducing it to self-interest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how deniers of objective moral values argue that the judgment against oppression is based on self-interest rather than intrinsic wrongness.

Core Idea
The deniers root all moral judgments in self-interest, not in objective right or wrong.

This lesson examines the first argument from deniers of moral objectivity (أهل التحسين والتقبيح). They respond to the claim that even oppressors instinctively know oppression is wrong. Instead, they argue that this judgment is a calculated move to protect one's own life and property. As you read, notice how they reduce moral intuitions to calculations of benefit and harm.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic once, then the translation. Notice the deniers' opening claim that all arguments reduce to benefit and harm.
Pay attention to the phrase 'يرجع حاصله إلى طلب المنفعة ودفع المضرة'.
2
Analyze the logical structure
Trace the deniers' response: they present the first argument (oppressor's intellect judges ugliness), then use conditional reasoning to show that if the oppressor judged oppression as good, he would harm himself.
3
Reflect on the motive
Consider the deniers' conclusion: the oppressor judges oppression ugly to protect his own life and property. How does this differ from saying oppression is intrinsically bad?
This is the core of their denial of objective moral values.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجاب المنكرون للتحسين وللتقبيح . فقالوا : جميع ما ذكرتم ، يرجع حاصله إلى طلب المنفعة ودفع المضرة . أما الحجة الأولى : وهي قوله : إن الظالم ينتفع بظلمه إن عقله مع يحكم بقبح ذلك الظلم . فنقول : لو حكم الظالم بحسن الظلم فحينئذ لا يمكنه دفع ذلك الظلم عن نفسه ، وحينئذ تصير روحه عرضة للقتل ، وماله عرضة للنهب فيجب عليه في رعاية مصالح نفسه وماله أن يحكم بقبح الظلم ، حتى تبقى روحه وماله محفوظين عن الهلاك والتلف .
And the deniers of moral approval and disapproval answered. They said: All that you have mentioned reduces in its outcome to seeking benefit and repelling harm. As for the first argument: it is his statement: 'The oppressor benefits from his oppression while his intellect judges that oppression as ugly.' We say: If the oppressor judged oppression as good, then he could not repel that oppression from himself, and then his soul would become exposed to killing and his property to plunder. So he must, in caring for the interests of his self and property, judge oppression as ugly, so that his soul and property remain preserved from destruction and loss.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage begins the answer of the deniers of objective moral values (al-tahsin wa al-taqbih). They state that all arguments for moral objectivity reduce to benefit and harm. They then take up the first argument: the oppressor's own intellect judges his oppression as ugly, even though he benefits from it. The deniers respond that this judgment is actually based on self-interest: if the oppressor allowed himself to see oppression as good, he would be unable to defend himself, leading to his death and loss of property. Thus he judges it ugly to protect his own life and wealth. The passage shows that even this seemingly moral judgment is rooted in personal benefit.

Key Terms
التحسين والتقبيح
المنفعة والمضرة
الظلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the deniers deny all morality.
Correction: They merely ground morality in benefit and harm, not intrinsic values.

Checkpoint
According to the deniers, why does the oppressor judge oppression as ugly?
A) Because it is inherently evil
B) To protect his own life and property
C) Because it harms others
D) Because God commands it

Key Takeaway
The deniers argue that even the oppressor's judgment of ugliness is driven by self-preservation, not by an objective moral standard.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you called an action 'wrong' or 'evil'. Was it because it threatened your own interests or those of someone you care about? Or did you truly consider the act in itself, independent of its consequences for you? Be honest: can you identify even a trace of self-interest in your moral judgment? Commit today to examine one such judgment you made recently.
?
The deniers reduce all moral judgments to self-interest. If that were true, would your own sense of right and wrong be nothing more than a sophisticated survival instinct? Does that possibility disturb you? Reflect: if morality is merely a tool for self-preservation, what would be the point of sacrificing your own interests for the sake of justice? Confront that question and let it lead you to examine the foundations of your convictions.
?
Imagine you were the oppressor in the example. You know you benefit from oppression, yet you also judge it as ugly. According to the deniers, you do so only to protect yourself. Is that true for you? When you condemn injustice in the world, are you primarily protecting your own safety and property, or is there something more—a genuine love for truth and fairness? This week, actively look for a situation where condemning injustice costs you something. See what happens inside you.

The Deniers' Answer: Insulting the Divine Leads to Chaos
Deniers answer the second argument with two reasons based on societal interests.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the deniers argue that calling a poem insulting angels and prophets 'good' is contrary to the interests of the world, and the two reasons they provide.

Core Idea
Permitting insult to the divine destroys societal order.

The deniers respond to the challenge of a hypothetical poem that insults angels and prophets. They argue that judging such a poem as 'good' would have disastrous consequences for society by undermining divine authority and removing incentives for virtue. Let's examine their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the English translation. Notice that the deniers are answering an argument, not presenting a new one.
The passage is a single unit of objection and answer.
2
Identify the two reasons
The deniers give two reasons why judging the poem as good is contrary to worldly interests. Find each reason and understand how it connects to social order.
The first reason focuses on divine commands, the second on the highest being (God).
3
Reflect on the underlying principle
Ask yourself: Are the deniers saying the poem is objectively bad, or that calling it good leads to harmful consequences? This lesson shows a utilitarian approach to moral judgment.
This argument is about social utility, not intrinsic value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الثانية : وهي القصيدة الغراء المشتملة على شتم الملائكة والأنبياء . فجوابها : إن الحكم بحسن ذلك على خلاف مصالح العالم . وبيانه من وجهين : الأول : إنا إذا جوزنا ذلك الشتم والإساءة فحينئذ لا يبقى لأمر الله - تعالى - ونهيه وقع في القلوب. وذلك يوجب الهرج والمرج ويوثب الأراذل من الخلق على أفاضلهم . والثاني : إن أشرف الموجودات هو الله سبحانه ، وأكثرهم إنعاماً على المحتاجين هو الله تعالى ، فإذا لم تكن إساءة القول فيه ممنوعا منه . فحينئذ لا يمكن التوسل بالفضائل إلى دفع المضار ، وذلك ضد مصلحة العالم
And as for the second argument: it is the brilliant poem containing insult of the angels and prophets. Its answer: that judging that as good is contrary to the interests of the world. Its explanation is from two aspects: First: If we permit that insult and offense, then there would remain no weight for the command of God Almighty and His prohibition in hearts. That causes chaos and turmoil and makes the lowly among people attack their superiors. Second: The noblest of existents is God, and the most beneficent to the needy is God Almighty. So if speech insulting Him is not prevented, then it becomes impossible to use virtues to repel harms, and that is against the interest of the world.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection and its answer. The objection is a hypothetical poem that insults angels and prophets. The deniers argue that calling such a poem 'good' would undermine divine commands, causing social chaos. Two reasons are given: First, if insult is allowed, God's commands lose authority, leading to turmoil. Second, since God is the most noble and beneficent, allowing insults against Him would destroy the incentive to use virtues against harms. Both reasons show that the judgment of 'good' or 'bad' here depends on worldly interest, not intrinsic value.

Key Terms
مصالح العالم
شتم
الهرج والمرج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument is about defending the honor of prophets and angels against blasphemy.
Correction: The deniers argue based on societal utility, not religious sentiment. They claim that calling the poem 'good' would have harmful social consequences.

Checkpoint
According to the deniers, what would happen if insult of the divine is permitted?
A) People would stop believing in God
B) Divine commands would lose weight and society would fall into chaos
C) The poem would be considered beautiful
D) Angels and prophets would be angry

Key Takeaway
The deniers argue that permitting insult to the divine leads to societal chaos and undermines the very purpose of divine commands.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever spoken lightly or disrespectfully about Allah or His prophets in private conversation? If so, what does this passage reveal about the hidden consequences of such speech—not just on society, but on the weight of divine commands in your own heart? Will you repent and resolve to guard your tongue?
?
The deniers argue that permitting insult makes the lowly attack the virtuous. Is there a person of piety you have mocked or looked down on because of their religious commitment? How does this passage force you to reconsider that attitude, and what will you do to change it?
?
If you truly believed that insulting God destroys the very mechanism of virtue in the world, how would that change the way you respond when you hear others blaspheme? Would you remain silent, or would you speak up out of love for Allah and concern for the world's well-being?

Third Argument: Beneficial Lying
Deniers answer the argument about beneficial lying being judged ugly by reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the deniers argue that permitting lying contradicts worldly interest and how this undermines the claim that beneficial lying is inherently ugly.

Core Idea
Permitting lying would collapse the trust needed for action, causing harm to worldly order.

The third argument from the proponents claims that beneficial lying is still judged ugly by reason. The deniers respond by shifting the focus from intrinsic ugliness to consequences: if lies are permitted, every statement becomes unreliable, actions based on them are wasted, and hearts become constricted. This passage explains why the prohibition of lying is actually based on harm to worldly interest.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic and English to get the flow of the argument. Notice how the deniers structure their response.
The passage is the deniers' answer to the third argument.
2
Identify the core claim
The deniers say permitting lying is contrary to worldly interest. Find how they justify this by listing consequences.
They mention wasted actions, constricted heart, wasted life.
3
Reflect on the logical structure
The deniers are not saying lying is good; they are saying its ugliness is due to harm, not intrinsic nature. Consider why this matters for the larger debate.
This connects to the debate about moral judgments being rational vs. based on consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الثالثة : وهي قولهم : الكذب النافع منتفع به ، مع العقل يقضي بقبحه . فالجواب : إن تجويز الكذب على خلاف مصلحة العالم لأنا إذا جوزنا الكذب فالذي نسمعه قد تنبني عليه أغراض كثيرة في الفعل والترك . فإذا ظهر أنه كان كذباً في تلك الأحوال ، فحينئذ تضيع تلك الأعمال المبنية عليه ، ويضيق قلب ذلك الفاعل ، ويضيع عمره . وكل ذلك على ضـد مصلحة العالم
And as for the third argument: it is their statement: 'Beneficial lying is benefited from, while reason judges it as ugly.' The answer: that permitting lying is contrary to the interest of the world. For if we permit lying, then what we hear may have many purposes built upon it in action and inaction. If it becomes apparent that it was a lie in those situations, then those actions built upon it are wasted, the heart of the agent becomes constricted, and his life is wasted. All of that is against the interest of the world.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage addresses the third objection: a lie that brings benefit is still judged ugly by reason. The deniers respond that permitting lying would undermine the entire system of communication on which actions depend. If lies are allowed, every statement becomes suspect; actions based on that information may be wasted, causing psychological distress and loss of effort. This shows that the moral judgment against lying is actually based on its harmful consequences to worldly order, not on an inherent quality.

Correction: The deniers do not claim lying is always wrong; they claim its wrongness stems from harm, not intrinsic evil. They do not say it is permissible, only that its ugliness is consequential.

The Deniers' Answer: Self-Interest Behind Altruism
The deniers answer the blind sick person argument with two utilitarian explanations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the deniers of objective moral values explain a seemingly selfless act of helping a blind sick person in a desert as rooted in personal interest and social convention.

Core Idea
Even kindness can be explained by self-interest.

One of the strongest objections against moral subjectivism is the case of a person who helps a blind stranger in a desert where no one sees them. How can this altruism be selfish? The deniers offer a multi-layered answer, invoking human psychology and social convention. This passage reveals their reasoning and challenges us to think about the foundations of ethics.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to get a clear sense of the author's words and structure.
Notice the two numbered reasons: الأول and الثاني.
2
Identify the two reasons
The passage gives two reasons. The first involves psychological projection causing personal pain if one does not help. The second involves social convention and mutual expectation. Pinpoint each.
3
Understand how these answer the objection
Consider how each reason reduces the apparently selfless act to a self-interested one. Does this explanation fully account for the moral feeling we have?
This connects to the broader debate about objective vs. subjective morality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الرابعة : وهي الإحسان إلى المريض الأعمى ، الذي يكون في صحراء ليس فيها أحد. فالجواب : أن فيه رعاية المصالح من وجوه : الأول : إن الإنسان جبل بحيث كل ما يراه في غيره من أبناء جنسه ، فإنه يفرضه في حق نفسه . فلما رأى هذا الشخص ذلك المريض على تلك الحالة ، سبق وهمه وخياله إلى فرض تلك الحالة في حق نفسه ، وحينئذ يميل طبعه إلى السعي في تخليصه من ذلك البلاء ، ولو لم يفعل ذلك فإنه يتألم قلبه فكان إقدامه على ذلك العمل موجباً لدافع الرقة الجنسية عن القلب . وهو مصلحة عظيمة . والثاني : إن من الأوضاع المعتبرة في حفظ مصالح العالم ترغيب الخلق في الإحسان ، على رجاء أنه لو اتفق له مثل تلك الحالة سعوا في إفاضة الرحمة عليه ، وإذا كان هذا المعنى معتبراً في مصالح العالم ، لا جرم اصطلح الناس على تحسينه وتقبيح تركه . ولما ألف الناس هذا الاصطلاح وتوافقوا عليه ، واستمروا عليه ، من أول العمر ، إلى آخره ، لا جرم تقررت تلك الأحوال في قلوبهم وعقولهم .
And as for the fourth argument: it is doing good to the blind sick person who is in a desert with no one in it. The answer: that there is consideration of interests in it from aspects: First: Man is created such that everything he sees in others of his kind, he projects onto himself. When this person saw that sick one in that state, his imagination and fancy preceded to project that state onto himself, and then his nature inclines to strive to rescue him from that affliction. If he did not do that, his heart would suffer pain. So his undertaking that act becomes a cause for repelling natural sympathy from the heart, and that is a great interest. Second: Among the established customs in preserving the interests of the world is encouraging people to do good, in hope that if such a state befalls him, they will strive to show mercy to him. And since this meaning is considered in the interests of the world, without doubt people have agreed upon deeming it good and deeming its abandonment bad. And when people became accustomed to this convention and agreed upon it, and continued upon it from the beginning of life to its end, without doubt those states become established in their hearts and minds.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage answers the fourth objection: an altruistic act of helping a blind sick person in a desert, which seems purely moral. The deniers give two explanations rooted in utility. First, humans naturally project themselves into others' situations; helping relieves their own sympathetic pain, thus serving their own psychological interest. Second, societies establish conventions of mutual aid because everyone hopes for help if needed. Over time, these conventions become ingrained as moral feelings. Thus, even this apparently selfless act is grounded in benefit and harm.

Key Terms
الإحسان
الرقة الجنسية
الاصطلاح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The story of the blind sick person proves that morality is objective and not based on self-interest.
Correction: The deniers argue that even here, the helper acts to relieve personal psychological distress or follows a useful social convention.

Checkpoint

B) Because they imagine themselves in that situation and feel personal distress
C) Because they seek praise from others
D) Because they fear divine punishment

Key Takeaway
The deniers explain seemingly selfless acts as ultimately self-interested, via psychological projection and social convention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

In your own practice, have you ever done something good that you were sure had no benefit to you at all—not even a feeling of satisfaction? If not, what would it take to do something truly for others?

Refuting the Argument from Beneficence
Refutation of the fourth argument: beneficence to a blind patient.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Fakhr al-Din al-Razi refutes the argument that altruistic actions prove objective moral values by reducing them to self-interest and social convention.

Core Idea
Even altruism can be explained by self-interest; thus, goodness is not objective.

This passage addresses a key debate in Islamic theology: whether actions are intrinsically good or bad. The opponent cites helping a blind stranger in the desert as a selfless act. The author shows that such acts are motivated by psychological relief and social norms, not objective moral properties.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the author's argumentative flow.
Pay attention to the two numbered aspects (الأول, الثاني).
2
Identify the two aspects of the refutation
The author presents two reasons why helping a blind patient is not purely altruistic. Find them and understand how each reduces to self-benefit.
First is natural sympathy causing pain relief; second is social convention of mutual aid.
3
Understand the conclusion about motives in God
The author concludes that all such motives are based on benefit/harm. Since God is free from need, He cannot have such motives. Therefore, objective goodness/badness do not apply to God.
This is the core theological point: divine actions are not bound by human moral categories.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider how this refutation challenges the Mu'tazilite view of objective moral values. What does it mean for our understanding of God's justice?
This also links to the Ash'ari position that goodness and badness are determined by divine command.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الرابعة : وهي الإحسان إلى المريض الأعمى ، الذي يكون في صحراء ليس فيها أحد. فالجواب : أن فيه رعاية المصالح من وجوه : الأول : إن الإنسان جبل بحيث كل ما يراه في غيره من أبناء جنسه ، فإنه يفرضه في حق نفسه . فلما رأى هذا الشخص ذلك المريض على تلك الحالة ، سبق وهمه وخياله إلى فرض تلك الحالة في حق نفسه ، وحينئذ يميل طبعه إلى السعي في تخليصه من ذلك البلاء ، ولو لم يفعل ذلك فإنه يتألم قلبه فكان إقدامه على ذلك العمل موجباً لدافع الرقة الجنسية عن القلب . وهو مصلحة عظيمة والثاني : إن من الأوضاع المعتبرة في حفظ مصالح العالم ترغيب الخلق في الإحسان ، على رجاء أنه لو اتفق له مثل تلك الحالة سعوا في إفاضة الرحمة عليه ، وإذا كان هذا المعنى معتبراً في مصالح العالم ، لا جرم اصطلح الناس على تحسينه وتقبيح تركه ان ولما ألف الناس هذا الاصطلاح وتوافقوا عليه ، واستمروا عليه ، من أول العمر ، إلى آخره ، لا جرم تقررت تلك الأحوال في قلوبهم وعقولهم . فيثبت مجموع هذه الوجوه التي ذكروها ، لا تخرج عن رعاية المصالح والمفاسد . إما بواسطة واحدة ، أو بوسائط كثيرة . فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن الحسن والقبح ، لا معنى لهما إلا السعي في جلب المنفعة ودفع المفسدة. ولما حصل الاتفاق على أن ثبوت هذا الداعي في حق الله تعالى محال . كان القول بإثبات الدواعي المبنية على الحسن والقبح محالا في حق الله تعالى . فهذا تقرير الكلام في هذا المقام .
As for the fourth argument: it is beneficence to the blind sick person who is in a desert where there is no one. The answer: that it involves consideration of interests from several aspects: First: man is naturally disposed such that everything he sees in others of his kind, he imagines it in himself. So when this person saw that patient in that state, his imagination and fancy preceded to imagine that state in himself, and then his nature inclines to strive to deliver him from that affliction, and if he does not do that, his heart is pained. So his undertaking that action is a cause for repelling natural sympathy from the heart, and it is a great interest. Second: among the established customs in preserving the interests of the world is encouraging people to beneficence, hoping that if such a state should happen to them, they would strive to bestow mercy upon him. And since this meaning is considered in the interests of the world, inevitably people have agreed upon its goodness and the evil of its abandonment. And when people established this convention and agreed upon it, and persisted upon it from the beginning of life to its end, inevitably those states became established in their hearts and minds. So the sum of these aspects they mentioned do not go beyond consideration of interests and harms, either by one intermediary or many intermediaries. So it is established by what we have mentioned: that goodness and badness have no meaning except striving to bring benefit and repel harm. And when agreement was reached that the establishment of this motive with respect to God is impossible, then the claim of establishing motives based on goodness and badness is impossible with respect to God. So this is the exposition of the discourse in this station.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an argument posed by an opponent (likely the Mu'tazila) who claimed that an act of beneficence to a blind stranger in an empty desert proves that actions can be intrinsically good or bad, independent of any self-interest. The author, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi (or the author of this work), responds by analyzing the motives behind such an act. He identifies two aspects: first, a natural psychological tendency—a person seeing another in distress imagines himself in that state, which causes inner pain (al-riqqa al-jinsiyya), and relieving that pain becomes a self-benefit. Second, social convention—societies encourage beneficence because everyone hopes that if they ever need help, others will help them. This convention reinforces the perception of goodness and badness. The author then concludes that all such aspects reduce to seeking benefit and repelling harm. Since God is free from needs and benefits, attributing motives based on goodness and badness to Him is impossible. Thus, the argument from beneficence fails to establish objective moral properties independent of benefit and harm.

Key Terms
الحسن والقبح
الداعي
المصالح
الرقة الجنسية
تحسين وتقبيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument from beneficence proves that actions are intrinsically good or bad, independent of consequences.
Correction: The author shows that even seemingly altruistic acts are motivated by self-interest (relieving personal pain or social reciprocity), so they do not demonstrate objective moral values.

Checkpoint

C) Intrinsic goodness of the act and love of God
D) Philosophical reasoning and moral obligation

Key Takeaway
The argument from beneficence fails because even altruistic acts are rooted in self-interest and social convention, not objective moral properties; thus, goodness and badness are relative to benefit and harm.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you helped someone when no one was watching. Can you honestly say that your action was completely free from any self-regarding motive—such as avoiding guilt, feeling good about yourself, or hoping for reward? How does this lesson unsettle your perception of your own 'selflessness'?
?
If, as this lesson argues, every 'good' deed can be reduced to seeking benefit or avoiding harm, does that make you less motivated to do good? Or does it challenge you to purify your intentions for Allah alone? What concrete step will you take today to act with pure intention?
?
The author says that people establish conventions of goodness and evil for social benefit. Consider a moral conviction you hold strongly—does it have any root in social convention or personal advantage? How do you know that your moral framework is not merely a product of your environment? This question is not comfortable—sit with it.

Critiquing Altruism: Al-Razi on the Motive of Need vs. Beneficence
Al-Razi defines two motives and argues only self-interest is witnessed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi argues that all apparent altruistic actions ultimately stem from self-interest (the motive of need) and why this critique challenges the Mu'tazilite concept of an independent motive of beneficence.

Core Idea
Every observed motive of beneficence reduces to self-interest.

Al-Razi challenges the notion that humans can act purely for the benefit of others. Through a vivid thought experiment, he argues that even the most apparently selfless acts are driven by the motive of need—a desire to achieve personal benefit or avoid personal harm. This passage is central to his critique of ethical objectivism.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
Start by reading the definitions of the two motives: dā'ī al-ḥāja (motive of need) is striving for one's own benefit; dā'ī al-ḥikma (motive of wisdom) is striving for the benefit of others.
These definitions set up the dichotomy.
2
Understand the thought experiment
Consider the example of a materialist (dahrī) helping a blind person in an empty desert. The author claims that even this act can be explained by self-regarding motives: self-image, relief of sympathy, and social conditioning.
Focus on how each motive reduces to self-interest.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Al-Razi concludes that if the three self-interested motives were absent, we cannot guarantee the act would occur. Therefore, no pure altruistic motive has been empirically proven.
This conclusion is epistemological, not ontological.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المقام الثاني : إنا إذا سلمنا حصول المغايرة بين جلب المنفعة ودفع المضرة ، وبين كون الشيء حسناً أو قبيحاً . نقول : قد ذكرنا أن حاصل الكلام في تفسير داعي الحاجة : السعي إلى إيصال الخير والنفع إلى النفس . وتفسير داعي الحكمة : السعي في إيصال الخير إلى الغير. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إن التجربة تدل على أن السعي في إيصال النفع إلى النفس . وفي دفع الضرر عن النفس يمكن حصوله مع عدم السعي في اتصال النفع إلى الغير، وفي دفع الضرر عن الغير من غير أن يكون المقصود منه السعي في إيصال النفع إلى النفس ودفع الضرر عن النفس . فهذا مما يثبت عندنا جوازه بالدليل . وتقريره : أن الواحد منا إذا أحسن إلى المريض الأعمى في الصحراء الخالية مع كون ذلك المحسن دهرياً ، منكراً للثواب والعقاب ، فله فيه أغراض ومنافع . أحدها : أنه إذا أتى بذلك العمل ، صار هو عند نفسه ، موصوفاً بصفة حميدة . وهي كونه محسناً إلى المحتاجين. وإذا لم يأت بذلك الإحسان ، فإنه يكون عند نفسه موصوفاً بالقسوة والغلظة وعدم الرحمة . فالحامل له على ذلك الفعل : تحصيل صفة الكمال لنفسه ، ودفع صفة النقص عن نفسه . وثانيها : ما ذكرناه في الفصل المتقدم أنه يدفع بذلك الفعل عن قلبه ألم الرقة الجنسية . وثالثها : إنه لما رأى ذلك الفعل خالياً للمدح والثناء في أكثر الصور . والنادر يلحق بالغالب في الخيال والوهم صارت هذه الصورة ملحقة بذلك الغالب . فأما لو قدرنا انتفاء هذه الأحوال الثلاثة ، فإنا لا نعرف أن ذلك الرجل يحسن إلى ذلك الفقير الأعمى ، بل ربما فعل ، وربما لم يفعل . فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أنه لم يثبت عندنا في الشاهد نوع آخر من الدواعي ، سوى القسم الذي سميتموه بداعية الحاجة . فاما القسم الذي سميتموه بداعية الإحسان ، فلم يثبت بالدليل وجوده في الشاهد البنة .
As for the second station: if we concede the occurrence of distinction between bringing benefit and repelling harm, and between a thing being good or bad, we say: we have already mentioned that the gist of the discourse concerning the interpretation of the motive of need (dā'ī al-ḥāja) is: striving to bring good and benefit to oneself. And the interpretation of the motive of wisdom (dā'ī al-ḥikma) is: striving to bring good to others. If you know this, we say: indeed experience indicates that striving to bring benefit to oneself and repelling harm from oneself can occur without striving to connect benefit to others, and repelling harm from others without the intention being striving to bring benefit to oneself and repelling harm from oneself. So this is something whose permissibility is established with us by evidence. And its exposition: that one of us, if he does good to the blind patient in the empty desert while that benefactor is a materialist (dahrī), denying reward and punishment, then he has in that purposes and benefits. One of them: that when he performs that action, he becomes in his own eyes described as a praiseworthy quality—namely being a benefactor to the needy. And if he does not perform that beneficence, he becomes in his own eyes described as harshness, roughness, and lack of mercy. So the motive for that action is: acquiring a quality of perfection for himself, and repelling a quality of deficiency from himself. Second of them: what we mentioned in the previous chapter, that he repels by that action from his heart the pain of natural sympathy. Third of them: that since he saw that action as subject to praise and commendation in most cases, and the rare is attached to the common in imagination and fancy, this case becomes attached to that common. But if we suppose the absence of these three states, then we do not know that that man would do good to that poor blind person; rather, perhaps he would do it, and perhaps he would not. So it is established by what we have mentioned: that there is not established with us in the witnessed world another type of motives besides the type you call the motive of need. As for the type you call the motive of beneficence (dā'ī al-ijtsān), its existence in the witnessed world has not been established by evidence at all.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a sustained argument distinguishing two types of motives: dā'ī al-ḥāja (motive of need), which seeks benefit for oneself, and dā'ī al-ḥikma (motive of wisdom, also called dā'ī al-ijtsān), which seeks benefit for others. The author, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi (or the author), first defines them. Then he argues from experience (tajriba) that the two can occur independently. However, he then tests the supposedly altruistic motive by considering a materialist (dahrī) who helps a blind patient in an empty desert. He identifies three personal benefits that motivate the materialist: (1) acquiring a self-image of being noble and avoiding a self-image of cruelty, (2) relieving the pain of natural sympathy (mentioned in the previous argument), and (3) the habit of praise and blame. If these three self-interested motives were absent, we cannot be sure the act would occur. Therefore, the only motive proven in the witnessed world is the motive of need (self-interest). The so-called motive of beneficence (purely altruistic) has no empirical evidence. This argument is part of a broader refutation of the Mu'tazilite view that actions can be intrinsically good or bad independent of consequences, and that God can act for the sake of others' benefit.

Key Terms
داعي الحاجة
داعي الحكمة
داعي الإحسان
الشاهد
دهري
الرقة الجنسية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-Razi denies that altruistic actions ever occur, or that he denies any possible goodness in helping others.
Correction: Al-Razi does not deny that actions appear altruistic; he denies that a purely altruistic motive (independent of self-interest) has been empirically demonstrated. The argument is about the motive, not the act itself.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude about the existence of a separate motive of beneficence (dā'ī al-ihsān) in the witnessed world?
A) It is proven by experience and reason
B) It is not proven by evidence at all
C) It is proven only in the case of prophets
D) It is proven but rare

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi concludes that the only motive empirically proven in the witnessed world is the motive of need; the motive of beneficence remains unsubstantiated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about a time you did a good deed in complete secrecy, where no one would ever know. According to al-Razi, what self-regarding motives might have been present? How does that make you feel about the sincerity of your own heart? Will you now examine your intentions more carefully before your next act of charity?
?
If al-Razi is correct that every observed good deed has a self-interested component, how does that challenge your understanding of your own acts of worship? Is your salah really for Allah alone, or are there hidden motives like self-image or relief from guilt? Which motive dominates your daily religious practice?
?
The materialist in al-Razi's example helps the blind man. If you were that blind man, would it matter to you that the helper's motive was ultimately self-regarding? Why or why not? How does this lesson force you to reconsider the value you place on the intentions of those who help you?

The First Opinion: Preponderance Without a Factor Is Impossible
Author states the first opinion: preponderance without preponderating factor is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first opinion of the mutakallimun that preponderance without a preponderating factor is impossible, yet action becomes more likely without becoming obligatory.

Core Idea
Preponderance needs a factor, but even then, action is not forced.

The mutakallimun debated whether a capable agent can prefer one action over another without any reason. The first group firmly denies this possibility, insisting that a preponderating factor must be present. However, they add an important nuance: even when the factor is present, the action only becomes more likely, not necessary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first opinion
Read the first part of the passage where the author introduces the first group. Note their concession that preponderance without a factor is impossible.
2
Understand the nuance
Notice that they say the action becomes 'awlā bil-wuqū‘' (more likely) not wājib (obligatory). This distinguishes their view from determinism.
Contrast with the second opinion.
3
Reflect on the consequence
Ask yourself: what is the implication for human freedom and God's power? The first opinion preserves a form of contingency.
This connects to the broader debate on free will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن المتكلمين قولهم في هذا المقام قولان : منهم من سلم أن الرجحان بدون المرجح محال . إلا أنهم زعموا : أن عند حصول المرجح يصير الفعل أولى بالوقوع ، إلا أن تلك الأولوية ، لا تنتهي إلى حد الوجوب . ومنهم من قال : إن الرجحان بدون المرجح في حق القادر غير ممتنع ، وضربوا لذلك أمثلة : منها المخير بين شرب القدحين ، ومنها المخير بين أكل الرغيفين ، ومنها الهارب من السبع إذا وصل إلى مشعب الطريقين ، فإنه يختار أحدهما دون الآخر ، لا لمرجح . قالوا : ولا بد مهنا من الاعتراف بإمكان أن يصدر عن القادر أحد مقدوريه ، دون الآخر لا المرجح . إذ لو وقفنا به على انضمام المرجح إليه وقد ثبت أن عند انضمام المرجح إليه يصير واجب الوقوع فحينئذ يلزم أن لا يبقى فرق بين الموجب وبين القادر ، لكن العلم بهذا الفرق ضروري ، فوجب الاعتراف بان القادر يمكنه ترجيح أحد مقدوريه على الآخر من غير مرجع .
And know that the mutakallimun have two sayings in this station: Among them are those who conceded that preponderance without a preponderating factor is impossible, but they claimed that when the preponderating factor occurs, the action becomes more likely to occur, except that this likelihood does not reach the level of obligation. And among them are those who said that preponderance without a preponderating factor regarding the capable is not impossible, and they gave examples of that: from them the one given a choice between drinking two cups, and from them the one given a choice between eating two loaves, and from them the one fleeing from a predator when he reaches a fork in the two roads, for he chooses one of them over the other, not due to a preponderating factor. They said: And necessarily here there must be acknowledgment of the possibility that from the capable one of his two possible objects may issue, without the other, not due to a preponderating factor. For if we make it dependent upon the addition of the preponderating factor to it, and it has been established that when the preponderating factor is added to it it becomes necessary of occurrence, then it follows that there remains no difference between the necessitating cause and the capable, but knowledge of this difference is necessary, so acknowledgment is necessary that the capable can preponderate one of his two possible objects over the other without a preponderating factor.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage presents two opinions among the mutakallimun (theologians) regarding the issue of preponderance (tarjīḥ) of one action over another by a capable agent (qādir). The first group concedes that preponderance without a preponderating factor (murajjiḥ) is impossible, but they claim that when a preponderating factor is present, the action becomes more likely (awlā) to occur, yet not to the point of necessity (wujūb). The second group holds that preponderance without a preponderating factor is not impossible for the capable agent. They give examples: a person choosing between two cups of drink, two loaves of bread, or a fleeing person at a fork in the road. In each case, the agent chooses one option without a discernible preponderating factor. They argue that if we insist on a preponderating factor, then the action would become necessary once it is present, eliminating the distinction between a necessitating cause (mūjib) and a capable agent. Since this distinction is known necessarily, we must admit that the capable can give preponderance to one possible act over another without a preponderating factor.

مرجح
وجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if a preponderating factor is present, the action must occur necessarily.
Correction: The first opinion explicitly states that the action only becomes more likely, not obligatory. Thus, even with a factor, the agent can still choose otherwise, preserving contingency.

Checkpoint
According to the first opinion, what happens when a preponderating factor is present?
A) The action becomes necessary.
B) The action becomes more likely but not necessary.
C) The action becomes impossible.
D) The action remains purely possible.

Key Takeaway
The first opinion holds that preponderance requires a factor, but even then, the outcome is only more likely, not forced.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If preponderance without a factor is impossible, then every inclination you have has a cause. Are you aware of what factors are tilting your heart? Is the most weighty factor the truth, or your desires? Will you examine your heart today and remove the factors that lead you away from Allah?
?
The mutakallimun conceded that a factor makes an action more likely, but not necessary. How does this truth challenge the way you blame others or yourself for sins? When you sin, do you claim you were forced, or do you recognize that you could have chosen otherwise? Will you take full responsibility for your next sin and repent sincerely?

The Second Opinion: Preponderance Without a Factor Is Possible
Author states the second opinion with examples and argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second opinion that a capable agent can choose one of two possible acts without any preponderating factor, with supporting examples and argument.

Core Idea
Choice can occur without a reason – examples from everyday life.

The second group of mutakallimun offers a different perspective. They argue that preponderance without a preponderating factor is not impossible for a capable agent. To support their view, they present concrete examples from human experience, such as choosing between two identical cups of water. They then provide a philosophical argument: if we insisted on a preponderating factor, then once it is present the action would become necessary, eliminating the distinction between a necessitating cause and a free agent. Since we know this distinction is necessary, we must admit that preponderance without a factor is possible.

Guided Path
1
Examine the examples
Read the examples: choosing between two cups, two loaves, or a fleeing person at a fork. Notice that the agent chooses without any apparent reason.
These are classic examples in kalam.
2
Understand the argument
The author presents a logical argument: if preponderance required a factor, then with the factor the action becomes necessary, making the agent like a necessitating cause. But we know the difference between a free agent and a necessitating cause is necessary knowledge.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Therefore, they conclude, the capable agent can give preponderance to one possible act over another without any preponderating factor.
Consider how this supports free will.

Source Text

The Only Two Possible Answers
Author concludes that only two opinions can answer the objection, reducing to two premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection can only be answered by denying one of two premises, and how these two denials correspond to two known opinions.

Core Idea
The objection is unanswerable unless you deny one of its two premises.

In this passage, the author wraps up a long discussion by showing that only two logical paths can avoid the force of the objection. Both paths involve rejecting one of two key premises. Understanding this conclusion helps you see the structure of the entire argument and why earlier discussions were necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's conclusion come through before interpreting.
Notice the repeated phrase 'لا مزيد' (no exceeding) which emphasizes finality.
2
Identify the two premises that would seal the objection
The passage says that if two premises are accepted, the question is complete and unanswerable. Find those two premises: (1) the capable agent needs a specifying factor, (2) upon addition of that factor, the action becomes obligatory.
These premises are introduced with 'إن ثبت أن' and 'وثبت أن'.
3
Understand the only escape: deny one premise
The author states that the objection cannot be repelled except by disputing one of the two premises. The two denials are then presented as the only two answers.
Each denial corresponds to a known opinion among theologians.
4
Relate to earlier discussions
The author notes that both answers have been thoroughly investigated earlier, so no repetition is needed. This shows the passage is a conclusion, not a new argument.
Also note the reference to the necessary distinction between the capable and the necessitating cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن هذين القولين لا مزيد عليهما في الجواب عن ذلك السؤال . لأنه إن ثبت أن القادر لا يصدر عنه الأثر المعين إلا عند إنضمام المخصص إليه . وثبت أن عند إنضمام المخصص إليه يجب الفعل ، فحينئذ يتم السؤال ، ولا يبقى عنه جواب البتة، فيثبت أنه لا يمكن دفعه إلا بواسطة النزاع في إحدى المقدمتين ، إما أن يقال إن صدور الفعل من القادر لا يتوقف على إنضمام المخصص إليه ، وهو قول من يقول : صدور الفعل عن القادر لا يتوقف على إنضمام الداعي إليه. وإما أن يقال : إنه وإن توقف على إنضمام الداعي إليه ، إلا أنه لا يصير واجب الوقوع عند حصول ذلك ، فيثبت أنه لا مزيد على هذين الجوابين . إلا أن البحث عن كل واحد منهما قد سبق على الاستقصاء والاستيفاء فلا حاجة إلى الإعادة . وقوله : لو لم نجوز ذلك ، لزم أن لا يبقى فرق بين القادر وبين أن هذا الفرق معلوم بالضرورة. فهذا الكلام أيضاً قد سبق البحث فيه فلا حاجة إلى الإعادة
And know that these two sayings are not exceeded in answering that question. Because if it is established that the capable does not produce the specific effect except upon the addition of the specifying factor to it, and it is established that upon the addition of the specifying factor to it the action becomes obligatory, then the question is complete, and no answer remains for it at all, so it is established that it cannot be repelled except by disputing one of the two premises: either it is said that the occurrence of the action from the capable does not depend on the addition of the specifying factor to it, and that is the saying of one who says: the occurrence of the action from the capable does not depend on the addition of the motivator to it; or it is said that although it depends on the addition of the motivator to it, yet it does not become necessary of occurrence when that occurs. So it is established that there is no exceeding these two answers, except that the investigation of each of them has preceded with thoroughness and completeness, so there is no need for repetition. And his saying: 'If we do not allow that, it would follow that no difference remains between the capable and [the necessitating cause], and this difference is known necessarily.' So this speech also has previously been researched, so there is no need for repetition.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion on the two opinions. The author states that these two opinions are the only possible answers to the question posed. The question is unanswerable if both premises are accepted: (1) the capable agent produces a specific effect only when a specifying factor (mukhaṣṣiṣ) is added, and (2) when that factor is added, the action becomes necessary. To escape the conclusion, one must deny one of these premises. The first premise is denied by those who say the action does not depend on a specifying factor (or motivator, dā‘ī). The second premise is denied by those who say that even with the factor, the action does not become necessary. The author notes that each of these answers has been thoroughly investigated earlier and need not be repeated. He also references a previous statement about the necessary distinction between the capable and the necessitating cause, which has also been discussed.

Key Terms
مخصص
داعي
مقدمة
وجوب
قادر
موجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the two opinions are two separate positive doctrines that directly answer the question.
Correction: They are not positive doctrines; they are merely the only two ways to reject one of the premises that make the objection unanswerable. They are defensive, not constructive.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is required to answer the question if both original premises are accepted?
A) Accept both premises and find a third opinion.
B) Deny one of the two premises.
C) Prove that the specifying factor is not necessary.
D) Show that the action does not become obligatory.

Key Takeaway
The only way to answer the objection is to deny either that the agent needs a specifying factor or that the action becomes necessary when the factor is present.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Which of your own assumptions might you be holding uncritically that leaves you vulnerable to such objections? Commit to examining one premise this week.
?
The difference between a capable agent (you) and a necessitating cause (a machine) is, the author says, known necessarily. In your daily actions, do you live as if your choices are determined by motives, or do you feel the reality of your freedom? When was the last time you acted against a strong desire and felt your capability? That moment is a proof of this distinction—do you honor it?
?
This passage reduces a complex debate to two simple alternatives. In your own life, you often face complex decisions. Are you honest enough to reduce your own excuses to their premises? What is one habit or sin you justify by saying 'I couldn't help it'? That is an implicit denial of your capability. Confess it and ask Allah to forgive your misuse of His gift of power.

The Impossibility of Power Being Acquired at the Time of Action
Razi refutes the second and third divisions of acquired power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that power cannot be acquired at the time of the action or after it, based on the impossibility of combining existence and non-existence.

Core Idea
Power over an action cannot be acquired at the moment of action, for that would require the impossible.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi continues his argument against the view that the power to act is acquired only at the time of action. He has already refuted the idea that power comes before the action; now he turns to the remaining possibilities: that it comes at the time of the action, or after it. By examining these, he shows that both lead to contradictions or are obviously absurd.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Notice how Razi divides the argument into three possibilities and systematically refutes the second and third.
The first possibility (before the action) is not included in this passage.
2
Identify the second division
Find where Razi addresses the claim that power (or correctness) is obtained at the time of the action. Read his reasoning: why does he say it is impossible?
Look for the argument about the combination of two contradictories.
3
Understand the third division
Now consider the third division: power after the action. Razi says this is obviously false by necessity and intuition. Ask yourself why no rational person would say this.
4
Draw the conclusion
Razi concludes that since all three divisions are false, the claim that power is an acquired attribute is false. Reflect on the significance of this for divine attributes.
This argument supports the Ash'ari position that power is an eternal attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني ( وهذا يرجع حاصله إلى أن القدرة على الفعل إنما تحصل حال حصول الفعل ) وهذا يقدح في قولنا : إن صحة الفعل إنما تحصل حال حضور الفعل. وأما القسم الثاني وهو أن هذه الصحة إنما تحصل حال حصول الفعل فنقول : هذا أيضاً محال . وذلك لأن حال وجود الفعل يكون عدمه محالاً ، لأن الجمع بين النقيضين محال ، وما كان محالاً لعينه ولذاته امتنع كون القادر قادراً عليه . فيثبت أن الجمع بين صحة أن يوجد وأن لا يوجد ، وبين كونه موجوداً جمع بين النقيضين . وذلك محال . فيثبت أن حصول هذه الصحة حال حصول الفعل محال . وأما القسم الثالث وهو أن هذه الصحة إنما تحصل بعد وجود الفعل ، فهذا قول معلوم البطلان بالضرورة والبديهة ولم يقل به أحد العقلاء . فثبت أن القادر بمعنى كونه محال يصح منه أن يوجد وأن لا يوجد ، لو حصل لحصلت هذه الصحة . إما قبل وجود الفعل ، وإما مع وجوده واما بعده وثبت أن الأقسام الثلاثة باطلة فكان القول بحصول هذه الصحة باطلاً
The second (and its outcome is that power over an action is only obtained at the time of the occurrence of the action) and this contradicts our statement: that the correctness of the action is only obtained at the time of the presence of the action. As for the second division—that this correctness is only obtained at the time of the occurrence of the action—we say: this is also impossible. That is because at the time of the existence of the action, its non-existence is impossible, because the combination of two contradictories is impossible, and whatever is impossible in itself and by its essence prevents the powerful being from being powerful over it. So it is established that combining the correctness of existence and non-existence with its being existent is a combination of two contradictories, and that is impossible. So it is established that the occurrence of this correctness at the time of the occurrence of the action is impossible. As for the third division—that this correctness is only obtained after the existence of the action—this is a statement known to be false necessarily and intuitively, and no rational person has said it. So it is established that if the powerful being in the sense of being such that it is permissible for it to exist or not exist were to obtain, this correctness would have obtained either before the existence of the action, or with its existence, or after it; and it is established that the three divisions are false, so the statement that this correctness is obtained is false.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage is an argument refuting the idea that the power (qudra) to act is obtained only at the time of the action. The author, likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, examines three possibilities for when the 'correctness' (siha) of an action—meaning the power to do it—could be obtained: before the action, at the time of the action, or after it. He already considered the first (before the action) earlier, and here he treats the second and third. For the second (at the time of action), he argues it is impossible because at the moment the action exists, its non-existence is impossible (since something cannot both exist and not exist). Since the power is the ability to both do and not do, attributing it to an existing action would require combining existence and non-existence, which is contradictory. For the third (after the action), he says it is obviously false by necessity and intuition, and no rational person ever claimed it. Thus all three possibilities are false, proving that such a correctness (power) is not obtained at all in the way the opponent claims.

Key Terms
قدرة
صحة
فعل
وجود
عدم
جمع بين النقيضين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power over an action is acquired at the moment the action occurs.
Correction: Razi shows that this leads to a contradiction because at the moment of action, the non-existence is impossible, so power as ability to do or not do cannot be present.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Razi claim that the view that power is obtained at the time of action is impossible?
A) Because it would require the action to be both existent and non-existent simultaneously.
B) Because power is an eternal attribute of God.
C) Because it would imply that God's power is acquired.
D) Because the action does not exist before its time.

Key Takeaway
Power cannot be acquired at the time of action or after it; therefore, it must be an eternal attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever rely on your own ability at the moment of action, forgetting that true power belongs to Allah alone? How does Razi's argument that power cannot be acquired at the time of action challenge your assumption that your own power is the cause of your actions? This week, what is one action you will consciously attribute to Allah's power rather than your own?
?
If power is an eternal attribute of Allah, not something you acquire as you act, what does that mean for your constant effort to be 'in control'? Identify one area of your life where you are desperately trying to be capable at the moment of action—and surrender it to the eternal power of Allah.
