The Dilemma of Possibility and the Need for a Determinant
Sanusi refutes an objection using a dilemma argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that God's power can create without a determinant leads to the collapse of the rational proof for God's existence.

Core Idea
Possibility without a determinant destroys the proof of a Creator.

This passage presents a powerful refutation of a key objection: if God's power could create without any reason, then the inference from the world's possibility to a Creator becomes invalid. Sanusi exposes the dilemma that the opponent faces and shows why their position is self-defeating.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the opponent claims divine power attaches on the basis of possibility, not obligation.
The opponent wants to avoid needing a determinant.
2
Identify the dilemma's two branches
Sanusi presents two options: possibility either requires a necessitating factor or it does not. Find these two branches in the text.
This is a classic conditional argument.
3
Analyze the first branch's consequence
If possibility requires a determinant, then the determinant is necessary, contradicting the opponent's claim that no determinant is needed.
4
Analyze the second branch's consequence
If possibility does not require a determinant, then the world's possibility cannot be used to infer a Creator, undermining the entire proof.
Thus both options refute the opponent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مرجع وقع على سبيل الوجوب ، وحينئذ يصير المؤثر موجباً . وذلك يقدح في قولهم : إنه على سبيل الصحة لا على سبيل الوجوب . فإن قالوا : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه تعلقت قدرته باقتضاء وجود العالم تعلقاً على سبيل الجواز ، لا على سبيل الوجوب . ومع ذلك فإنه لا يفتقر إلى المرجح ؟ فنقول : هذا قول بأن الجائز لا يتوقف رجحان حصوله على عدمه على حصول المرجح . ولما جوزتم ذلك ، فحينئذ ينسد عليكم الاستدلال بجواز العالم وبحدوثه على افتقاره إلى الصانع ، وكل فرع أفضى إلى بطلان الأصل كان باطلاً . والحاصل أن ( الجواز إما أن ( (١) يحوج إلى المقتضي أو لا يحوج إليه . فإن أحوج إلى المقتضي فاينها حصل وجب حصول الاقتضاء إلى المقتضى . فبطل قولهم : إن تعلق قدرة الله بأحداث العالم وقع على سبيل الصحة والجواز ، مع أنه لا يفتقر إلى المقتضى . وإن قلنا : إن الجواز من حيث هو جواز لا يحوج إلى ضى ، فحينئذ لا يلزم من كون العالم جائز الوجود افتقاره إلى المقتضى وذلك يسد باب الاستدلال بحدوث العالم ، أو بإمكانه على افتقاره إلى المؤثر . فهذا تمام الكلام في تقرير هذا البحث .
Returned occurs on the basis of obligation, and then the effect becomes necessitating. And that undermines their statement: that it is on the basis of validity, not obligation. For if they say: Why may it not be said that His power is attached to necessitating the existence of the world in an attachment on the basis of possibility, not obligation, and yet it does not need a determinant? We say: This is a saying that the possible does not depend for its preponderance of occurrence over non-occurrence on the occurrence of the determinant. And when you permitted that, then the door of inference from the possibility of the world and its temporal origination to its need for the Maker is blocked for you. And every branch that leads to the invalidity of the root is invalid. The summary is that possibility either (1) requires a necessitating factor or does not require it. If it requires a necessitating factor, then wherever it occurs, the necessitation of the necessitated necessarily occurs. Thus their statement is invalid: that the attachment of God's power to the origination of the world occurred on the basis of validity and possibility, while it does not need the necessitating factor. And if we say: possibility as such does not require anything, then it does not follow from the world being possible of existence that it needs a necessitating factor, and that blocks the door of inference from the temporal origination of the world, or its possibility, to its need for an effect. So this is the complete speech in establishing this discussion.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The opponent claims that God's power attaches to creating the world on the basis of possibility, not obligation, so no determinant is needed. The author refutes by showing that possibility either requires a determinant or it does not. If it requires a determinant, then the determinant is needed, contradicting the opponent. If it does not require a determinant, then the inference from the world's possibility to a creator is invalid. Thus both options lead to unacceptable consequences, proving the opponent's position untenable.

Correction: This passage shows that possibility without a determinant either contradicts the opponent's own position or blocks the inference to a Creator, making the concept untenable.

The refutation shows that the opponent's position is logically untenable because either possibility requires a determinant (contradicting themselves) or it does not (undermining the proof for God).

Why a Capable Agent Cannot Be Capable of Leaving
Al-Taftazani presents two proofs that non-existence cannot be an object of power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two rational proofs that a capable agent cannot be capable of leaving (non-existence), and why power relates only to existence.

Core Idea
Power is an effective attribute; it cannot be attached to pure non-existence.

This lesson presents a crucial argument in Islamic theology about the scope of divine power. The author argues that if Allah is truly capable, He cannot be capable of 'leaving' something undone in the sense of remaining in non-existence. The argument relies on two logical principles about the nature of power and non-existence. We will unpack each reason and see the conclusion that power is only related to existence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the main claim
Read the opening statement: 'That a capable agent being capable of leaving is impossible.' Note that 'leaving' here means remaining on original non-existence.
This sets the stage for the two proofs.
2
Study the first proof
Power is an effective attribute, non-existence is pure negation. Attributing pure negation to an effective attribute is impossible. Understand each term.
This is a direct incompatibility of natures.
3
Study the second proof
Non-existence while it remains cannot be attributed to an effect (mu'aththir), because that would entail tahsil al-hasil (obtaining what is already obtained), which is impossible.
This is a logical reductio ad absurdum.
4
Follow the conclusion
From both proofs, it is established that attributing continuous non-existence to a capable agent is impossible. Therefore, the capable agent cannot be capable of leaving.
The conclusion directly follows from the two proofs.
5
Reflect on the implication
The passage ends by noting that if the capable agent has attachment only to existence, there remains no difference between him and a necessitating agent (mujib). Consider why this is a significant point.
This sets up a further discussion on determinism vs. free will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : إن كون القادر قادراً على الترك محال . لأن المراد ههنا بالترك بقاؤه على عدمه الأصلي. والعدم الأصلي محال أن يكون مقدوراً لوجهين : الأول : أن القدرة صفة مؤثرة ، والعدم نفي محض . فإسناد النفي المحض إلى القدرة التي هي صفة مؤثرة محال . والثاني : إن العدم حال بقائه ، لا يمكن إسناده إلى المؤثر، لأنا لو أسندنا الباقي إلى المؤثر ، لزم تكوين الكائن وتحصيل الحاصل، وهو محال . فيثبت بهذين الوجهين أن إسناد العلم المستمر إلى القادر : محال . وإذا ثبت هذا امتنع كون القادر قادراً على الترك . وإذا ثبت أن القادر لا تعلق له إلا بجانب الوجود ، وأما جانب العدم فلا تعلق له به ، وإذا كان القادر لا صلاحية له إلا في التأثير في جانب الوجود بعينه ، فحينئذ لا يبقى بينه وبين الموجب فرق . وتمام تقرير هذا الكلام قد سبق في أول الباب الأول
The sixth proof: That a capable agent (qadir) being capable of leaving (tark) is impossible. Because what is meant here by leaving is his remaining upon his original non-existence. And original non-existence is impossible to be an object of power (maqdur) for two reasons: First: That power (qudra) is an effective attribute (sifa mu'aththira), and non-existence (adam) is pure negation (nafy mahd). So attributing pure negation to power, which is an effective attribute, is impossible. Second: That non-existence while it remains, its attribution to the effect (mu'aththir) is impossible, because if we attribute that which remains to the effect, it would entail the creation of what already exists (takwin al-ka'in) and the acquisition of what is already obtained (tahsīl al-hasil), which is impossible. Thus it is established by these two points that attributing continuous non-existence (al-‘adam al-mustamirr) to the capable agent is impossible. And when this is established, it becomes impossible for the capable agent to be capable of leaving. And when it is established that the capable agent has attachment only to the side of existence, and as for the side of non-existence, he has no attachment to it, and when the capable agent has no aptitude except for effecting the side of existence specifically, then there remains no difference between him and the necessitating agent (al-mujib). And the complete exposition of this statement has already preceded in the beginning of the first chapter.
Source pages p. 85

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A capable agent must be equally capable of creating and leaving (non-existence).
Correction: The passage argues that non-existence is pure negation, so power, being an effective attribute, cannot relate to it. Hence, 'capable of leaving' is not a genuine capability.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why can't non-existence be attributed to power?
A) Because power is an eternal attribute.
B) Because non-existence is pure negation.
C) Because non-existence is created.
D) Because power only acts on existence.

Key Takeaway
Power, as an effective attribute, cannot be directed toward pure non-existence; thus, a capable agent's power is confined to the realm of existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The passage concludes that when power is confined to existence, there is no difference between a capable agent and a necessitating agent. Does this challenge your understanding of God's free will? How do you reconcile this with your own sense of freedom and responsibility before Him?

Refuting the Objection: The Difference Between Capable and Incapable
Refuting the claim that necessary knowledge of the difference between qadir and 'ajiz disproves the argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the intuitive distinction between capable and incapable does not imply that the capable agent has the power to leave an action.

Core Idea
The difference between capable and incapable is about the side of existence, not non-existence.

This passage addresses an objection that relies on our necessary knowledge of the difference between a capable agent and an incapable one. The objection claims this knowledge proves that the capable agent can choose not to act. The author refutes this by showing that the distinction only concerns the occurrence of action when motives are present, not the ability to refrain.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage where the objector claims necessary knowledge of the difference between capable and incapable.
The objector asserts that the incapable cannot avoid action, while the capable can.
2
Identify the refutation's starting point
Notice that the author says the answer is weak and begins to explain why.
The refutation aims to show the flaw in the objection's reasoning.
3
Understand the causal explanation
The refutation explains that action follows only when motive and power are combined; otherwise, no action occurs.
This highlights that the difference is about the conditions for action, not about a power to refrain.
4
See the conclusion
The difference is about the side of existence, so it does not prove capability of leaving.
The conclusion directly counters the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحاول بعضهم أن يجيب عن هذا الكلام . فقال : نحن نعلم بالضرورة أن العاجز لا يجد من نفسه أنه إن شاء لا يفعل أمكنه أن لا يفعل . أما القادر على الفعل فإنه يجد من نفسه أنه إذا شاء أن لا يفعل فإنه يمكنه أن لا يفعل وهذا الفرق معلوم بالضرورة. ولما كان هذا الفرق معلوماً بالضرورة ، لم يكن بنا حاجة إلى بيان كيفية هذا الفرق. ولقائل أن يقول : هذا الجواب ضعيف . وبيانه : أنا نجد من أنفسنا أنه إذا حصل الداعي إلى الفعل ، فإنه يترتب على مجموع القدرة ( مع تلك الداعية الخاصة المعينة ((۱) حصول ذلك الفعل المعين (٢) ( وإذا لم يوجد ذلك المجموع . إما لأنه فقد الداعية ، أو لم يقدر بالقدرة (۳) فحينئذ يبقى ذلك العلم المستمر ، كما كان . والفرق الذي نجده من أنفسنا بين القادر وبين العاجز : عائد إلى جانب الوجود ، وأن القادر موصوف بصفة إذا حصلت له الداعية المعينة إلى الإيجاد، فإنه يحصل الفعل . والعاجز ليس كذلك . فهذه التفرقة حاصلة بين القادر وبين العاجز . إلا أن هذه التفرقة عائدة إلى جانب الوجود ، لا إلى جانب العدم. فيثبت : أن الفرق الذي يجده كل عاقل من نفسه، لا يقتضي كون القادر ، قادراً على الترك .
And some attempted to answer this speech. They said: We know necessarily that the incapable person does not find in himself that if he wills not to act, he can not act. As for the capable agent, he finds in himself that if he wills not to act, then he can not act. And this difference is known necessarily. And since this difference is known necessarily, we have no need to explain the manner of this difference. And it can be said: This answer is weak. Its explanation is: We find in ourselves that when the motive (al-da'i) to the act occurs, then upon the totality of power (with that specific particular motive) the occurrence of that specific act follows. And when that totality does not exist — whether because the motive is absent, or because he is not with power — then that continuous non-existence remains as it was. And the difference we find in ourselves between the capable and the incapable is returning to the side of existence, and that the capable is described with an attribute such that when the specific motive to bringing about occurs for him, then the act occurs. And the incapable is not like that. So this distinction occurs between the capable and the incapable. However, this distinction returns to the side of existence, not to the side of non-existence. Thus it is established: That the difference which every rational person finds in himself does not necessitate that the capable agent be capable of leaving.
Source pages p. 86

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The intuitive distinction between capable and incapable proves that the capable agent has the power to refrain from acting.
Correction: The distinction only concerns the side of existence: the capable agent acts when conditions are met; the incapable does not. It does not imply a power over non-existence (the ability to leave).

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, what does the intuitive difference between capable and incapable actually relate to?
A) The ability to choose.
B) The side of existence.
C) The side of non-existence.
D) The freedom of the will.

Key Takeaway
The intuitive difference between capable and incapable does not prove capability of leaving; it only indicates that action occurs when conditions are met.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that what we think of as the 'ability to choose' may actually be merely the absence of a strong motive. In your own life, when you claim you 'can't' do something, is it truly a lack of ability, or just a lack of compelling motive? What would it take for you to cultivate the motive to act on what you know is right?
?
The incapable agent does not find in himself that he could avoid an act if he willed — because he lacks power. Ask Allah to give you both the da'i and the qudra to change.
?

Two Refutations of Mu'jib bi al-Dhat
Objection and two refutations of mu'jib bi al-dhat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mutakallimun refute the idea that God is a necessary cause (mu'jib bi al-dhat) through two distinct arguments based on the impossibility of an eternal world and the absurdity of infinite regress.

Core Idea
God acts by choice, not by necessity — an eternal world is impossible.

In this passage, an objector tries to salvage the notion of God as a necessary cause (mu'jib bi al-dhat) by appealing to the possibility of the world's pre-eternal possibility. The mutakallimun answer with two powerful refutations: the first combines the impossibility of an effect lagging behind its cause with the impossibility of an eternal world; the second shows that if God were a necessary cause, we would face an absurd infinite regress of creations. Read carefully to see how they dismantle the objection.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening hypothetical (fa'in qīla) carefully. The objector presents a dilemma: either the world was possible in pre-eternity (al-azal) or not. If possible, the world would be eternal; if impossible, the cause could still exist without effect because the recipient's state matters.
Notice the objector tries to use the recipient's possibility to allow a necessary cause without an eternal effect.
2
Analyze the first answer
The first refutation argues that both the effect's delay and the world's eternity are impossible. Since both cannot be true, the necessary cause cannot have existed in pre-eternity. Therefore, the eternal agent must be an agent by choice, not by necessity.
This answer establishes that the world's eternity is impossible, so any cause that would necessitate it cannot be present.
3
Analyze the second answer
The second refutation grants for argument's sake that pre-eternity might be an obstacle, but then shows that the obstacle is removed at the moment of creation. This leads to an infinite regress: for any time before creation, the world must have already existed, which is absurd.
This reductio demonstrates that mu'jib bi al-dhat leads to an infinite chain of prior creations.
4
Synthesize the conclusion
Both answers converge on the same point: God cannot be a necessary cause; He must be a free agent by choice (fa'il bi al-ikhtiyar). The objection fails because it cannot avoid the absurd consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: العالم إما أن يكون ممكن الوجود في الأزل، وإما أن لا يكون ممكن الوجود في الأزل. فإن كان ممكن الوجود في الأزل فحينئذ يلزم قدم العالم، وليس لكم أن تقولوا: إنه محال أن يكون العالم قديماً، لأن هذا التقدير هو تقدير أن كون العالم أزلياً، ليس بممتنع. وإن كان محال الوجود في الأزل. فنقول: على هذا التقدير، لم لا يجوز أن يقال: العلة القديمة لوجود العالم، وإن كانت موجودة في الأزل، لا يلزم منها كون العالم أزلياً، لأنه كما أن حال المؤثر معتبر في حصول الأثر، فكذلك حال القابل معتبر، وأولى الأحوال بالاعتبار كونه ممكناً في نفسه، فإذا كان الإمكان ثابتاً في الأزل، لم يلزم من حصول العلة الموجبة في الأزل حصول الأثر في الأزل؟ أجاب المتكلمون عنه من وجهين: الأول: أن نقول: تخلف المعلول عن العلة محال، وكون العالم قديماً أيضاً محال. وهذان المعنيان لما اجتمعا علمنا أن العلة الموجبة للعالم ما كانت موجودة في الأزل. وقد كان المؤثر الأزلي موجوداً في الأزل، فعلمنا أن ذلك المؤثر ما كان موجباً بالذات. بل كان فاعلاً بالاختيار. الوجه الثاني: في الجواب عن السؤال المذكور أن نقول: هب أن كونه أزلياً يمنع من كون الموجب موجباً له في الأزل، إلا أن هذا المانع زائل فيما لا يزال، فوجب أن يصدر العالم عنه فيما لا يزال. وإذا كان كذلك كان لحدوثه أول معين. ولا شك أنه لو حدث قبل ذلك الوقت بمائة سنة كان حادثاً أيضاً، فلا يصير بسبب هذا التقدير من الزيادة أزلياً. وعلى هذا فلا وقت إلا وكانت العلة الموجبة لوجود العالم موجودة قبله، وكان الأزل الذي هو العائق عن صدوره عنه زائلا قبله، فيلزم أن يقال: أنه لا وقت إلا ويجب أن يكون حادثاً قبله. وهذا المحال إنما لزم من فرض كونه تعالى موجباً بالذات.
If it is said: The world is either possible of existence in pre-eternity (al-azal), or it is not possible of existence in pre-eternity. If it is possible of existence in pre-eternity, then the eternity of the world follows, and you cannot say that it is impossible for the world to be eternal, because this supposition is a supposition that the world's being eternal is not impossible. And if it is impossible of existence in pre-eternity, we say: On this supposition, why may it not be said that the eternal cause of the world's existence, even if it existed in pre-eternity, does not necessarily entail that the world is eternal, because just as the state of the agent is considered in the occurrence of the effect, so likewise the state of the recipient is considered, and the most important state to consider is its being possible in itself. So if possibility was established in pre-eternity, it does not follow from the existence of the necessary cause in pre-eternity that the effect occurs in pre-eternity? The mutakallimun answered it in two ways: First: we say that the effect's lagging behind the cause is impossible, and the world's being eternal is also impossible. When these two meanings are combined, we know that the necessary cause of the world did not exist in pre-eternity. The eternal agent existed in pre-eternity, so we know that that agent was not necessary by essence (mu'jib bi al-dhat), but rather was an agent by choice (fa'il bi al-ikhtiyar). The second way in answering the aforementioned question: we say: Suppose that its being eternal prevents the necessary cause from effecting it in pre-eternity, yet this obstruction is removed for the future without end (fima la yazal), so necessarily the world should issue from Him for the future without end. If this is so, then its origination has a specific first moment. There is no doubt that if it had occurred one hundred years before that time, it would also be originated, and it does not become eternal by this increase of time. And on this, there is no time except that the necessary cause of the world's existence existed before it, and the pre-eternity which was the obstacle to its issuing from Him was removed before it, so it follows that there is no time except that it must have originated before it. This absurdity only follows from the supposition that the Exalted is necessary by essence.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is an objection and refutation regarding the possibility of God being mu'jib bi al-dhat. The objector argues: The world was either possible in pre-eternity (al-azal) or not. If it was possible, then the world would be eternal (since a necessary cause with all conditions met would produce its effect immediately). If it was impossible, then why can't we say that the eternal cause does not necessitate the world's existence because the recipient's state (being possible in itself) matters? The mutakallimun answer with two arguments. First: The effect lagging behind the cause is impossible, and the world's eternity is also impossible; since both cannot be true, the cause must not have been present in pre-eternity. The eternal agent (God) existed, so He cannot be a necessary cause; He acts by choice. Second: Even if pre-eternity was an obstacle, once that obstacle is removed (at the moment of creation), the world exists; this implies a specific first moment. But then one can ask: why at that moment and not earlier? If we push the moment back, it still has a first moment, never becoming eternal. This leads to an infinite regress of moments before which the world must have existed, which is absurd. Therefore, God cannot be mu'jib bi al-dhat.

Key Terms
ممكن الوجود
الأزل
علة موجبة
فاعل بالاختيار
المؤثر الأزلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if the world was possible in pre-eternity, then a necessary cause would indeed produce it eternally, but that would make the world eternal — which is acceptable.
Correction: The mutakallimun hold that the world's eternity is impossible (on theological grounds). The first answer uses this impossibility to deny that the necessary cause existed in pre-eternity, thereby rejecting mu'jib bi al-dhat.

Checkpoint
In the second answer (al-wajh al-thani), what absurdity does the author say follows from supposing God is mu'jib bi al-dhat?
A) The world becomes eternal.
B) The cause cannot exist in pre-eternity.
C) There is no time at which the world could have begun.
D) There must be an infinite number of creations before any given creation.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun's two refutations show that God cannot be a necessary cause (mu'jib bi al-dhat) because that would either require an eternal world or lead to an infinite regress of creations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God acts out of free will in every event of your life, or do you subconsciously think of Him as a sort of cosmic machine that must reward you if you obey and must punish you if you sin? What would change in your relationship with Allah if you constantly reminded yourself that His actions are by choice, not by necessity?
?
The mutakallimun proved that God cannot be a necessary cause because then the world would be eternal. But consider your own assumptions: Have you ever felt entitled to something from God—expecting that because you did X, He automatically owes you Y? How does the truth that God acts by choice, not compulsion, challenge that sense of entitlement?
?
Imagine if God were 'mu'jib bi al-dhat'—your destiny would be fixed without any room for grace or mercy. Now that you know He is 'fa'il bi al-ikhtiyar', how does this reaffirm your hope in His mercy? What specific sin or bad habit have you given up on because you thought God's forgiveness was 'automatic' or 'impossible'? Will you now turn to Him with that, trusting His free choice to forgive?

Second Proof: Similarity of Bodies and Need for a Particularizer
The second proof argues from the similarity of bodies to their differing attributes, requiring a free agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the second kalam proof for God as an agent by choice, based on the similarity of bodies and the need for an external particularizer.

Core Idea
Similar bodies with different attributes point to an external chooser.

This lesson presents the beginning of the second proof that God acts by choice, not by necessity. The argument starts from a key premise: all bodies are essentially alike. Yet they differ in attributes and locations. Since what is common cannot cause what is different, something external must specify each body. The mutakallimun then examine four possible sources for this specification and refute the first three, leading toward the conclusion that only a free agent remains. The passage here covers the first part of this reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
Read the opening statement: all bodies are similar in their complete essence. This is a foundational claim for the argument.
This is a rational claim, not an empirical one.
2
Identify the logical necessity
Notice that since bodies are similar, their differing attributes must be contingent and require a particularizer. This follows from the principle that like things have like judgments.
3
Examine the four divisions
The author lists four possibilities for the particularizer: the body's essence, a locus, an accident, or something external. Follow the refutation of the first three.
4
Recognize the incomplete argument
The text cuts off after refuting the third possibility. Understand that the fourth option (an external free agent) is the intended conclusion, but it is not fully stated here.
The argument continues in subsequent pages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية للمتكلمين في إثبات كونه تعالى فاعلا بالاختيار، لا موجباً بالذات: هي أن نقول: قد دللنا على أن الأجسام بأسرها متماثلة في تمام الماهية، فاختصاص كل واحد منها بصفته المعينة وحيزه المعين، لا بد وأن يكون من الجائزات. لما ثبت أن حكم الشيء يساوي حكم مثله، وكل جائز فلا بد له من مرجح. وذلك المرجح إما أن يكون ذات الجسم أو غيره. وذلك الغير إما أن يكون محلاً للجسم، أو ما يكون حالاً فيه، أو ما لا يكون محلاً له، أو لا حالاً فيه، والأقسام الأربعة باطلة. أما القسم الأول: وهو أن يكون المقتضي لتلك الأحوال والصفات ذات الجسم. فهذا باطل. لأن ذوات الأجسام متماثلة، وصفاتها مختلفة، وما به الاشتراك لا يكون علة لما به الامتياز. والقسم الثاني باطل. لأنا دللنا على أن الجسم ذات قائمة بنفسها، فيمتنع أن يكون لها محل. والقسم الثالث أيضاً باطل. لأن الأجسام كما أنها مختلفة في هذه الصفات المحسوسة فهي أيضاً مختلفة في الصفات المقتضية لهذه الصفات المحسوسة، فهي لا بد وأن تكون لصفات أخرى. ويلزم التسلسل. ثم
The second argument of the mutakallimun in proving that the Exalted is an agent by choice (fa'il bi al-ikhtiyar), not necessary by essence (mu'jib bi al-dhat), is: we say: We have demonstrated that all bodies are similar in the entirety of their essence (mahiyyah). Therefore, the specification of each one with its particular attribute and its particular place must be among contingent things (al-ja'izat). Since it is established that the judgment of a thing equals the judgment of its like, and every contingent must have a particularizer (murajjih). That particularizer is either the essence of the body or something else. That something else is either a locus (mahall) for the body, or something that inheres in it (hall), or something that is neither a locus nor an inhering accident. The four divisions are invalid. As for the first division: that which necessitates those states and attributes is the essence of the body. This is invalid, because the essences of bodies are similar, and their attributes are different, and that by which there is commonality cannot be a cause of that by which there is distinction. The second division is invalid, because we have demonstrated that the body is a self-subsistent essence, so it is impossible for it to have a locus. The third division is also invalid, because bodies, just as they differ in these sensible attributes, also differ in the attributes necessitating these sensible attributes, so they must be due to other attributes, and an infinite regress (tasalsul) follows. Then
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is the second proof that God acts by choice, not by necessity. The argument proceeds from the observation that all bodies are essentially similar (mumathala) in their complete essence (mahiyyah). Yet each body is distinguished by specific attributes (e.g., temperature, color, motion) and a specific place. Since the essence alone is common, it cannot be the cause of the differences. Therefore, the particularizer (murajjih) that specifies each body must be external to the body. The author then examines four possibilities for that external factor: (1) the body's own essence (already ruled out), (2) a locus for the body (impossible because body is self-subsistent and cannot be in something else), (3) an accident inhering in the body (this leads to an infinite regress because the accidents themselves are different and would require further particularizers), (4) something that is neither a locus nor an accident (i.e., a separate particularizer, which must be a free agent). The argument is left incomplete in the window, but the structure is clear: after refuting the first three, only the fourth option remains, which is God acting by choice.

Key Terms

الماهية
الجائزات
مرجح
محل
حال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument depends on empirical observation of bodies.
Correction: The argument is purely rational. It relies on the principle that like things have like judgments, not on empirical data. The premise of similarity of essences is a rational claim established elsewhere in kalam.

Checkpoint
Why does the author rule out the possibility that the essence of the body itself is the particularizer of its attributes?
A) Because bodies are different in essence.
B) Because the essence is common to all bodies, but the attributes differ.
C) Because the essence is not self-subsistent.
D) Because the body cannot have a locus.

Key Takeaway
The second proof uses the similarity of bodies to show that their differences require an external particularizer, which must be a free agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that every attribute you have – your height, skin color, talents – is contingent and requires an external chooser. Do you truly live as if your unique traits are gifts from Allah, or do you secretly attribute them to your own merit? What would change if you fully internalized that they could have been otherwise?
?
The argument shows that differences between similar bodies require a free agent. Look at your own life: where have you been claiming that your circumstances are 'just the way things are' (as if necessary), when in fact they are contingent and chosen for you by Allah? Will you now thank Him specifically for those aspects?
?
If every contingent thing needs a particularizer, then your very existence and every action you take is dependent on Allah's choice. Are you comfortable with that dependence, or do you resist it? Take a moment to sincerely ask yourself: Are you submitting to His choice in how you live this day?

Three Faces for Self-Presence and Critique of the First
Presentation of three arguments for self-presence and refutation of the first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three arguments for self-presence and why the first argument is considered weak by the author.

Core Idea
Self-presence does not require distinction—otherwise, absurdities follow.

In this passage, the author presents three possible arguments (wujuh) for the doctrine that a thing can be present to itself. After outlining them, he critiques the first argument as extremely weak. Understanding this critique is key to grasping the epistemology of self-knowledge in Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the passage carefully to see the three faces and the critique. Pay attention to the author's own words.
2
Identify the three faces
The text presents three arguments: the first is truncated, but the second and third are clearly stated. What are they?
The first face seems to concern essence, the second individuality, and the third the composite.
3
Understand the critique of the first face
The author argues that the concept of 'presence' is more general than 'presence to something else', so the first face's reasoning is invalid. It would lead to absurdities like a thing being a cause of itself.
4
Appreciate the strength of the second and third faces
The second face involves partial distinction (between composite and part), which suffices for a relation. The third face relies on common linguistic usage, which people accept.
Consensus on 'my self' and 'your self' supports the possibility.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this matters for self-knowledge. The critique shows that self-presence does not require a distinct subject and object in the way the first face assumed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الماهية. والثاني: تلك الشخصية. والثالث: المجموع الحاصل من الماهية والشخصية. وإذا ثبت هذا، فقد حصل التغاير بين المجموع وبين أحد أجزائه، فلم يبعد أن يقال: إنه حضرت تلك الماهية عند ذلك الشخص؟ الثالث: إن كل أحد يعلم بالضرورة صحة قوله: ذاتي كذا وكذا وذاتك كذا وكذا. فيضيف ذاته إلى نفسه. وذلك يدل على صحة إضافة الشيء إلى نفسه، وإذا صح هذا لم يمتنع أن يقال: إن هذه الذات حضرت عند نفسها. وهذه الوجوه أقصى ما يمكن أن يقال في تقرير هذا الكلام. واعلم أن الوجه الأول في غاية الضعف. لأن قولنا: حضور الشيء عند الشيء أعم من قولنا: حضوره عند شيء يغايره. وكذلك كون الشيء محركاً للشيء ومؤثراً في الشيء أعم من كونه محركاً لشيء غيره، ومن كونه مؤثراً في غيره. فإن صح ما ذكرتم لزم جواز كون الشيء محركاً لنفسه ومؤثراً في نفسه، وعلة لنفسه. وإن لم يلزم صحة هذا، فكذلك لا يلزم صحة ما ذكرتم. وأما الوجه الثاني فهو أحسن من الوجه الأول، لأنه قد حصل التغاير فيه من بعض الوجوه، وحصول التغاير بوجه ما، يكفي في حسن الإضافة، وفي إمكانها. ثم تأكد هذا المعقول بالوجه الثالث، وهو إطباق الناس على صحة إضافة الذات إلى نفسها، حيث قالوا: ذاتي وذاتك. فهذا تمام الكلام في هذا السؤال.
The essence. And the second: that individuality. And the third: the composite resulting from the essence and the individuality. And if this is established, then there is a distinction between the composite and one of its parts, so it is not far-fetched to say: that essence is present to that person. The third: Everyone knows necessarily the correctness of his saying: 'my self is such and such' and 'your self is such and such'. So he relates his essence to himself. And that indicates the correctness of relating a thing to itself, and if this is correct, it is not impossible to say: this essence is present to itself. And these faces are the utmost that can be said in establishing this statement. Know that the first face is extremely weak. Because our saying: 'the presence of a thing to a thing' is more general than our saying: 'its presence to a thing different from it'. And likewise, a thing being a mover of a thing and an effector in a thing is more general than its being a mover of something else and an effector in something else. If what you mentioned were valid, it would necessitate the permissibility of a thing being a mover of itself and an effector in itself and a cause of itself. And if this necessity does not hold, then likewise what you mentioned does not hold. As for the second face, it is better than the first, because there is a distinction in it from some aspects, and the occurrence of distinction in some way suffices for the goodness of the relation and its possibility. Then this rational point is strengthened by the third face, which is the consensus of people on the correctness of relating the essence to itself, when they say: 'my self' and 'your self'. So this is the complete discussion of this question.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments (وجوه) for the possibility of a thing being present to itself, and then critiques the first. The first face, though only hinted at, concerns the presence of the essence to a person. The second is the presence of the individuality, and the third is the composite of essence and individuality. The author notes that if the composite is distinct from its parts, it is plausible to say the essence is present to the individual. Then a third face is added: common linguistic usage, where people say 'my self' and 'your self', indicating that relation to oneself is accepted. After presenting these faces, the author declares the first face very weak. The reasoning is that 'presence of a thing to a thing' is a general term that does not necessarily imply distinctness. If every case of presence required distinctness, then being a mover or cause would also require distinctness, leading to absurdities like a thing being a cause of itself. Since these absurdities are not accepted, the first face fails. The second face is stronger because it involves at least some distinction (between the composite and its part), which suffices for the relation. The third face—consensus on self-reference—reinforces the second. The passage concludes that this completes the discussion of this question.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Self-presence implies spatial presence or complete identity between subject and object.
Correction: Self-presence is a technical epistemological term, not spatial. It does not require complete identity; partial distinction suffices.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the first face weak?
A) Because it relies on an analogy that fails.
B) Because the presence of a thing to itself is impossible by definition.
C) Because the concept of presence is too general and does not necessitate distinctness.

Key Takeaway
The first argument for self-presence fails because it assumes presence requires distinctness, but that assumption leads to absurdities; the second and third arguments are stronger due to partial distinction and common usage.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The first face fails because if presence required distinctness, then a thing could be its own cause—an absurdity. In your own life, are there areas where you have acted as if you are your own cause, relying on your own power and ignoring your utter dependence on Allah? How does the logical failure of the first face expose the spiritual bankruptcy of self-sufficiency? Will you repent and turn back to Allah as the sole Cause of your existence?

The Three States of Concomitants
The author enumerates the three states of an essence relative to other realities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three possible relationships an essence can have with other realities: being implied (mustalzimah), being contrary (munafiyah), or being neither implied nor contrary.

Core Idea
Every essence relates to others in one of three ways: as a necessary concomitant, a contrary, or neutral.

This lesson introduces the threefold classification of concomitants according to their relationship to an essence. Understanding this classification is essential for the subsequent argument about knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully. It describes three states of an essence in relation to other realities.
The passage uses the terms 'mustalzimah' and 'munafiyah'.
2
Identify each state
Locate each of the three states: implied, contrary, and neutral. Notice how they are defined.
The third state is defined as 'la mustalzimah wa la munafiyah'.
3
Reflect on the importance
Consider why this classification is foundational. It sets up the later argument that knowledge of an essence does not entail knowledge of its concomitants.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العقل إليها، فإن لها بالنسبة إلى سائر الحقائق أحوال ثلاثة : إما أن تكون مستلزمة لها ، أو منافية لها، أو لا مستلزمة لها ولا منافية . وكونها مستلزمة للأمر الفلاني لازم من لوازم تلك الماهية ، وكونها منافية لنوع آخر من الماهيات ، لازم آخر من لوازمها ، وكونها بحيث لا تكون مستلزمة لنوع ثالث ولا منافية له ، نوع ثالث من اللوازم . فلو لزم من العلم بماهية من الماهيات ، العلم بلوازمها - ونحن قد علمنا بعض الماهيات - وجب أن يعلم كونها مستلزمة للنوع الفلاني ، وكونها منافية للنوع الثاني ، وكونها غير مستلزمة للنوع الثالث ولا منافية له . ومتى علمنا ذلك وجب أن نعلم جميع الماهيات ، فلو لزم من المعلم بالماهية العلم بلوازمها ، لزم من المعلم بماهية واحدة العلم بجميع الماهيات التي لا نهاية لها . ولما كان ذلك باطلاً فكذلك ما ذكروه يجب أن يكون باطلاً.
The intellect [refers] to them, for with respect to all other realities they have three states: either they are implied by them, or contrary to them, or neither implied nor contrary. And their being implied by a certain thing is a necessary concomitant (lāzim) of that essence (māhīya), and their being contrary to another type of essences is another concomitant of them, and their being neither implied by a third type nor contrary to it is a third type of concomitants. So if knowledge of an essence among essences necessitated knowledge of its concomitants — and we have known some essences — then it would be necessary to know that it implies a certain type, that it is contrary to the second type, and that it neither implies nor is contrary to the third type. And whenever we know that, we would have to know all essences. So if knowledge of an essence necessitated knowledge of its concomitants, then knowledge of one essence would necessitate knowledge of infinite essences. And since that is false, likewise what they mentioned must be false.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage presents a reductio ad absurdum argument against the claim that knowledge of an essence gives knowledge of all its necessary concomitants. The author first classifies the relationship of any essence to other realities into three possible states: (1) being implied by them, (2) being contrary to them, or (3) neither implied nor contrary. These three states are themselves necessary concomitants of that essence. The argument proceeds: if knowing an essence required knowing its concomitants, then knowing one essence would require knowing that it implies certain types, is contrary to others, and is neutral to a third. But to know that, one would need to know all essences, which is impossible. Therefore the original premise is false, and the opponent's position collapses.

Key Terms
مستلزمة
منافية
لازم
ماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that essences only have two possible relationships: either they imply or are contrary, neglecting the neutral case.
Correction: The author explicitly identifies a third state: neither implied nor contrary, which is itself a necessary concomitant.

Checkpoint

A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
An essence relates to others in one of three ways: implied, contrary, or neutral, and each of these is a necessary concomitant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Impossibility of Knowing All Concomitants
The author presents a reductio argument against the claim that knowledge of an essence necessitates knowledge of its concomitants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reductio ad absurdum argument proving that knowledge of an essence does not give knowledge of all its necessary concomitants.

Core Idea
If knowing an essence gave knowledge of all its concomitants, we would have to know infinite essences — an absurdity.

Building on the threefold classification, the author now presents a powerful argument that refutes the claim that essence-knowledge implies knowledge of all concomitants. This argument uses a reductio to show that such a position leads to absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the reductio argument
Read the passage from 'فلو لزم' to the end. This is a self-contained argument.
The argument is a conditional: if A then B, but B is false, so A is false.
2
Follow the logical steps
Notice the chain: assume knowledge of an essence gives knowledge of its concomitants; then from one essence we would know its three types of relationships; but each relationship involves reference to another essence; thus we would have to know all essences.
This is a typical kalam reductio.
3
Understand the conclusion
Since knowing all essences is impossible (they are infinite), the initial assumption is false. Therefore, knowledge of an essence does not entail knowledge of all its concomitants.
The last sentence: 'wa lamma kana dhalika batilan fa kadhalika ma dhakaruhu yajibu an yakuna batilan.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العقل إليها، فإن لها بالنسبة إلى سائر الحقائق أحوال ثلاثة : إما أن تكون مستلزمة لها ، أو منافية لها، أو لا مستلزمة لها ولا منافية . وكونها مستلزمة للأمر الفلاني لازم من لوازم تلك الماهية ، وكونها منافية لنوع آخر من الماهيات ، لازم آخر من لوازمها ، وكونها بحيث لا تكون مستلزمة لنوع ثالث ولا منافية له ، نوع ثالث من اللوازم . فلو لزم من العلم بماهية من الماهيات ، العلم بلوازمها - ونحن قد علمنا بعض الماهيات - وجب أن يعلم كونها مستلزمة للنوع الفلاني ، وكونها منافية للنوع الثاني ، وكونها غير مستلزمة للنوع الثالث ولا منافية له . ومتى علمنا ذلك وجب أن نعلم جميع الماهيات ، فلو لزم من المعلم بالماهية العلم بلوازمها ، لزم من المعلم بماهية واحدة العلم بجميع الماهيات التي لا نهاية لها . ولما كان ذلك باطلاً فكذلك ما ذكروه يجب أن يكون باطلاً.
The intellect [refers] to them, for with respect to all other realities they have three states: either they are implied by them, or contrary to them, or neither implied nor contrary. And their being implied by a certain thing is a necessary concomitant (lāzim) of that essence (māhīya), and their being contrary to another type of essences is another concomitant of them, and their being neither implied by a third type nor contrary to it is a third type of concomitants. So if knowledge of an essence among essences necessitated knowledge of its concomitants — and we have known some essences — then it would be necessary to know that it implies a certain type, that it is contrary to the second type, and that it neither implies nor is contrary to the third type. And whenever we know that, we would have to know all essences. So if knowledge of an essence necessitated knowledge of its concomitants, then knowledge of one essence would necessitate knowledge of infinite essences. And since that is false, likewise what they mentioned must be false.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage presents a reductio ad absurdum argument against the claim that knowledge of an essence gives knowledge of all its necessary concomitants. The author first classifies the relationship of any essence to other realities into three possible states: (1) being implied by them, (2) being contrary to them, or (3) neither implied nor contrary. These three states are themselves necessary concomitants of that essence. The argument proceeds: if knowing an essence required knowing its concomitants, then knowing one essence would require knowing that it implies certain types, is contrary to others, and is neutral to a third. But to know that, one would need to know all essences, which is impossible. Therefore the original premise is false, and the opponent's position collapses.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that knowing the essence of a thing gives complete knowledge of all its properties and relations.
Correction: The author shows that this would lead to knowing infinite essences, which is impossible; thus the belief is false.

Checkpoint
What is the author's conclusion about the relationship between knowledge of an essence and knowledge of its concomitant properties?
A) Knowledge of essence necessarily gives knowledge of all concomitants.
B) Knowledge of essence gives knowledge only of its implied concomitants.
C) Knowledge of essence does not necessitate knowledge of all its concomitants.
D) Knowledge of essence is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of an essence cannot entail knowledge of all its concomitants, because that would require knowing infinite essences.

Are you willing to accept that you cannot master everything? How does that acceptance affect your pursuit of knowledge about your faith?

The Second Proof: Knowledge of Essence Does Not Entail Knowledge of Its Concomitants
The author uses the body's essence to disprove a general claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that refutes the claim that knowing an essence gives knowledge of its necessary attributes, using the example of the body.

Core Idea
Knowing a thing's essence does not mean knowing its debated attributes.

In this passage, the author presents a second argument against the idea that understanding the essence of something automatically yields knowledge of its necessary properties. He uses the example of the body: we know its essence intuitively, but its temporal origination or eternity is disputed. If the claim were true, everyone would intuitively know which one is correct. Since they do not, the claim must be false.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
Recognize that we intuitively conceive the essence (māhīya) of the body. This is a starting point of the argument.
The author takes this as obvious.
2
Identify the alleged implication
If knowledge of essence necessitated knowledge of its necessary concomitants (lawaazim), then from intuitive knowledge of the body's essence we would intuitively know whether it is eternal or created.
3
Recognize the reductio
Since people disagree about whether the body is eternal or created, the conclusion is false. Therefore, the implication must be false, and the original claim is refuted.
This is a logical refutation by contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : إنا نعقل ماهية الجسم بالبديهة. ومن لوازم هذه الماهية إما حدوثها أو قدمها - على اختلاف القولين - فلو كان العلم بالماهية يوجبه العلم بلوازمها لزم من العلم البديهي بماهية الجسم حصول العلم البديهي بكونه قديما أو محدثاً ، ولما كان ذلك باطلاً لزم بطلان ما ذكروه ....
The second [proof]: We conceive the essence (māhīya) of the body intuitively (bil-badīha). And among the necessary concomitants (lawaazim) of this essence are either its temporal origination (ḥudūth) or its eternity (qidam) — according to the different views. So if knowledge of the essence necessitated knowledge of its concomitants, then intuitive knowledge of the essence of the body would necessitate intuitive knowledge of its being eternal or temporally originated. And since that is false, what they mentioned must be false.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof against the claim that knowledge of an essence yields knowledge of its concomitants. The author points out that we know the essence of a body intuitively (without reasoning). One of the necessary concomitants of the body's essence is either its being temporally created (ḥudūth) or eternal (qidam) — different theological schools hold different views. If the claim were true, then everyone who knows the body intuitively would intuitively know whether it is eternal or created. But this is clearly not the case; many people dispute this, and it is not intuitively obvious. Therefore, the claim is false.

Correction: The author shows this is not so: we know the body's essence intuitively, but its eternity or creation is not intuitively known.

B) The body would have no essence.

Key Takeaway
The second proof shows that intuitive knowledge of an essence does not provide intuitive knowledge of its controversial necessary attributes.

Why Self-Knowledge is Not All-Knowledge
The author argues that soul's self-knowledge does not yield intuitive knowledge of its attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the soul's self-knowledge does not automatically provide intuitive knowledge of its immateriality, temporal origination, and survival, as argued in the third proof.

Core Idea
Self-knowledge does not equal exhaustive knowledge.

In this passage, the author presents a third argument against the claim that the soul's self-awareness yields complete knowledge. He takes the opponent's premise that the soul knows its own essence and shows that if that were sufficient for knowing all its attributes, then everyone would intuitively know that the soul is immaterial, originated, and eternal. Since this is clearly not the case, the premise is false.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the logical structure: a conditional statement followed by a counter-evidence and conclusion.
The Arabic has a minor OCR uncertainty ('يفه'), but the meaning is clear from context.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent asserts that if the soul knows its own essence, then it knows all its necessary concomitants (lawaazim).
3
Trace the consequence
If true, the soul would intuitively know its immateriality, necessary temporal origination, and necessary permanence.
4
Observe the counter-evidence
These are not intuitive for most people; therefore, the opponent's claim is false.
This shows the gap in the opponent's reasoning.

الثالث : وهو أن جوهر نفسنا جوهر قائم بنفسه مجرد يفه مجرد عن الجسمية وعلائقها . فعلى ما تعين قولكم نفسنا لنفسنا ، فنفسنا عالمة بماهية نفسنا ، وجب أن يلزم من علمها بنفسها المخصوصة علمها بجميع آثارها ولوازمها ، فكان يجب أن يكون علم النفس بكونها مجردة عن الجسمية ويكونها واجبة الحدوث ويكونها واجبة البقاء ، علماً بديهياً . وحيث لم يكن الأمر كذلك علمنا أنه لا يلزم من العلم بالماهية العلم بجميع لوازمها
The third [proof]: That the substance (jawhar) of our soul is a self-subsistent substance, immaterial, ? [abstracted] from corporeity and its attachments. So according to what you have determined, our soul is known to itself, and our soul knows its own essence (māhīya). Then it would necessarily follow from its knowledge of its particular self that it knows all its effects and concomitants (lawaazim). Therefore, knowledge of the soul of its being immaterial from corporeity, its being necessarily temporally originated (wājib al-ḥudūth), and its being necessarily permanent (wājib al-baqā') would have to be intuitive knowledge (badīhī). But since that is not the case, we know that knowledge of the essence does not entail knowledge of all its concomitants.

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof. The author states that the soul is a self-subsistent, immaterial substance (the word 'yafah' is uncertain but the meaning is clear: it is abstracted from bodily attributes). According to the opponent's position, since the soul knows its own essence intuitively, it should know all its necessary concomitants: that it is immaterial, that it is necessarily temporally originated (a position some theologians hold), and that it necessarily persists after death. These should be intuitively obvious to everyone, but they are not. Hence the opponent's claim is false.

Can We Know God and Other Things at Once?
Second objection and its refutation regarding simultaneous knowledge of God's essence and other things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against simultaneous knowledge of God's essence and other things, and its refutation by distinguishing between knowing an attribute and knowing the essence.

Core Idea
Knowing that God is Necessary does not mean knowing His essence.

The second objection challenges the possibility of knowing God's essence while also knowing other beings. It uses psychological and sensory analogies to argue that intense knowledge overwhelms weaker knowledge. The opponent counters that knowledge of God as Necessary Existence is required for knowing the world's dependence. Sanusi refutes this by clarifying that knowing an attribute is not knowing the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the full Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the two main arguments: the mental focus argument and the sensory perception analogy.
The objection claims that the mind cannot focus on two objects at once, and that strong perceptions overwhelm weak ones.
2
Understand the opponent's counter
Note the opponent's argument: knowledge of the world's dependence on the Necessary Existence involves a relation, which requires knowledge of both relata. So knowledge of God must be present alongside knowledge of the world.
3
Focus on Sanusi's refutation
Observe how Sanusi responds: he calls it 'pure sophistry' and distinguishes between knowing an attribute (being the Necessary Existence) and knowing the specific essence. The objection only proves knowledge of an attribute.
This distinction is crucial for the overall argument about the possibility of knowing God.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Ask yourself: Why is it important to distinguish between knowing an attribute and knowing the essence? How does this answer the objection?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني : سلمنا أنه يصح أن تصير ذات الله تعالى معلومة . فلم قلتم : إنه يصح العلم بها ، مع العلم بغيرها ؟ وتقريره : أنا نجد من أنفسنا وجداناً ضرورياً ، أنا متى وجهنا الذهن نحو استحضار معلوم فإنه لا يمكننا في ذات الوقت توجيه الذهن نحو استحضار معلوم آخر . والعلم بذلك ضروري. ولذلك فإن من كان مشتغل الفكر في مسألة منطقية فإنه في تلك اللحظة لا يمكنه توجيه الذهن نحو مسألة هندسية . والعلم بذلك ضروري . وهذا يدل على أن الجمع بين علمين في الذهن دفعة واحدة محال ، أو نقول إنه وإن كان ذلك ممكناً في الجملة ، إلا أنه ههنا غير ممكن ، لأنه ثبت بالاستقرار : أن القوى الحاسة حال شعورها بالمحسوسات القوية ، لا يمكنها الشعور بالمحسوسات الضعيفة . فالسراج إذا وضع في مقابلة قرص الشمس لا نحس بذلك السراج، والبعوضة إذا طنت عند ظهور الرعد لا نحس بطنين البعوضة ، فكذلك مهنا ذات الله تعالى أعظم الموجودات . وعليه فإنه أعلم المعارف ، وعند حضور عرفانه في العقل ، يمتنع حضور عرفان غيره . فلم قلتم : إن الأمر ليس كذلك ؟ فإن قالوا : إنا إذا عرفنا أن هذا العالم مفتقر في وجوده إلى إيجاد واجب الوجود . فهذا علم بإضافة مخصوصة بين العالم وبين واجب الوجود ، والعلم بإضافة أمر إلى أمر مشروط بالعلم بالمضافين ، فحال حصول العلم بهذه الإضافة وجب أن يكون العلم بالعلم ، والعلم بواجب الوجود حاضراً . وذلك يقتضي جواز حصول العلم بواجب الوجود مع العلم بغيره. فنقول : هذا مغالطة محضة ، وذلك لأن العلم بكونه واجب الوجود علم بصفة من صفاته ، وليس هذا علماً بذاته المخصوصة ، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك لم يلزم من علمنا بمعنى كونه واجب الوجود ، علمنا بذاته المخصوصة . فزال هذا السؤال .
The second question: We grant that it is possible that the essence of God Most High becomes known. So why do you say that knowledge of it is possible together with knowledge of other than it? Its exposition: We find from ourselves by necessary intuition that whenever we direct the mind toward recalling an object of knowledge, we cannot at the same time direct the mind toward recalling another object of knowledge. And knowledge of that is necessary. Therefore, whoever is occupied in thought about a logical question, at that moment he cannot direct the mind toward a geometric question. Knowledge of that is necessary. This indicates that combining two knowledges in the mind at once is impossible, or we say that even if that is possible in general, it is not possible here, because it is established by experience: that the sensory faculties when they perceive strong sensations cannot perceive weak ones. So if a lamp is placed opposite the disk of the sun, we do not perceive that lamp; and if a mosquito buzzes when thunder appears, we do not perceive the mosquito's buzz. Similarly here, the essence of God Most High is the greatest of existents, so it is the greatest of objects of knowledge, and when its knowledge is present in the intellect, the presence of knowledge of other than it is impossible. So why do you say that the matter is not so? For if they say: When we know that this world is dependent in its existence on the bringing into existence of the Necessary Existence, this is a knowledge of a specific relation between the world and the Necessary Existence, and knowledge of the relation of one thing to another is conditional on knowledge of the two relata. So when knowledge of this relation occurs, knowledge of the thing and knowledge of the Necessary Existence must be present. That necessitates the permissibility of obtaining knowledge of the Necessary Existence together with knowledge of other than it. We say: This is pure sophistry, because knowledge that He is the Necessary Existence is knowledge of an attribute among His attributes, and this is not knowledge of His specific essence. When the matter is thus, it does not follow from our knowledge of the meaning that He is the Necessary Existence, [that we have] knowledge of His specific essence. So this question is removed.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection and its refutation. The objection argues that simultaneous knowledge of God's essence and other things is impossible for two reasons: first, the mind cannot focus on two objects at once (the psychological argument); second, strong perceptions overwhelm weak ones (the sensory perception analogy). The opponent counters that knowing the world's dependence on the Necessary Existence involves a relation, and knowledge of a relation requires knowledge of both relata, so knowledge of God must be present simultaneously with knowledge of the world. The author refutes this by distinguishing between knowing an attribute of God (i.e., His being the Necessary Existence) and knowing His specific essence. The objection only proves knowledge of an attribute, not the essence, so the impossibility of simultaneous knowledge of the essence remains intact.

العلم بالله
واجب الوجود
العلم بالإضافة
مغالطة محضة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowing that God is the Necessary Existence is sufficient for knowing His essence.
Correction: Being the Necessary Existence is merely an attribute; the specific essence is distinct and not directly known through this attribute alone.

Checkpoint
According to the response to the second objection, why does knowing that God is the Necessary Existence not entail knowing His specific essence?
A) Because the mind cannot know two things at once.
B) Because being the Necessary Existence is merely an attribute, not the essence itself.
C) Because the essence is unknowable entirely.
D) Because the relation between world and God is unknown.

Key Takeaway
The second objection fails because it conflates knowledge of God's attribute (Necessary Existence) with knowledge of His specific essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that God is Necessary—you say 'Allah is Wajib al-Wujud.' But this lesson warns that such knowledge is only of an attribute, not His essence. Have you ever mistaken your conceptual understanding of God for true knowledge of His very self? What would it take for you to move beyond concepts and seek a deeper, direct recognition of His essence in your heart? Commit to a specific practice—like reflecting on His names as windows, not the end—that pushes you beyond mere attributes.
?
When you pray, do you find your mind wandering to other matters—worries, plans, even other religious thoughts? The objection in this lesson says the mind cannot focus on two strong objects at once. If God is the greatest, why is your mind still preoccupied with lesser things? Is it because your knowledge of God is still weak, not yet overwhelming? Ask yourself: What would it mean to let the reality of God's majesty so fill your consciousness that every other thought fades? Decide today to spend five minutes in salah genuinely striving to hold nothing but the presence of God before you.
?
The opponent in this lesson thought that knowing God through the world's dependence was enough—a relational knowledge. Do you do the same? Do you feel satisfied knowing God through His creation, through proofs, through attributes, without ever yearning for the essence itself? This is a dangerous comfort. Ponder: If you were given only one thing to know of God—an attribute or His very self—which would you choose? Would your choice reveal a love for God or a love for your own understanding? Set a concrete intention this week to ask God in your du'a to grant you a true knowledge of His essence, not just His signs.

The Third Objection: Induction Cannot Prove Universality
Third objection: experience only covers some cases, so impossibility of simultaneous knowledge is not universal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the third objection argues that empirical evidence cannot establish the impossibility of simultaneous knowledge of God and other things universally.

Core Idea
Induction from finite cases cannot prove a universal rule.

The third objection challenges the earlier claim that simultaneous knowledge is impossible by pointing out the limits of empirical evidence. The opponent argues that experience only covers a few cases, not all, and since the total number of objects of knowledge is infinite, universal verification is impossible. This objection forces us to reconsider the inductive basis of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the English translation to grasp the objection's flow.
Notice the hypothetical opening: 'Suppose that it is valid...'
2
Identify the structure of the objection
Break down the objection into its parts: the conditional acceptance, the critique of induction, and the infinite verification problem.
The objection uses a conditional to grant a point, then attacks the inference.
3
Reflect on the philosophical implication
Consider why the objection considers infinite verification impossible. How does this limit the use of empirical arguments in theology?
This is a key move in kalām: empirical claims cannot cover infinite domains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث : هب أنه يصح منا أن نجمع بين العلم بذات الله تعالى وبين العلم بكذا وكذا ، إلا أن هذا الاستقرار إنما يطرد في الأشياء التي جربناها ووجدنا أنه لا يمتنع الجمع بين العلم بالله تعالى وبين العلم بتلك الأشياء ولا يلزم من صحة هذا الجمع في بعض الصور صحته في جميع الصور فلعل ههنا معلومات لا يمكن الجمع بين العلم بها وبين العلم بالله اللهم إلا إذا حصلت التجربة في جميع المعلومات التي لا نهاية لها ، إلا أن دخول ما لا نهاية له في الوجود محال ، فكانت هذه التجربة ممتنعة ، فكان الحكم بصحة المقارنة بين العلم بذات الله تعالى وبين العلم بكل واحد من المعلومات حكماً مبنياً على الاستقرار والتجربة في الصور القليلة .
The third question: Suppose that it is valid for us to combine knowledge of the essence of God Most High with knowledge of this and that, except that this experience is only consistent in the things we have tried and found that it is not impossible to combine knowledge of God Most High with knowledge of those things, and it does not follow from the validity of this combination in some cases its validity in all cases. Perhaps there are objects of knowledge for which it is impossible to combine knowledge of them with knowledge of God, unless experience were obtained of all objects of knowledge which are infinite, but the occurrence of what is infinite into existence is impossible. So this experience is impossible, and the judgment of the validity of the comparison between knowledge of the essence of God Most High and knowledge of each one of the objects of knowledge is a judgment based on experience in few cases.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection, which challenges the argument that impossibility of simultaneous knowledge is universal. The objector grants that in some cases we can know God together with other things, but argues that this does not prove it for all cases. The evidence from experience is limited to only a few instances, and since the totality of possible objects of knowledge is infinite, we cannot empirically verify every case. Therefore, the original claim that simultaneous knowledge is impossible cannot be generalized from a few examples. The objection thus undermines the inductive basis of the opponent's argument.

Key Terms
استقرار
تجربة
ما لا نهاية
صور قليلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A few empirical examples can prove a universal rule.
Correction: The objection shows that induction from finite cases cannot establish a universal impossibility when the domain is infinite.

Checkpoint
Why does the third objection claim that the impossibility of simultaneous knowledge cannot be proven by experience?
A) Because experience is unreliable.
B) Because we have not experienced all possible objects of knowledge, and they are infinite.
C) Because the essence of God is unknowable.
D) Because the opponent's argument is contradictory.

Key Takeaway
The third objection highlights that inductive generalization from finite experience cannot prove a universal negative claim, especially when the domain is infinite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Consider the humility required to admit that our experience is finite and cannot encompass Allah's infinity. How does this realization call you to repent of any overconfidence in your own reasoning and to rely more on revelation?

Proof that Mental Image is Not Identical to the Sky
Proof by contradiction against identity of mental image and external object

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a mental image cannot be identical to an external object, based on the difference between accident and substance.

Core Idea
Mental images differ in essence from external objects; identity is impossible.

This passage presents a concise proof by contradiction against the claim that our mental image of something is identical to the thing itself. The author uses the example of the sky to demonstrate that a mental image, being an accident, cannot be the same as the physical substance. This argument is foundational for understanding the nature of perception and knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the argument is built: a conditional statement, then a contradiction, then the reason.
The argument is short but dense; read it twice.
2
Identify the conditional assumption
Find the 'if' part: If the mental image were completely identical to the sky, then the mental image would be the sky itself.
3
Understand the contradiction
The author says this is known to be false because the mental image is an accident (non-physical) while the sky is a body (physical). They differ in essence.
This is the core of the proof.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Thus, mental images are not identical to external objects. This applies to all acts of perception.

And the fact that this mental image is different from the substance of the sky in essence is known necessarily.

Commentary
This passage presents a proof by contradiction against the claim that mental images are identical to external objects. Therefore, the mental image cannot be identical. The argument is clear: the mental image is an accident that is not sensible, while the sky is a sensible body, so they differ in essence.

Fifth Argument: The Absurdity of Mental Images Being Knowers
Reductio ad absurdum against mental image identity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth argument that refutes the claim that mental images are identical to external objects by showing it leads to the absurd conclusion that mental images would have to be knowing.

Core Idea
If mental images mirror external objects, they'd have to be knowers—an absurdity.

In this fifth argument, the author uses the principle that identical things share all properties. The opponent claims mental images are essentially identical to external objects. The author then shows that this would force us to say mental images are knowing, which is clearly false. Therefore, the claimed identity cannot hold.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle of similarity
The argument relies on the principle: 'everything valid for a thing is valid for its like.' If two things share the same essence, they must share all properties.
This is a logical principle used throughout Islamic theology.
2
Apply the principle to the opponent's claim
The opponent claims mental images are identical in essence to external objects. According to the principle, whatever is true of external objects must also be true of mental images.
3
Derive the absurd consequence
External objects can be knowers (e.g., they can be said to 'know' things in some sense?). If mental images are identical, they too must be knowers. But mental images as such are not knowing. This contradiction shows the initial claim is false.
The author concludes that the opponent's argument does not follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الخامس : إنه لما كانت الصور الذهنية مساوية في تمام الماهية للذوات الخارجية ، وثبت أن كل ما صح على الشيء ، صح على مثله ، فلها كانت هذه الذوات الخارجية يصح عليها أن تكون عالمة بالأشياء وجب أن على هذه الصورة الذهنية حال كونها ذهنية أن تكون عالمة بالأشياء ، ولما لم يلزم ذلك من التماثل المذكور ، فكذلك لا يلزم ما ذكروه . والله أعلم .
The fifth aspect: Since the mental images are equal in complete essence to external entities, and it is established that everything that is valid for a thing is valid for its like, then since these external entities are valid for them to be knowing of things, it must be that for these mental images, while they are mental, they are knowing of things. And since that does not follow from the mentioned similarity, so too what they mentioned does not follow. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage presents a fifth argument against the opponent's position. The author uses the principle that if two things are identical in essence, then whatever is valid for one is valid for the other. The opponent claims mental images are identical to external objects. If that were true, then since external objects can be knowers (a claim the opponent might accept), mental images would also have to be knowers. But that is absurd because mental images are not knowing. Therefore, the initial claim of identity is false.

The fifth argument uses the principle that identical things share all properties to show that the opponent's claim of identity between mental images and external objects leads to an absurdity, thus refuting the claim.
