The Second Proof: Foreknowledge and Present Knowledge Are Distinct
Al-Razi presents a proof that foreknowledge is distinct from knowledge of present existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi uses the immutability of essences to prove that foreknowledge and knowledge of present existence are distinct.

Core Idea
Foreknowledge and present knowledge differ in essence, and essences never change.

Al-Razi now presents his second proof, building on a fundamental principle: the essences of things are fixed. He applies this to two types of knowledge about the same thing—before it exists and after it exists. By showing that these two knowledges have different essences, he concludes that they cannot be identical.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Notice how al-Razi structures his argument from a premise to a conclusion.
2
Identify the key premise
Al-Razi begins with a self-evident truth: the essences of things (like blackness, whiteness, knowledge) do not change. Understand why this is foundational for his argument.
The premise is that essences are immutable.
3
Follow the argument for distinct essences
He argues that foreknowledge and knowledge of present existence have different essences because they cannot replace each other without causing ignorance. Trace this reasoning.
Consider the hypothetical before and after existence.
4
Connect premise to conclusion
Since essences cannot change, and the two knowledges have different essences, one cannot become the other. Therefore, they are not identical.
The conclusion follows necessarily from the premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : في بيان العلم بأن الشيء سيوجد ليس نفس العلم بوجوده إذا وجد : هو أنا نقول : من البين في بديهة العقل أن حقائق الأشياء لا تنقلب ، فالسواد لا ينقلب بياضاً ، والعلم لا ينقلب ضده (۱) . إذا عرفت هذا . فنقول : العلم بأن الشيء سيوجد ماهية مخالفة لماهية بأن الشيء موجود في الحال . والدليل عليه هو أنه ، يمتنع قيام كل واحد منهما مقام (٢) الآخر وذلك لأن قبل وجود الشيء لو اعتقد فيه كونه موجوداً ، لكان ذلك جهلا ( عند وجوده . ولو اعتقد فيه أنه غير موجود ، وأنه سيوجد ، لكان ذلك جهلا ) (۳) فيثبت أن ماهية كل واحد من هذين العلمين مخالفة لماهية العلم الآخر . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : قد بينا أن الماهيات والحقائق يمتنع عليها الانقلاب والتغير ، فوجب أن يمتنع أن يصير أحد العلمين نفس العلم الثاني ، فيثبت أن العلم بأن الشيء سيوجد ، يمتنع أن يكون نفس العلم بوجوده إذا وجد .
The second proof: in demonstrating that knowledge that a thing will exist is not the same as knowledge of its existence when it exists: that we say: it is evident in the primary intuition of the intellect that the essences of things do not change; blackness does not become whiteness, and knowledge does not become its opposite (1). If you understand this, then we say: knowledge that something will exist is an essence different from the essence of knowledge that something exists in the present. The evidence for that is that each of the two cannot take the place of the other (2); because before the existence of the thing, if one believed it to exist, that would be ignorance (when it exists; and if one believed it not to exist and that it will exist, that would be ignorance) (3). So it is established that the essence of each of these two knowledges is different from the essence of the other knowledge. And when this is established, we say: we have shown that essences and realities cannot undergo inversion and change; so it must be impossible for one of the two knowledges to become the same as the second knowledge; thus it is established that knowledge that something will exist cannot be the same as knowledge of its existence when it exists.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a complete proof (ḥujja) presented by al-Rāzī. He begins by stating a foundational premise: it is self-evident that the essences (ḥaqāʼiq) of things do not change. For example, blackness does not become whiteness, and knowledge does not become its opposite. He then applies this to the two states of knowledge: knowledge that something will exist and knowledge that it does exist now. He argues that these two knowledges are different in essence because before the thing exists, if one believed it existed, that would be ignorance; likewise, if one believed it would exist, that would also be ignorance when it exists (the text appears to have a parenthetical clarifying the conditional). Therefore, the essences are different. Finally, since essences cannot change, one cannot become the other. The conclusion is that foreknowledge cannot be identical to knowledge after existence.

Key Terms
علم
ماهية
وجود
انقلاب
حقائق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Foreknowledge and present knowledge are the same because both are knowledge of the same thing.
Correction: Al-Razi shows they have different essences: one relates to future existence, the other to present existence. Since essences are immutable, they cannot be identical.

Checkpoint

D) Because the intellect cannot comprehend two states at once.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi demonstrates that the immutability of essences necessitates that foreknowledge and knowledge after existence are distinct.

Al-Razi's Third Proof: Conditionality Proves Distinction Between Foreknowledge and Present Knowledge
Al-Razi presents a proof based on conditional relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's argument that foreknowledge and present knowledge are distinct because of their different conditional relations to the object's existence.

Core Idea
What is conditioned cannot be identical to what is unconditioned.

Al-Razi presents his third proof for the distinction between foreknowledge and present knowledge. He builds on a simple rational principle: if one thing depends on a condition and another does not, they cannot be the same. Applying this to knowledge, he shows that foreknowledge is not conditioned by the present existence of the object, while knowledge of present existence is. Therefore they are different. He even strengthens the argument by showing they are opposites.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the rational principle
Al-Razi starts with an evident principle: a thing conditioned by a condition is different from a thing not conditioned by that condition. Also, a thing existing now is different from a thing that will exist later.
This is the foundation of the entire proof.
2
Apply the principle to foreknowledge and present knowledge
Foreknowledge (knowledge that something will exist) is not conditioned by the object's present existence; in fact, it is incompatible with it. Present knowledge (knowledge that it exists now) is conditioned by the object's present existence. Therefore, they are different.
Notice the contrast in conditioning.
3
See the incompatibility and absurdity of identity
Foreknowledge exists now and vanishes when the object exists; present knowledge does not exist now and arises when the object exists. They behave like opposites. Claiming they are identical is like saying one opposite is the other, which is absurd.
This strengthens the proof to a level of impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : أن نقول : من البين في بديهة العقل : ( أن الشيء الذي يكون مشروطاً بشرط ، فإنه يكون مغايراً للشيء الذي لا يكون مشروطاً بذلك الشرط . بل نقول ((٤) : إن الشيء الذي هو موجود في الحال ، مغاير للشيء الذي هو غير موجود في الحال، بل سيوجد بعد ذلك . وإذا عرفت هذا فنقول : العلم بأن الشيء سيوجد غير مشروط بكون ذلك الشيء موجوداً في الحال ، بل يكون ذلك منافياً له ، وأما العلم بأنه موجود في الحال ، فإنه مشروط يكون ذلك الشيء موجوداً في الحال، فوجب الجزم بكون أحد العلمين مغاير للآخر . بل نقول : إن العلم بأن الشيء سيوجد حاصل في الحال ، ولا يبقى عند وجوده. والعلم بأنه غير موجود غير حاصل (0) في الحال ، وسيوجد عند وجوده ، فهذان العلمان كالمتنافيين المتضادين ، فالقول بأن أحدهما عين الآخر ، يجري مجرى أن يقال : إن أحد الضدين نفس الآخر ، وذلك محال لا يقبله العقل
The third proof: we say: it is evident in the primary intuition of the intellect that a thing which is conditioned by a condition is different from a thing which is not conditioned by that condition. Rather we say: the thing which exists in the present is different from the thing which does not exist in the present but will exist afterwards. If you understand this, we say: knowledge that something will exist is not conditioned by that thing being existent in the present; rather that is incompatible with it. As for knowledge that it is existent in the present, it is conditioned by that thing being existent in the present. So it is necessary to judge that one of the two knowledges is different from the other. Rather we say: knowledge that something will exist is present (in the mind) now, and it does not remain when it exists. And knowledge that it is not existent is not present (0) now, and will exist when it exists. So these two knowledges are like incompatible opposites. So the claim that one of them is the very same as the other runs like saying that one of two opposites is the same as the other, and that is impossible, not accepted by the intellect.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a complete proof. It uses the principle that if something is conditioned by a condition, it is different from something not so conditioned. Al-Razi applies this to knowledges: foreknowledge is not conditioned by the present existence of the object; indeed it is incompatible with it (if the object already exists, we don't have foreknowledge of it). Knowledge of present existence is conditioned by the object's present existence. Therefore they are different. He further emphasizes that foreknowledge exists in the mind now and vanishes when the object exists, while knowledge of present existence is absent now and arises when the object exists. Thus they are opposites. Saying they are identical is like saying opposites are identical, which is absurd.

Key Terms
شرط
مناف
مغايرة
ضد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that foreknowledge and present knowledge are the same mental act because both involve awareness of the object.
Correction: Al-Razi shows they have opposite conditional relationships: foreknowledge requires the object's non-existence, while present knowledge requires its existence. They cannot be identical.

Checkpoint

B) It is conditioned by the future existence of the object.
C) It is not conditioned by the present existence of the object; indeed it is incompatible with it.
D) It is conditioned by the intellect's state of attention.

Key Takeaway
Foreknowledge and present knowledge are distinct because they have opposite conditional relationships to the object's existence, making their identity impossible.

Al-Razi's Refutation of the First Objection on the Multiplicity of Knowledge
Al-Razi refutes the objection that knowledge does not multiply with multiple knowns.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's two-pronged refutation of the objection that knowledge does not change when knowns change.

Core Idea
Knowledge either conforms to its object or relates to it—both entail multiplicity and change.

This lesson examines Al-Razi's response to a key objection against the multiplicity of knowledge. The objection claimed that since knowledge does not multiply with multiple knowns, it also does not change when knowns change. Al-Razi provides two compelling arguments: first, he denies the premise by showing that knowledge indeed multiplies; second, even conceding the premise, he attacks the logic of the objection. This passage sharpens our understanding of the nature of knowledge as either a form or a relation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the literal translation. Let the flow of the argument settle in before analyzing.
Notice the structure: 'two aspects' (وجهين) divides the passage.
2
Identify the two responses
The passage explicitly states two responses. Identify the first response ('الأول') and the second response ('الثاني'). Mark where each begins.
3
Analyze the first response
Al-Razi does not concede the premise. He argues that knowledge is either a form or a relation. Work through each option and see how it leads to multiplicity.
He provides a separate evidence for each option.
4
Understand the second response
Even if the premise is granted, Al-Razi rejects the inference to no change. He calls it an analogy without a common link and refers to his own decisive proof.
Focus on the phrase 'قياس من غير جامع' – a key logical criticism.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how both responses together form a strong refutation. Think about why Al-Razi includes both—does one strengthen the other?
This shows his thorough method in kalām.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما الجواب عن الوجه الأول : وهو أنه لما لم يتكثر العلم بتكثر المعلومات ) وجب أن لا يتغير بتغيرها ، فهو من وجهين الأول : إنا لا نسلم أن العلم لا يتكثر بتكثر المعلومات (۲) والدليل عليه : أن العلم إما أن يكون صورة (۳) مطابقة للمعلوم ، وإما أن تكون نسبة مخصوصة بين العالم وبين المعلوم. فإن كان الأول وجب تكثر المعلوم عند تكثر المعلومات ، لأن الأشياء المطابقة للماهيات المختلفة ، يجب كونها مختلفة . وإن كان الثاني فكذلك أيضاً لما بينا أن النسبة إلى الشيء مغايرة للنسبة إلى غيره . بدليل : أنه يصح تعقل إحدى النسبتين حال الذهول عن الأخرى. والثاني : هب أنا سلمنا أن العلم لا يجب أن يتكثر عند تكثر المعلومات . فلم قلتم : إنه يلزم أن لا يتغير عند تغير المعلومات ؟ فإن ذلك قياس من غير جامع . والدليل القاهر الذي ذكرناه يدل على أنه يجب أن يتغير عند تغير المعلومات .
As for the answer concerning the first point — namely that since knowledge does not multiply with the multiplicity of knowns, it must not change with their change — it is from two aspects. The first: we do not concede that knowledge does not multiply with the multiplicity of knowns. The evidence for that is that knowledge is either a form conforming to the known, or a specific relation (nisba) between the knower and the known. If it is the former, then the known must multiply when the knowns multiply, because things conforming to different essences must be different. If it is the latter, then likewise, because we have shown that the relation to one thing is different from the relation to another; evidence for that is that it is possible to conceive one relation while being oblivious to the other. The second: suppose we concede that knowledge does not necessarily multiply with the multiplicity of knowns; then why do you claim that it necessarily does not change with the change of knowns? That is an analogy without a common link. The decisive proof we have mentioned shows that it must change with the change of knowns.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection. The objection claimed that since knowledge does not multiply when the knowns multiply, it also does not change when the knowns change. Al-Razi gives two responses. First, he denies the premise that knowledge does not multiply. He argues that knowledge is either a conforming form or a relation. If it is a form, different knowns require different forms, so it does multiply. If it is a relation, then relations to different things are different, so again it multiplies. Second, even conceding the premise, he argues that the conclusion (no change) does not follow; the objector's reasoning is an analogy without a common link. Al-Razi asserts that his own decisive proof shows that knowledge must change with changing information.

Key Terms
تكثر
صورة
نسبة
تغير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge is a single, simple entity that does not change when its objects change.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that knowledge either conforms to or relates to knowns, both of which entail multiplicity and change, thus refuting the notion that knowledge is static.

Checkpoint
What is the first part of al-Razi's response to the objection?
A) He accepts the premise but rejects the conclusion.
B) He denies that knowledge does not multiply with the multiplicity of knowns.
C) He argues that knowledge is a single quality.
D) He claims that the objection is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi refutes the objection by either proving knowledge multiplies or by exposing the faulty logic of the objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter new information that contradicts what you already 'know,' do you insist your knowledge is complete and unchanging, or do you allow it to be reshaped like a form conforming to a new object? What would it take for you to truly change your mind about a deeply held belief?
?
Al-Razi distinguishes between knowledge as a conforming form and knowledge as a relation. In your own life, do you treat your knowledge as a static possession or as a dynamic connection to reality? How does this affect your openness to learning?
?
The objector assumed that if knowledge doesn't multiply, it must not change. Reflect: Have you ever clung to a mistaken belief because you thought admitting one error would force you to change everything? What would it cost you to let that belief be transformed?

Rational Judgments
beginner
6 min
The Fourth Proof: Knowledge of Future vs Present Existence
Author presents a deductive proof using correspondence theory of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth proof that knowledge of future existence is necessarily different from knowledge of present existence, based on the correspondence between knowledge and its object.

Core Idea
Different objects of knowledge demand different forms of knowledge.

Sanusi now presents a crisp logical argument: if knowledge is a mental form that matches its object, then two objects with different meanings must be known by two different forms. Since 'will happen' and 'is happening now' are necessarily different in meaning, the knowledge of a future event cannot be the same as the knowledge of that event when it becomes present.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the key term 'ماهية' (quiddity) and the logical flow.
The author uses a very compressed style; the translation will help unpack it.
2
Understand the first premise
Identify the claim that knowledge is a form (صورة) that corresponds to (مطابقة) the known object. This is the foundation of the proof.
3
Compare the two quiddities
The author says 'سيحدث' (it will happen) and 'حادث وحاصل' (happening and present) have different quiddities. Think about why these meanings are necessarily different.
The difference is self-evident (بالضرورة) – future and present are distinct times.
4
Trace the conclusion
If the objects differ, their corresponding forms must differ. Therefore, the knowledge of future existence cannot be the same as knowledge of present existence.
This is a clear deductive argument.
5
Reflect on why this matters
Ask yourself: why does this argument strengthen the case that Allah's knowledge encompasses future events without implying change?
This connects to broader theological discussions about divine omniscience and immutability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة: إن العلم صورة مطابقة للمعلوم، ثم من المعلوم بالضرورة: أن ماهية قولنا: سيحدث مخالفة لماهية قولنا: إنه الآن حادث وحاصل، ولما كانت الماهيتان مختلفتين كانت الصورتان المطابقتان لهما، وجب أن يكونا مختلفين، لأن المطابق للمختلف يجب أن يكون مختلفاً، وإذا كانت إحدى الصورتين مخالفة للأخرى، امتنع القول بأن إحداهما عين الأخرى فهذه وجوه جلية قريبة من أن تكون بديهية في بيان أنه يمتنع أن يكون العلم بأن الشيء سيوجد، نفس العلم بوجوده، إذا وجد
The fourth proof: Knowledge is a form corresponding to the known. Then it is known necessarily that the quiddity of our statement 'it will happen' is different from the quiddity of our statement 'it is now happening and present'. Since the two quiddities are different, the two forms corresponding to them must be different, because that which corresponds to different things must itself be different. And if one of the two forms is different from the other, it is impossible to say that one of them is the other. These are clear points close to being self-evident in demonstrating that it is impossible for the knowledge that a thing will exist to be the same as the knowledge of its existence when it exists.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a proof. The author argues that knowledge is a form corresponding to the known object. He then observes that the meaning of 'it will happen' is necessarily different from 'it is happening now'. Since the known objects are different, the corresponding knowledge forms must be different. Therefore, knowledge of future existence is not the same as knowledge of present existence. This is presented as a clear argument.

Key Terms
العلم
صورة مطابقة
الماهية
البديهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that since knowledge is a single faculty, it cannot have different forms for the same object at different times.
Correction: The argument shows that the objects (future vs present existence) are different in meaning, so the knowledge forms differ. The faculty of knowledge is not the issue; it's the content that differs.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth proof, why must the knowledge that a thing will exist differ from the knowledge that it exists?
A) Because the two knowledge forms are identical
B) Because the quiddities of 'will happen' and 'is happening' are different
C) Because knowledge is not a form
D) Because the known object is one

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of future existence is distinct from knowledge of present existence because the quiddities of 'will happen' and 'is happening' are necessarily different, making the corresponding knowledge forms different.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You constantly think about what will happen tomorrow, next year, or in the afterlife. After this lesson, ask yourself: Is your confidence in 'I know Allah will forgive me' built on the same certainty as your knowledge that you are breathing now? If not, how should that difference affect your daily striving?
?
When you say 'I know this trial will end', are you actually just hoping? How does the distinction between future knowledge and present knowledge challenge you to be more honest about what you truly know versus what you assume?
?
If your knowledge of future events is inherently different from your knowledge of present realities, how does that change the way you prepare for the Hereafter? Are you treating your belief in the Day of Judgment as a present certainty or a distant guess?
